CHAPTER 2
ML Problem Formulation: Setting the Right Objective
Introduction
 
This chapter discusses perhaps the most important step in any data
science project that involves machine learning problem formulation. A
problem can be formulated in various ways employing different
solutions: machine learning or not. Further , there are multiple possible
ML approaches. Some problems can be solved as supervised
classiﬁcation problems, while others as clustering problems.
Additionally , the dependent variables and the set of features are
choices that signiﬁcantly impact the performance of the machine-
learning model. This chapter motivates the idea of setting the right
objective for solving the business problem at hand; deﬁning the
formulation to get the best result. Finally , we will see that for a
machine learning solution to create maximum impact, the model
objective should be aligned with the business objective.
Structure
 
In the following chapter , we will discuss the following topics:
 
•Identifying a Machine Learning problem
 
•Choosing the ML style
 
•Problem 1: Fraud detection
 
•Problem 2: Predicting high-selling products
 
•Problem 3: Question de-duplication for an e-commerce website
 
•Blending the ML styles
 
∘For better accuracy
 
∘For dimensionality reduction
 
∘For better data representation
 
•Choosing the right dependent variable
 
•Business objective vs. ML objective
Objectives
 
In this chapter , we will discuss the ﬁrst critical step in a machine
learning/ artiﬁcial intelligence project – formulating the business
problem as a machine learning problem. First, we will learn how to
identify problems that are suitable for machine learning. Then, we will
see whether a problem can be solved in multiple ways employing
different ML approaches. We will see some thumb rules and then
understand how these boundaries are blurry in real-world situations.
We will also see how choosing the right dependent variable and
features can set up a project for success. We will conclude this chapter
the idea that maximum impact is created when the model objective
aligns with the business objective. At the end of this chapter , you will
be able to identify the right formulation for your business problem and
set up the machine learning/artiﬁcial intelligence endeavor for success.
Identifying a Machine Learning problem
 
In the Machine Learning approach to solving problems, we use data to
make the model learn the patterns needed to make
decisions/predictions. The promise of Machine learning is that it
eliminates the need to manually encode your knowledge and domain
understanding as a set of rules. You could feed thousands and even
millions of data points to the model, and it, almost magically , ﬁgures
out all it needs to make a prediction. Machine Learning approaches
have gained such prominence that many data problems are
straightaway considered ML problems, sometimes with great success,
and sometimes with some hard lessons learnt. For any data science
professional, it is imperative to understand that not all problems need
to be solved as Machine Learning problems.
 
Machine Learning comes with its own set of requirements and
challenges. The most common necessity of the popular ML approaches
is a high(er) volume of training data that the machine needs to learn
from to create a model. Additionally , building robust Machine
Learning models is a time-consuming process involving multiple steps
- fetching data, cleaning it up, other pre-processing and
transformations, model building, evaluation, and scoring. While in
some situations, the time and effort spent are worth the result, this is
not always the case, especially considering that competing rule-based
solutions might provide an acceptable result quicker with lesser effort.
 
Consider a new online job application portal, where users can submit
their proﬁles in PDF format, following speciﬁc guidelines on the
resume format. The portal would then automatically extract
information from the resume such as contact information, job titles,
graduation dates, and so on. Such a task falls in the category of
extraction” tasks, an area of Natural Language Processing. Deep
Learning-based models have shown great performance on such tasks,
given adequate training data and time to train the data.
 
An alternative approach would be to use Regular Expressions –
pattern-based text matching and extraction techniques. The modeller
can deﬁne the pattern expected in a ﬁeld to extract that information
from the text. For example, if the phone numbers have a (few)
consistent format(s), then the modellers can describe the pattern using
Regular Expressions and extract the phone number with ease.
Likewise, Regular Expressions can be used to extract other relevant
pieces of information. Since the portal has guidelines on the resume
format, we expect the patterns to be consistent across the resumes.
This means that we can expect the Regular Expression-based solution
to provide good results in a fraction of the time needed for a Machine
Learning-based approach. ML-based solutions would be overkill in
this situation, and a simple rule-based solution can do the job.
 
Consider another situation in which the online fast fashion giant Azra
wishes to understand why its Net Promoter Score has been reducing,
that is, they wish to understand the factors that are negatively
impacting customer satisfaction. Using Machine Learning, this can be
solved using complex models. The features/predictors would include
many user signals and their interactions with the platform and its
products. We are talking about large volumes of data that would need
to be collected and carefully synthesized into smart features to use in
the model. Such activity typically takes several months. But there is an
alternative: well-designed user surveys (or even talking to a few of the
subject matter experts). Again, Machine Learning is not always the
best solution for every problem and simpler approaches can provide
pragmatic solutions to pressing business problems.
 
How do you decide if the problem at hand needs to be solved using
Machine Learning? Here are some considerations:
 
•How much time do you have?
 
•Do you have data/enough data?
 
•Does it need high accuracy?
 
•Does the solution need to be scalable?
 
•Is it a Minimum Viable Product ( MVP  ), or in other words, a ﬁrst
version that will be superseded?
 
•Is it a one-time ﬁx or a long-term solution that needs to be
maintained?
 
The ideal template for a Machine Learning solution is a problem where
you have enough data, sufﬁcient time, and higher accuracy
requirements. For situations requiring immediate ﬁxes and where high
accuracy is not a requirement, rule-based approaches often sufﬁce.
Additionally , if the situation needs a stop-gap solution that will be
eventually superseded by a thorough, sophisticated solution, this ﬁrst
attempt can very well be a rule-based solution. In this case, the
solution is a long-term one, needing frequent retraining and
maintenance, where the Machine Learning approach is ideal. Similarly ,
scalability requires employing Machine Learning techniques, as rule-
based systems are difﬁcult to maintain and even more difﬁcult to scale.
 
With these guidelines, you should be able to decide if the problem at
hand can be addressed using rule-based approaches, or if they indeed
need a Machine Learning solution. In case you do need to take a
Machine Learning approach, you need to choose the appropriate ML
approach. Let us discuss that in the following section.
Choosing the ML style
 
We discussed some Machine Learning styles in the previous chapter . The
implicit, simple decision process for choosing the appropriate style is
shown in Figure It captures the most popular guidelines for making such a
choice:
 
 
Figure Choosing a machine learning style
 
Additionally , there are various thumb rules for choosing the speciﬁc
formulation of the machine learning problem for a given task/situation:
 
•Supervised : If the target value is available
 
•Classiﬁcation : If the target value is a category
 
•Regression : If the target value is a quantity
 
•Unsupervised : If the target label is not available
 
•Clustering : To group entities
 
•Dimensionality reduction : Distilling the information into a smaller set of
features
 
•Reinforcement learning : To identify the best action in a situation
 
A note in the previous chapter said that the boundaries between the ML
styles and the problem formulations can be blurry in some cases. Now , we
expand that little note into a key idea; you can formulate the problem
appropriately and choose between the various ML styles. Indeed, this
formulation can make all the difference in the success of your data science
project.
  
Pause and ponder: Consider the task of classifying an email as spam/non-
spam. Which Machine Learning style is suitable for the task? Also, think
about the requirements for the ML style to work.
  
An extremely important idea is that the boundaries between these
formulations can often be blurry , which means that a problem can be
solved using multiple Machine Learning styles. A problem that is usually
thought of as a supervised classiﬁcation problem can also be considered
and solved as an unsupervised (clustering) problem. This choice of
formulation can make all the difference in the success of your data science
project. To make the idea more concrete, let us take a look at a few
examples.
Problem 1: Fraud detection
 
Fraud is a problem that affects virtually all industries. In the e-
commerce and the Banking, Financial Services, and Insurance sectors,
this is a particularly big problem. In e-commerce alone, the losses due
to payment fraud were around 20 billion USD in 2021. Auto insurance
companies lose about 29 billion USD each year. In 2019, organizations
globally lost more than 5 trillion USD to fraud. Clearly , fraud is a big
problem and one that Machine Learning techniques have frequently
been using to combat. Indeed, fraud detection is one of the most
impactful use cases of Machine Learning. Interestingly , there are (at
least) two prominent ML approaches for this.
Approach 1: Supervised classiﬁcation
 
The obvious approach for detecting fraud is by treating it like a
supervised classiﬁcation task. The outcome/dependent variable is a
binary indicator (0/1) of the transaction being fraudulent, which is
predicted based on a lot of features related to the user and their
behavior and the transaction itself. Indeed, this is the most popular
formulation employed for fraud detection. This approach is successful,
but also comes with a lot of challenges.
 
Pr (Y = 1) = f (user attributes,transaction attributes)
 
Fraud has a low incidence rate – which means that typically a very low
proportion of customers/ transactions are fraudulent. For instance, only
about 0.1% of credit card transactions are fraudulent - 1 in 1000 is a
low incidence/ occurrence rate. The numbers vary by industry , but
fraud transactions/users typically constitute <1% of all
transactions/users. In supervised Machine Learning terms, there is a
heavy class imbalance - the majority class records (non-fraudulent)
heavily outnumber the minority class records (fraudulent). Such heavy
imbalance is always problematic since models have a hard time
learning the patterns needed to discern the two classes, simply because
the information and representation for fraudulent cases are too low to
learn from. Getting satisfactory results from the supervised
classiﬁcation models is almost always challenging. Fortunately , there
is another (very different) potential approach that deals with class
imbalance better .
Approach 2: Unsupervised clustering
 
The idea is simple and powerful – fraudulent transactions/customers have
speciﬁc behaviors that are very different from regular ones. As an
example, fraudulent transactions are typically performed with accounts
opened more recently , having more digits in the email address, preferring
some payment methods and more commonly from certain locations.
Additionally , the number of attempted transactions from the user, or the
number of items in the transaction could be unusually high. If all this
information were to be represented by data, and if clustering were to be
performed properly - the fraudulent transactions would emer ge as a
separate cluster . Or these would appear as loose points far away from
clusters belonging to regular , non-fraudulent transactions. As illustrated in
Figure all the tiny clusters and individual points are different from the
bulk of the transactions and are therefore good candidates for fraudulent
transactions.
 
 
Figure Unsupervised approach to fraud detection
 
As you may suspect, the unsupervised clustering approach does not
naturally ﬁnd class imbalance challenging. If the data contains features
that are well discriminating between the fraudulent and the non-fraudulent
transactions, clustering techniques would work well with imbalance. It is
important to note that this does not naturally imply that the unsupervised
approach performs markedly better . This just means that far less effort is
needed to overcome the class imbalance issue, and an acceptable baseline
can be achieved with less effort.
 
Which of the two approaches works better naturally depends on the
speciﬁc situation and data. Regardless, we understood that the fraud
detection problem can be tackled using more than one approach. Let us
take a look at another problem that has more than one solution.
Problem 2: Predicting high-selling products
 
You are a Data Scientist at the online fashion giant, Azra. The task you
are working on is identifying new items to merchandise/display on the
home page of the Azra app. Only potentially high-sales items, among
the top 10% by revenue, are to be displayed. The data you have is
around attributes of the product – for example., color , brand, category ,
style, etc., using which you need to identify potentially high-selling
(revenue) products.
Approach 1: Supervised Regression
 
You may already have ﬁgured out the obvious formulation - predict the
sales (revenue) of the product based on its features that is; this is a
Supervised Regression problem with revenue being the dependent/
outcome variable and product attributes being the features/ predictors.
 
Y (Revenue) = f (product attributes)
  
Pause and ponder: Do you need to predict the exact revenue of the
product, or is it sufﬁcient to predict that the product would be in the
top 10%? Are these two different formulations?
Approach 2: Supervised Classiﬁcation
 
This is a natural formulation of the task and by far the most popular
approach. But consider this - you know that only the top 10% of
revenue products would be allowed to be displayed. The precise value
of the revenue of the product would not matter to you; you would not
be doing much with it. Rather , the product being in the top 10% is of
interest. This realization leads us to an alternative formulation to that
of supervised classiﬁcation. The dependent/ outcome variable would
be an indicator of the product being in the top 10%.
 
Pr (Y = 1) = f (product attributes)
 
The beneﬁt of this formulation is that precise prediction of the sales is
irrelevant now. Revenues are generally hard to predict as they typically
exhibit high variance from product to product. Therefore, model
evaluation metrics for regression (example - would show lower values.
While this is not an issue on its own, it is misleading as the modeller
would be focusing on getting more precise, which does not matter as
much. The classiﬁcation formulation, on the other hand, is more
aligned with the end objective, identifying the top 10% product and
therefore naturally more effective.
Problem 3: Question de-duplication for an e-commerce website
 
This problem was posed as a thought experiment earlier in this section:
let us try to answer it now. Imagine that you are a Data Scientist
working at the online fashion giant Azra, in the Product Page team.
The product page contains detailed information about the product,
including ratings and reviews provided by customers, and questions
that the users asked about the product. There seems to be severe
duplication of the questions on the product pages users seem to be
asking the same question with minor variations in language. For
example, “Is the material durable?” can be considered a duplicate of
“Is the shirt durable?”. This is a problem as a few popular questions
would suppress the visibility of other useful questions and their
answers, withholding useful information from the users and affecting
the sale of the products. Your task is to identify questions that are
duplicates.
Approach 1: Supervised classiﬁcation using Deep Learning
 
Recurrent Neural Networks and more recently Transformer -based
architectures have shown tremendous success in text classiﬁcation tasks.
Indeed, Deep Learning methods provide state-of-the-art results on most
text-related tasks. Therefore, the ﬁrst formulation is a supervised
classiﬁcation problem. Concretely , you could take pairs of questions and
run them by a model which predicts whether the pair is just a duplicated
question, as illustrated in Figure 2.3:
 
 
Figure Deduplication using supervised classiﬁcation.
 
This makes intuitive sense as we expect Deep Learning methods to shine
in such situations if we have enough labelled data. Deep Learning
methods are extremely data-hungry and need large volumes of data to be
effective. For a text deduplication task like ours, we would need at least a
few thousand pairs of questions labelled. The problem is that labelling
tens of thousands of question pairs is not easy. Theoretically , the labelling
can be done manually , but this would take numerous man-hours and some
solid guidelines for the labellers so that their labels are in agreement.
Clearly , it might take a long time possibly several weeks to prepare such
training data from scratch. We note that the supervised classiﬁcation
approach has promise, but we presumed the availability of a lot of data for
this formulation. Fortunately , an alternative approach of unsupervised
clustering can save the day.
Approach 2: Unsupervised clustering of questions
 
Unsupervised clustering does not need any labelled data. This
formulation, therefore, immediately does away with the need for
labelling tens of thousands of pairs of questions. This formulation is
simple conceptually - perform clustering on the questions. If the
clustering is performed well (with appropriate distance measures
applied), we expect duplicate questions to form very tight clusters. In
the ideal case, we expect as many clusters as there are unique
questions in the data. This simple approach is extremely powerful and
perhaps the only feasible solution for our situation.
 
To conclude this section, a Machine Learning problem can usually be
formulated in multiple ways. The obvious go-to formulation may have
requirements that we might be able to meet and may not be feasible.
The alternative formulations may not be obvious or intuitive initially
but could be much more practical for your situation. It is therefore
essential that the smart Data Scientist be aware of multiple
formulations for a given task and be able to identify the right
formulation for the task. This choice can very well make all the
difference in the success of the Machine Learning project. But do we
always have to choose between the formulations? Or is there a way we
can beneﬁt from the strengths of the various formulations? Let us now
discuss a key idea – the blending of Machine Learning styles.
Blending ML styles
 
We saw that there are problems that can be solved using multiple
approaches and that which approach performs better is dependent on
the situation. Each approach may have some drawbacks/beneﬁts, and
there may be some cases in the data where one algorithm works better
than the other . Here is a seemingly wild idea - why not combine
different Machine Learning styles? If you could do this well somehow ,
the combined approach would be better than the individual approaches
- one approach potentially overcoming the drawbacks of the other . The
good news is that such a ‘blending’  of approaches can be done.
For better accuracy
 
Let us revisit the Fraud detection problem that we discussed in the
previous section. We understood that heavy class imbalance is a
problem for a purely supervised approach, but the unsupervised
approach does not struggle with this problem as much. Also, recall that
in the unsupervised approach, we obtained some clusters that we
suspected to be outliers and further analysis was needed to identify the
fraud cases. Therefore, either approach has a drawback. Consider this -
what if we created some clusters using the unsupervised approach, and
used these clusters as features for the supervised classiﬁcation model?
 
model?
model?
 
 
 
 
Table Using unsupervised cluster as a feature for a supervised model
 
Table 2.1 shows how this can be achieved very simply - the
unsupervised cluster assignments for the instances are simply included
as a predictor in the training data for the classiﬁcation model. The
beneﬁt of this approach is that the new feature, Cluster , is extremely
informative and can help the supervised model perform better despite
the heavy class imbalance. The problem of having features dominated
by the information from the majority class is reduced by adding this
new feature. Overall, we arrive at a blended solution that will likely
work better than the individual approaches.
For dimensionality reduction
 
Blending supervised and unsupervised approaches also help when dealing
with high-dimensional data (lots of features) and many training records.
Predictive modelling with several hundred (if not thousands) predictors is
a common situation in the Banking, Financial Services, and Insurance
domain. Even with a few hundred thousand records, a model using a
thousand predictors becomes unwieldy to the extent of being impractical.
 
 
Figure Dimensionality reduction techniques in action
 
A common strategy is to use dimensionality reduction techniques like
Principal Component Analysis and Singular Value Decomposition In both
approaches (PCA  and SVD), the original features are used to derive new
dimensions or components. These new components are linear
combinations of the original features that capture most of the information
contained in the dataset. Typically , a very low number of dimensions
capture the information contained in a thousand features. Indeed, it is
common that 50 dimensions capture most of the information contained in
about a thousand original features. This is a serious reduction in
dimensionality - from about 1,000 to 50 - and improves the speed of the
model by leaps. This is yet another example where blending approaches
can give us signiﬁcant gains in efﬁciency .
For better data representation
 
Most of the modern Natural Language Processing tasks employs a
similar combination of unsupervised and supervised learning
approaches. Before 2013, the prevalent approach to represent text was
using term frequencies - each text record would be represented by
several thousand features/columns (one for each word found in the
entire training data, about 10,000 columns wasn’ t rare). In 2013,
Mikolov et. al. proposed scalable word embeddings (low-dimension
vectors) which quickly became the most common approach to
representing text data These embeddings help in the following two
ways:
 
1. Dimensionality reduction: The size of the embedding for a term is
much small (usually between 50 and 300).
 
2. Better representation capturing ‘meaning’: These embeddings are
derived in a way that they represent concepts and it has been
demonstrated that these embeddings capture many relationships
between words
 
The derivation of the embeddings mostly did not care about the labels
in the data (for example word2vec algorithm, the most popular by far)
and was therefore unsupervised in that sense. Reducing the feature set
from 10,000 relatively dumb frequencies to about 100 smart features
that capture meaning: It is a major beneﬁt that was possible by
blending approaches.
 
We now understand and appreciate that Machine Learning problems
can be solved using multiple formulations, each having its own
beneﬁts and drawbacks. We also saw that we need not always choose
between competing formulations. Instead, we could blend multiple
approaches in different ways and derive tremendous value from the
data. Let us now turn our attention to another aspect of problem
formulation - the choice of the dependent variable.
Choosing the right dependent variable
 
In the section, Problem 2: Predicting high-selling products, we saw
that we can also tweak the choice of the dependent/outcome variable.
The outcome variable could have been the revenue of the product (in
the obvious formulation), but we decided that we could instead model
whether the product would be in the top 10% of products. This choice
changed the formulation from a Regression to a Classiﬁcation
problem. More importantly , the second formulation prevented us from
focusing on chasing precise revenue predictions and instead got us
focused on the outcome that aligned very well with the business
objective. Clearly , choosing the right dependent variable is an
important decision that affects the effectiveness of the solution.
 
Consider another situation: as a Data Scientist in the search team at the
popular online fashion platform Azra, your goal is to improve the
relevance of the search results. A better ranking will surface more
relevant products higher up in the results, helping the shoppers ﬁnd
what they are looking for more easily . You are sure about this being a
supervised learning problem, and you have tons of historical data to
model on. The key question is: what should the dependent variable be?
Let us ﬁrst think about our options and take inspiration from current
approaches in the industry .
 
An extremely popular formulation is that of predicting a click on the
search results. A search is successful if any of the top N (a
predetermined number) results is clicked. Therefore, the dependent
variable Y is:
 
0: if none of the top N results was clicked on
 
1: if any of the top N results were clicked on
 
Note that this formulation does not care about the position of the click.
A search where the last item, say 40th, is clicked is treated to be
equally successful as a search where the topmost item was clicked on
by the user. Does this make sense? One might argue that the click
position is extremely relevant - a higher click position (rank 1 is the
highest position) indicates a better search result set.
 
This brings us to another formulation; treating the problem as a
ranking problem. Note that this is still a supervised learning approach.
But instead of focusing on just a click, we try to get the click in the
topmost result. In such a formulation, the dependent variable could
simply be the click position and a lower value is better . A model that
minimizes this value would surface more relevant items at the top and
can arguably be more suitable for our objective.
  
Note that such formulation choices are common for recommender
systems - both formulations, viz. ranking vs. click prediction, are
commonly employed in the industry .
  
It is therefore essential that we reﬂect on the choice of the dependent
variable. While often there is a natural choice that works well, there
could be situations where you choose a dependent variable more in
line with the task at hand.
Business objective vs. ML objective
 
Let us continue with the search relevance example we discussed in the
previous section. The goal of the data science project was to improve
the relevance of the search results. We discussed a couple of choices
for the dependent variable and discussed that we could treat this as a
click prediction problem or a ranking problem. In both formulations,
the primary objective was getting the user to click on an item.
 
It is well known in the e-commerce industry that lower -priced items
gather more clicks. Consider a search ranking algorithm that always
surfaces very cheap items at the top. What would be your expectations
around the click-through rate (Searches with Clicks/T otal Searches) for
such an algorithm? Of course, it would lead to more clicks (as users
click on cheaper items). Does this mean that such an algorithm is
better for the platform?
 
The answer is no. Surfacing cheaper items at the top will mostly lead
to more clicks and improve the click-through rate metric. However ,
there could be a few drawbacks of such an approach:
 
•The overall revenue for the platform could drop simply because the
average selling price could drop.
 
•Lower quality items on top (cheaper items could be lower quality
items) could harm the platform’ s perception.
 
•Lower quality items may lead to higher returns and therefore not just
revenue loss but also reverse logistics costs.
 
•Surfacing cheaper items, without caring about the distance from the
shopper , may lead to increased logistics costs and reduce proﬁtability
for the company .
 
•Overall conversion (purchases/visits) may drop as after clicking, the
product details for lower quality items and their reviews may
discourage shoppers from buying.
 
•Essentially , there are various, potentially competing objectives for a
task like ranking. An approach that solely focuses on the click-through
rate of the search may, and probably will, negatively impact the other
objectives of the business. It is therefore imperative that the objectives
of the Machine Learning modelling process mirror closely the
objectives of the business.
 
For our example, it would help to model instead a metric that blends
click-through rates and monetary value (or some other objectives). An
example could be to maximize the estimated revenue from a click.
Such a metric marries the probability of a click with the revenue
coming from the click and helps balance two objectives. Even if you
do not utilize such a blended metric, it would be a good idea to track
metrics corresponding to the other business objectives. After all, the
goal is to solve a business problem, and any acceptable solution should
improve things rather than harm them.
Conclusion
 
ML has tremendous promise and adds great value by solving several
business problems. However , not all problems need ML – many can be
solved using much simpler rule-based approaches. We discussed some
considerations to enable you to decide. Next, we discussed the various
ML styles that a practitioner can choose from and saw that the lines
between the styles can be blurry . Using examples from several
domains, we saw that problems can have multiple formulations with
different ML styles. The smart Data Scientist must be able to identify
the right formulation for the task. This choice can very well make all
the difference in the success of the ML project.
 
We then saw that blending ML styles is a powerful approach. There are
several motivations for doing so - dimensionality reduction, better
accuracy , better data representation – and combining supervised and
unsupervised learning approaches is a common practice that yields
great beneﬁts. Choosing the right dependent variable is also a part of
the formulation, and we saw that this choice can not only greatly
simplify the modelling process, but also help obtain better outcomes.
Finally , we saw that to add true value, the objective of the Machine
Learning project should be well aligned with the business objective.
 
In the next chapter , we will learn the next crucial step in the data
science solution-building process – data pre-processing. We will learn
how to process data to reﬁne it to best grade before modeling to derive
maximum value from it.

CHAPTER 3
Data Collection and Pre-processing
Introduction
 
Garbage in, garbage out; you may have heard this adage before
regarding the effectiveness of systems. This is true for machine
learning processes as well. Regardless of the sophistication level of the
machine learning technique, using poor data leads to poor results. With
the popularity of data-hungry deep learning methods, AI and machine
learning systems require more and more data. But better data beats
more data each time, so understanding how to get the data right is
critical.
 
In this chapter , we will learn more about the data understanding and
data preparation steps of the CRISP-DM framework. It is essential to
understand the various factors that inﬂuence the data collection process
and how you should tackle associated problems. We will take a look at
the entire pre-processing process as a pipeline, for which we can
employ a principled approach. With the help of a case study , we will
learn about the various details of the pipeline and implement them
hands-on.
Structure
 
In this chapter , we will cover:
 
Building a machine learning solution
 
The case study
 
The pre-processing process
  
Step 1: Gather data + basic checks + corrections
 
Step 2: Separate into train and test datasets
 
Step 3: Outlier treatment
 
Step 4: Missing value treatment
 
Step 5: Categorical feature handling
 
Step 6: Transformation of numeric features
 
Case study steps review
Objectives
 
In this chapter , we learn how to process data to reﬁne it to the best
grade before modeling. We will ﬁrst learn about the various factors
that can inﬂuence the data and your understanding of it. We will learn
about various possible issues with data and how to identify and handle
them. We will introduce a principled approach to data pre-processing
that can be applied to different solutions. We will learn the ways to
avoid common pitfalls (like data leakage) and learn extremely useful
data cleanup approaches. We will then see how to manipulate the
features in the data (feature engineering), focusing on the task at hand.
We will take a holistic look at the data pre-processing We will apply all
these concepts in a real-world case study .
 
By the end of this chapter , you will be able to master the process of
data understanding and data preparation. You will be able to identify
peculiarities of the domain and the data and approach the problem
accordingly . You will be able to perform extensive data cleanup to
enhance the quality of the data. You will also be able to make various
transformations on the data to get the maximum performance from
your machine learning/artiﬁcial intelligence solution.
Building a machine learning solution
 
Building a machine learning-based solution is not merely the technical
exercise of ﬁtting the model to the data. The solution building starts with
business understanding, which guides the solution design and the steps
afterwards. Unless done correctly , it can jeopardize the entire machine
learning solution. Understanding the data and data preparation are the next
two steps. Data understanding involves exploration of the data and
understanding the patterns. Using this understanding, the data can be best
prepared to extract maximum value from it and build the best machine
learning solution. It is only after these steps that we approach the
modelling part, the evaluation, and the deployment steps. The entire
process is encapsulated in the Cross-Industry Standard Process for Data
Mining framework as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure 3.1: CRISP-DM framework
 
The takeaway is that the model ﬁtting part is one of the many steps in the
process. The understanding and preparation steps can be called The model
ﬁtting process is more technical and parts of it can be standardized, or
even automated if the pre-processing has been done right. The pre-
processing part, on the other hand, is rarely standard, it depends on the
data collection process, the nature of the data, some domain-speciﬁc
considerations, and the task at hand. Pre-processing is where the
practitioner ’s skill makes a big difference; these are the bits that are part
art and part science. Let us understand how each aspect inﬂuences the pre-
processing process.
The data collection process
 
How has the data been collected? Are you collating data from multiple
disparate systems, involving complex joins using different criteria? or
are you consuming well-curated data from a single table that has been
standardized and its quality established? In the ﬁrst case, having
missing values for multiple ﬁelds would not be surprising.
Understanding the root cause of a missing value may take effort and
discussions with the engineering teams. In the latter case, the reasons
for missing values would be well studied, and you would know how to
interpret missing values.
The nature of the data
 
Let us think about surveys. Can you think of any peculiarities of data
generated from survey responses? For one, survey data is understood
to contain volunteer bias meaning it usually does not represent all
users. Another peculiar phenomenon is the halo A user who had a bad
experience with one aspect of the product usually rates all aspects
poorly , and likewise for users with great experience with one aspect.
Third, often there are some questions the user might not be
comfortable responding honestly to. So, the user either skips the
question or adds some dishonest value (potentially useless for your
task). The data scientist working on the survey data must understand
these peculiarities of the data to create any valuable solution from it.
Similarly , transaction data from an e-commerce platform may not have
these challenges but may have its peculiarities.
Domain-speciﬁc aspects
 
Consider that your dataset contains the ﬁeld age and in a sample, you
encounter a value of 300. Should this surprise you? You may have
encountered a relatively young tree’s data if the data is about sequoia
trees. A value of 6,000 would be surprising. Now , let us say that you
are working with data for humans. 300 should be surprising but only if
measured in years. 300 months translates to 25 years, again not so
surprising. How about a negative value, say -4 in the age column? Not
too surprising if you’re looking at a birthing center ’s data for babies
that are yet to be born, and the negative value makes sense suddenly .
The key idea is that speciﬁc aspects of the domain reﬂect peculiarities
in the data. The wise data scientist must understand these peculiarities
and account for them to create a valuable machine learning / AI
solution.
Inﬂuence of the task at hand
 
The considerations for an unsupervised machine learning problem are
usually very different from those for a supervised one. The data
processing needed is, therefore, also very different. For example, a
clustering activity may require you to standardize the features to give
them equal importance and the output can vary greatly with and
without standardizing. Standardization isn’t necessary for several
supervised machine learning techniques. It can help with faster
conver gence for some machine learning algorithms but usually doesn’ t
result in wildly different outputs.
The case study
 
Azra is a leading private ﬁnancial institution with various ﬁnancial
products and wealth management services. The bank wishes to run a
telemarketing campaign for its new product which is a deposit scheme
with great returns. The bank had run a similar campaign for a comparable
product a few months back and wishes to use that data to identify
customers that are most likely to convert, that is, buy the product and
commit to a deposit. The assumption is that the type of customers who
responded positively to the previous campaign are likely to do so for this
campaign as well. The data science objective is to build a highly
performant predictive model. As you appreciate by now, the natural ﬁrst
step is gathering and pre-processing the data.
 
The data is in the ﬁle Azra_Bank_Marketing_v1.zip is a compressed CSV.
The ﬁrst few records are shown in Figure
 

 
Figure A glimpse of the dataset
 
Brief descriptions of the key ﬁelds in the dataset are as follows:
 
The age of the customer at the time of the marketing campaign
 
The customer ’s occupation
 
Highest education level of the customer (primary to tertiary)
 
Bank balance at the time of the campaign
 
Whether the customer had a housing loan (yes/no)
 
Whether the customer had a personal loan (yes/no)
 
Preferred contact channel of the customer (telephone/cellular)
 
Month on contact in the campaign
 
The duration (seconds) of the phone call in the campaign
 
Outcome of the campaign before this campaign
 
Whether the customer bought the product (yes/no) – the outcome
 
There is quite a lot of information about the customer , their ﬁnancial state
(balance, loan or not and so on), and some information about the
campaign itself (contact month, duration). Using these columns, the goal
is to make a model that predicts the ‘deposit’  ﬁeld (the outcome).
 
So, the model would be as follows:
 
deposit = f (age, job, …, poutcome)
 
That is, we are modeling deposit as a function of the features.
  
Note: All the columns we see might not eventually be a part of the model.
We will likely discard some of the columns we see as not valuable for our
task. Feature selection is the process of choosing the required set of
features. Additionally , we might create some new features derived from
the existing features. This is part of feature engineering that we will
perform shortly .
  
We will use this data and case study throughout the chapter . We will
perform all the data pre-processing steps to make all the ideas concrete.
But what are those steps? Is there a standard ‘pipeline’  of steps that
applies to all modeling tasks? Or is each case special? Let us discuss this
in the next section.
The pre-processing process
 
Pre-processing refers to all the steps from data gathering to the point
where you are ready to ﬁt the model. This includes basic checks and
ﬁxes like formatting ﬁxes and complex operations like imputation of
missing values. Generally , these steps can be considered part of a
process or a One such pipeline is shown in Figure You can see that
there are varied tasks included in this process. We will study each step
in detail in this chapter and apply the learnings to the marketing case
study . Refer to Figure 3.3 for the steps in the pipeline:
 
 
Figure 3.3: Some general pre-processing steps
 
It is important to understand that there is no standard pipeline. There is
no standard process for any of these steps. As we discuss throughout
the chapter , a lot of data and domain speciﬁcs must be accounted for,
along with the task’ s requirements. This makes standardization
extremely difﬁcult. You can potentially automate some parts of
individual steps and maybe even have a guiding template of steps.
However , a wise data scientist should not leave this critical step to
automated processes and must always perform the ﬁnal checks.
Step 1: Gather data + basic checks
 
The ﬁrst step in the processing pipeline is obtaining the data. This
could mean loading CSV  ﬁles into Python or working with tables from
databases/data lakes. This could mean reading in a single table or
joining multiple tables using distributed storage. This could be done
using a single script or employing multiple scripts and interacting back
and forth with a database to painstakingly create a dataset from
multiple sources. This could also be using APIs to fetch JSONs of
data. As mentioned in the previous section about the Data Collection
as a data scientist you must understand this process and the artefacts
this can create in the data.
 
You then need to check whether the data loading was performed
correctly and ﬁx any issues. For instance, if you realize that the
loading process induced incorrect formats or did not read certain
special characters correctly , you must alter the loading process to ﬁx
these issues. Often, there are issues with date formats not being read
correctly because of which you must ﬁx them manually . Think of this
as a “basic clean-up of the data”.
  
Note: We will perform cleanup on the data again later. Which will
include outlier treatment, missing value treatment and so on. You can
think of that as the deep clean compared to the clean-up mentioned in
this section.
  
For our case study on bank marketing for Azra Inc., we will obtain the
data by reading a CSV  ﬁle into Python and performing some of these
checks.
 
Let us head towards the exercise:

for Azra Inc. and perform some basic exploration to understand the data,

With these simple commands, you have gathered the data, performed
some basic proﬁling, checked out some sample records, and investigated
the unique values, and counts of a particular column of interest. Using
these outputs, let us understand the dataset and the case study better .
 
A basic proﬁling of the dataset shows that it has 12 columns in total and
11,162 records. The proﬁling also shows that the number of non-null
values does not match the number of records for all the columns that is
some columns have missing values. Doing a basic distribution check on
the target column, that is we see that about 52% of the customers did not
make a deposit, while the campaign was successful for about 47% of
customers. Next, we should perform more checks on the data and make
some basic corrections.
 
At this stage, we should check for imperfections in the data and ﬁx those
we can already . Data format issues during loading are common as Python
may not be able to correctly detect all of them (for example, common for
date ﬁelds,). We must identify such issues and ﬁx them. Some formats
may have been correctly loaded, but not optimal for us. For example, in
the dataset, we see that deposit is a categorical ﬁeld with values “yes” and
“no”. We would require this to be a numerical value (0/1) for modeling.
This is yet another quick ﬁx we can perform at this stage itself.
 
Another extremely important check is to identify missing information.
Note that this is not as straightforward as identifying the number of null
values. Data logging/creation processes often enter a placeholder value
when the true value is unavailable, and these commonly appear as
unknown or In other cases, there are domain-speciﬁc codes for such
missing information. Therefore, understanding the domain is also essential
to correctly identify missing values.
  
Note: At this stage, we only identify the missing values and do not handle
them. Handling will be performed at a later stage as an explicit step. The
only exception is missing values in the outcome/ target variable, and such
records can be dropped. For all others, we need to perform careful
handling.

Caution: Leakage during pre-processing
   
These activities before the train test split can lead to leakage – detailed
univariate analysis, correlation and other multivariate analyses,
scaling/standardizing features, and outlier handling. Unfortunately ,
these are sometimes done before the train test split. This is not a good
practice and leads to leakage, that is, you end up learning information
from the test set and use it for pre-processing.
   
Caution: Category encoding before train-test split
   
It is common to handle categorical data (for example, one hot
encoding) before the train test split. This could be problematic in real-
world situations where the test set contains a new value that was not
encountered in the train set. As a best practice, such handling should
be performed after the train-test split.
Step 2: Separate into train and test datasets
 
The primary purpose of most predictive models is to not do well on the
existing data for which we have the ground truth. Indeed, the true value of
the predictive model is that it can make useful predictions on new data
that the model has never encountered before. We can only trust that the
model generalizes well and can be employed.
 
Such an assessment of model performance is only possible if a dataset is
reserved solely for this purpose. This is accomplished by splitting the
available dataset into two parts train and test. This is the most common
validation scheme, that is, a method to assess the model’ s general
performance. The act of deriving these two sets from the data is called a
“train test Let us now understand how to best use the train and test
datasets:
 
 
Figure 3.6: Train test split
 
Using the train set
 
For the entire learning and training process, understanding the
distributions of the ﬁelds, logic for deﬁning and treating outliers, and
information that informs any of the eight steps in the pre-processing
pipelines. The train set is used for eventually ﬁtting the model, deciding
on the model’ s hyper -parameters 6, Hyperparameter and debugging the
same 4, Model Evaluation and
 
Using the test set
 
The test set is used only for validation of model performance, no
information from this dataset is derived until then. You must not learn any
patterns or make any decisions pertaining to training using the test set, and
this dataset is to be kept aside until the very end.
 
We reiterate that the test set must not be used to inﬂuence the training
process in any way. Not adhering to this, leads to where you effectively
used the test set to train the model. The risk is that you overestimate
model performance (on a good day). On a bad day and if done incorrectly ,
this could lead to drastic differences between performance on the seen
versus unseen data, potentially rendering the model useless.
  
Note: If the dataset was created painstakingly using multiple sources, you
might have already performed many integrity and sense checks, and
maybe even outlier checks and performed descriptive summaries. In such
cases, you can’t unlearn what you learned about the data already . But it is
important to recognize that what you learned might not apply to a
completely fresh test dataset and you should closely monitor the model
performance after deployment.
  
Let us perform a train-test split for our bank marketing for Azra Inc. in the
next exercise.

done properly , and we are ready to move to the next step: outlier
detection and treatment.
Step 3: Outlier treatment
 
There are many ways to deﬁne (univariate) outliers, and the
appropriate choice depends on the nature of the feature and the
observed values. Therefore, the ﬁrst question should be What is an
outlier to me in this feature?
 
A few clarifying questions; Are both high and low values outliers? Are
outliers only values that are sufﬁciently high (For example, an age
value of 25 for high schoolers)? Or are sufﬁciently low values also
outliers (For example, age 5 for high schoolers)? Are negative values
outliers (For example, item delivery time)?
 
Let us look at a couple of deﬁnitions of the term outlier:
 
The usual/classic Unusual values that differ from the others in the data.
This deﬁnition deﬁnes outliers as usually much higher/lower than
regular values, and simply numerical approaches are sufﬁcient to
identify such outliers.
 
A more holistic In this deﬁnition, some values could be rare, but make
sense from a domain perspective and not surprising. These could point
to genuine extremes that we observe (For example, a 25-year -old high
schooler), albeit rarely . Another example is negative bank balances it is
rare, but not surprising and makes perfect sense from a domain
perspective.
 
It is important to note that we must think about the deﬁnition of outlier
for each feature separately .
  
Note: Remember that deﬁning outliers, rules to identify them, and
handling strategy must ALL  be based on the training data alone.
Otherwise, this would be a classic case of data leakage. Once decided,
only the outlier detection logic and handling can be applied to the test
set.
  
Once you have deﬁned what is surprising for each feature, you can
proceed to use one of the methods from the following (non-exhaustive)
list:
 
Percentile threshold/ cut-of f (e.g., p99, p995)
 
Z score approach
 
Box plots / Tukey criterion
 
Let us understand each of them brieﬂy .
Percentile threshold approach
 
This approach is used when we believe a sufﬁciently high/sufﬁciently
low value is an outlier . Consider item delivery times for an e-retail
giant. E-retail companies typically keep track of delivery times by way
of percentiles p95 percentile), or p99 percentile) are common measures
and these companies have a notion of healthy levels for these metrics.
For example, a p99 value of 5 days might be acceptable/expected, but
the p99 value rising to 10 days could point to a major problem. Quite
simply , 1% of orders being delivered after 10 days could be alarming.
 
Such percentile values, therefore, capture what surprising values
should be and are a good approach for outlier detection. Likewise,
order value on an e-commerce platform also sees extremely high
values. Values of 0 are not very surprising (no order was placed), but a
p99 value of $100,000 could be surprising for a B2C clothing
platform.
  
Pause and ponder: Consider the ‘balance’  feature in our bank
marketing case study that captures the customer ’s bank balance. What
kind of values do you expect in it? Which values would be surprising
to you? Sufﬁciently high values?
  
Translating this idea to a simple rule that can be implemented in
Python is as simple as saying any value above a certain percentile is
considered an outlier . For example, the highest 0.5 percentile values
are outliers (above the percentile):
 
cutof f = quantile(feature, p)
 
where p could be high percentiles like 95, 99, 99.5, and so on, or very
low percentiles like 0.05, 1, and so on. Let us now take a look at the Z
Score approach for outlier detection.
Z score approach
 
The Z score approach is suitable when we believe the feature loosely
follows a Gaussian distribution, meaning that we expect most values to
belong to a certain central range. Values signiﬁcantly away from this
central range are considered outliers. In this approach, both extremes
high and low are considered surprising.
 
For each observation, the Z score is calculated as:
 
Z-Score = (X - mean) / std
 
where mean is the simple average of the feature value and std is its
standard deviation. In a Gaussian distribution, values with a
sufﬁciently high or low Z score are considered less probable and more
surprising. It is therefore usual to assign some thresholds based on the
Z score to deﬁne outliers.
  
Pause and ponder: Considering that this approach uses mean and
standard deviation in the criteria. Is this approach robust to outliers? If
your answer is “no”, can you think of ways to mitigate this?
  
A common threshold is 3, that is, values with Z scores greater than 3 or
lower than -3 are considered outliers. In a normal distribution, values
beyond these scores are expected to occur only 0.3% of the time.
Another popular cut-of f is 2.5 which, as expected, is slightly more
relaxed. Choose these or any other cut-of f that you deem suitable for
the data at hand. Let us now look at the third approach the Tukey
criterion.
Box plots/T ukey criterion
 
Another extremely popular approach is the Tukey criterion. Like the Z
score approach, the Tukey criterion is a simple numerical approach
suitable when you expect the data to behave somewhat normally and
both high and low extremes are considered surprising. The Tukey
criterion overcomes the biggest drawback of the Z score approach and
is more robust to extreme values.
 
Mathematically , values outside the following interval are considered
outliers:
 
[Q1 – k x IQR, Q3 + k x IQR];
 
where IQR = Inter Quartile Range is deﬁned as Q3 percentile) – Q1
percentile)
 
From this criterion, the lower threshold for regular value is Q1 – k (Q3
- Q1) and the higher threshold is Q3 + k(Q3 -
 
A very common value of k is 1.5. This value is employed in the
standard implementation of box plots (including matplotlib and
seaborn packages in Python). This is also known as the Tukey
criterion. For k = 1.5, the interval for regular values is:
 
[Q1 – 1.5 x IQR, Q3 + 1.5 x IQR]
  
Note: Many distributions in the real world follow exponential
distributions, where you have a very large number of observations at
the lower end and a few extremely high values. Think of wealth – a
very small percentage typically commands a very high proportion of
the total wealth. Another example could be the number of orders a
customer places in a visit on an e-retail platform where most customers
have 0 orders placed.
   
Pause and ponder: Consider an exponential distribution like that of
bank balance. Do you think the Tukey criterion/Box plot approach is
suitable for this?
  
With this, we have learnt 3 approaches to detecting outliers. As a wise
data scientist, you must understand these approaches, the
considerations in using each and their strengths, and drawbacks. Now ,
let us try these approaches in our bank marketing case study .
Exercise 3.4: Outlier detection and treatment
 
In this exercise, we will apply the outlier detection techniques we learned
to the marketing case study for Azra Inc. We will evaluate the 2 numeric
ﬁelds for outliers, viz. age and balance. We will employ suitable outlier
handling approaches for each case if outliers are detected. Let us begin.
 
Create a box plot for the age feature from the df_train dataset using the
pandas series plot method, using the following code:
 
df_train.age.plot.box(ﬁgsize=[3,5])
 
plt.show()
 
You should get the following output:
 
 
Figure 3.9: Box plot of age
 
The box plot shows that there are some high values above 75. Using the
Tukey criterion, these can be considered potential outliers (i.e., the cut-of f
would be 75). Let us check out the true cut-of fs as per the Tukey criterion.
 
Calculate IQR (Q3 – Q1) and subsequently the lower and upper thresholds
based on the Tukey criterion. Display the obtained thresholds. Use the
following code:
 
Q3 = df_train.age.quantile(0.75)
 
Q1 = df_train.age.quantile(0.25)
 
IQR = Q3 - Q1
 
print("Lower cutof f:", Q1 - 1.5*IQR, \
 
"; Upper cutof f:", Q3 + 1.5*IQR)
 
The cut-of fs should be printed as:
 
Lower cutof f: 6.5; Upper cutof f: 74.5
 
Note that the upper cutof f matches with the box plot.
 
Based on our domain understanding, values preceding 75 are not
problematic, and we do not consider these as genuine outliers and
therefore do not handle them.
  
Note: The age column has a continuous range with a few high values.
These are not far off and seem connected to the main values. These seem
to be genuine high values and not truly outliers. One approach would be
to NOT  perform any outlier treatment for this column. Instead, we could
transform this column to follow any preferred distribution (in a later
section).
   
Create a box plot for the balance feature using the following code:
 
df_train.balance.plot.box(ﬁgsize=[4,5])
 
plt.show()
 
This should result in the following output:
 
 
Figure 3.10: Box plot of balance
 
We seem to have some values far from the usual, and values like 60,000
are way higher than the median, which seems much closer to 0. Let us
ﬁnd out the thresholds based on the Tukey criterion.
 
Calculate and display the thresholds for balance based on the Tukey
criterion. Use the following code:
 
Q3 = df_train.balance.quantile(0.75)
 
Q1 = df_train.balance.quantile(0.25)
 
IQR = Q3 - Q1
 
print("Lower cutof f:", Q1 - 1.5*IQR, \
 
"; Upper cutof f:", Q3 + 1.5*IQR)
 
The thresholds get printed as:
 
Lower cutof f: -2245.5; Upper cutof f: 4062.5
 
The upper cutof f seems to be which is way lower than the observed highs
around 80,000. Recall that we expect the balance feature to not follow a
Gaussian, but an exponential/power distribution. Let us see the top
percentiles for it:
 
Calculate percentiles p95, p99, p995 for the balance variable:
 
df_train.balance.quantile([0.95, 0.99, 0.995])
 
You should get the following output:
 
0.950 5915.900
 
0.990 13341.160
 
0.995 20061.165
 
Name: balance, dtype: ﬂoat64
 
The percentile is around 5.9K (note that this is higher than the Tukey
criterion value). Also, note that around 5.9K, we have a fair number of
observations. A higher cut-of f is therefore more suitable, and a cut-of f of
around 20K seems more suitable.
 
Using 20K as the drop all records with a higher balance value. Print the
shape of the dataframe to see the updated number of records:
 
cutof f_balance = 20000
 
df_train = df_train[df_train.balance <= cutof f_balance].copy()
 
df_train.shape
 
The dimensions get printed as:
 
(8876, 11)
 
Now , apply the same cut-of f to remove outliers in balance from the test
data. Print out the updated dimensions of the test dataset.
 
df_test = df_test[df_test.balance <= cutof f_balance].copy()
 
df_test.shape
 
The updated dimensions are:
 
(2220, 11)
 
In this exercise, we applied different outlier detection methods to the
numeric ﬁelds in our dataset. We observed that while ‘age’  has higher
values, we do not consider them genuine outliers and therefore did not
perform any outlier treatment. The feature balance has some extremely
high values that are quite different. Using the percentile cut-of f approach,
we dropped the outliers from the train and test set. Note that the cut-of f
was obtained solely from the train set and only the cut-of f was applied on
the test set. With this, we are ready to learn to tackle another common
problem with real-world data – missing values.
Step 4: Missing value treatment
 
Missing values are a reality of data science projects that plague
practically every machine learning project. Every machine learning
practitioner meets them virtually in their ﬁrst machine learning project.
This problem is particularly challenging because dealing with missing
values is not simple. Doing it right involves several steps and a great
deal of understanding of the data, the domain, and the task at hand. Let
us discuss a few considerations.
  
Note: About the impact of the task at hand. For time series forecasting,
the approach to handling missing values completely differs from usual
predictive models where the observations are independent. A new
observation in time series data inherently depends on the previous. The
strategies for dealing with missing values must be different. Handling
missing values in time series is out of the scope of this book. This
Arxiv paper (https://arxiv .org/abs/201 1.11347) discusses some
approaches.
  
First, not all missing values are obvious. In Exercise we saw some null
values in the Pandas dataframe with obvious misses that are easy to
identify . We also saw that three columns had values indicating that the
information was somehow missing –p_outcome, contact, and
education had an unknown value. This indicates that the information is
missing, but because these are not null values in the dataframe, it was
not easy to detect them. This is a good example of hidden missing
 
Once the missing values are identiﬁed, the practitioner must meditate
on the cause of these missing values. This understanding (to the best of
your capacity) is critical for a good outcome. Sometimes, the missing
value could be due to random factors that have nothing to do with the
customers or their information (For example, random drops when
logging streaming data with a high event rate). Such missing values
are known to be Missing completely at random These are the least
harmless missing values as they are truly non-informative and do not
cause systematic issues with the data.
 
The other , insidious, extreme is the Missing not at random situation. In
such a case, there is an underlying logic because of which certain
observations or features are missing more than the others. Revisiting
the survey data example, surveys containing potentially sensitive
questions may be skipped by some respondents more than others. In
such a case, these missing values are not uninformative the fact that
they are missing itself is information. Extreme care must be taken in
handling these missing values.
 
The approach to handling missing values changes with the type of
missing value and the mechanism from which the values stemmed.
Consider the case of e-commerce customer data with the number of
orders they placed in the past month as a ﬁeld. The ﬁeld could be
missing for a lot of customers. But this could simply be because of the
way the data was joined which resulted in null values for customers
who did not place any order . In this case, a missing value had a
Handling this case could be as simple as replacing the null values with
0.
 
In general, there are a few approaches to handling missing values:
 
Replace by true meaning (if known).
 
Replace (impute) with a simple central tendency (mean and median).
 
Estimate (impute) the probable value using multivariate analysis.
 
Drop the records.
 
Use techniques that work with missing values.
 
We have already seen an example of option 1 – the e-commerce data
where null values could be replaced by their true meaning, that is, a 0.
Options 2 and 3 are related in that both replace the missing value with
some expected value, with the second approach being more complex.
Option 5 is also safe, even though it restricts the options for machine
learning techniques you can use. Option 4 is a special case and a tricky
one at that.
 
Losing records drops potential information in the dataset that could be
useful in improving your predictive model’ s performance. This could
be a viable option if the missing values are few and are missing
completely at random. But if you believe these are not missing at
random, that is systematic misses, then dropping these would drop
information and potentially some important records. The dataset may
not represent the general data and the true performance of the model
will suffer. Therefore, we must be careful and drop only when we do
not suspect systematic misses.
 
There are various approaches to handling such situations, and Option 5
is one. Additionally , in case the ﬁeld must be dropped, the fact that the
ﬁeld was missing for certain records could be informative and can be
provided to the model using an indicator variable containing 1 when
the original ﬁeld was missing a value, 0 otherwise. If there is a pattern
in missingness, the model can potentially detect it with the help of this
ﬁeld.
  
Note: All the techniques we discussed here are for dealing with the
features/ independent variables. In a predictive modeling exercise,
records with values missing for the outcome must be dropped, as we
did for the ‘deposit’  ﬁeld in Exercise
   
Note: Handling missing values is a very complex topic. Over the past
decades, various techniques have been developed for handling them.
While the techniques could be complicated, the principles aren’ t
different from what we discussed.
  
It is important to remember that handling the missing values in the
data should be done separately for train and test datasets. Even if you
take the simple approach of mean imputation the mean must be
calculated using the train data alone and using the entire dataset would
lead to data leakage.
 
Let us see these techniques in action and handle the missing values in
our data in the next exercise.
Step 5: Categorical feature handling
 
In general, the dataset will have variables of mixed types numerical
and categorical. All implementations of predictive modeling
techniques in Python are built to handle numerical features, and most
predictive modeling technique implementations in Python do not
handle categorical values. Therefore, the categorical values must be
encoded into numerical values. But before we start encoding, there are
some smart approaches you can employ . These are often ignored but
can help greatly simplify this part of the pre-processing and help avoid
losing potential information from the data. A more holistic approach
would have the following steps:
 
Reduce levels (club similar ones) in a
 
We look at the unique values in the feature and how they relate to the
outcome/tar get. Levels that contain similar information and have a
similar relation to the target (For example, similar average outcome
value) can be clubbed/condensed into a single level. This step alone
can help you reduce the levels signiﬁcantly .
 
Combine features with similar/complementary
 
Often, we have features that contain similar or even complementary
information. A classic example from the health domain is weight and
height contain complementary information for heart disease prediction.
These can be combined into Body Mass Index which is a simple ratio
between weight and height. Similarly , in our case study , both features
around a personal loan or a housing loan capture similar information
that the customer has taken a loan and can potentially be clubbed. We
will see these details later in the section on feature engineering.
 
Encoding (dummy , target, effect, and so
 
After making these simpliﬁcations, we can proceed to convert the
categorical values to numbers by using one of the many encoding
strategies. Schemes like One Hot Encoding/Dummy Encoding, Label
Encoding, Target Encoding, Effect Encoding, and Deviation Coding
are a few examples. At the end of this process, the original categorical
column is replaced by one or more numerical features.
  
Note: 1 and 2 are non-standard and again require an understanding of
the data and the domain and are task dependent. A thorough analysis of
the train data must be performed to perform these steps efﬁciently .
Solving our case study
 
In our case study for marketing for Azra Inc., we have a few categorical
features:
 
The customer ’s occupation
 
Highest education level of the customer (primary to tertiary)
 
Whether the customer had a housing loan (yes/no)
 
Whether the customer had a personal loan (yes/no)
 
Preferred contact channel of the customer (telephone/cellular)
 
Month on contact in the campaign
 
Outcome of the campaign before this campaign
  
Pause and ponder: For our case study , which strategies can you already
think of? Which features can be potentially clubbed? For which columns
would you like to evaluate condensing levels?
  
First, we will explore the different values in the column and how they
relate to the target/outcome. This will help us identify some candidate
levels that we can potentially club. The simplest way to do this will be to
calculate the average value of the target for each of the levels in the
categorical feature and plot them. Let us begin by doing so for the job
column. Use the following code to plot the average target value for each
job category:
 
bank1.groupby('job')['deposit'].mean().sort_values().plot.barh()
 
plt.title()
 
plt.show()
 
The output should be as follows:
 
 
Figure 3.11: Job categories and corresponding success rates
 
We notice that there are 12 unique job categories in the ﬁeld, and we can
see that the success rate varies signiﬁcantly by category . We also observe
that some categories, though logically very different, seem to have very
similar values for the outcome. This understanding will help immensely
while handling this feature. Let us create similar views for all the
categorical features in one go. Use the following code:
 
cat_cols = df_train.select_dtypes("object").columns
 
plt.ﬁgure(ﬁgsize=[15,12])
 
for ind, col in enumerate(cat_cols):
 
plt.subplot(3,3,ind+1)
 
bank1.groupby(col)['deposit'].mean().sort_values().plot.barh()
 
plt.title(col)
 
plt.show()
 
The output should be as follows:
 
 
Figure 3.12: Levels and success rates for all categorical features at once
 
Figure 3.12 is extremely informative. An analysis of each of the sub-plots
is necessary to understand each feature and gain ﬁrst ideas on handling
them. Let us start with the handling strategy for the feature job.
 
We saw that the feature job has 12 unique levels. Using an encoding
scheme like Dummy Encoding or One Hot Encoding directly on this
would create 11 or 12 new features from a single feature. This is not a
great idea as it increases model complexity , and you can potentially lose
information when dropping some of these new features later. A better idea
would be to perform target encoding. Let us understand it better .
 
The job categories services, housemaid, entrepreneur , and blue-collar have
similar success rates. These jobs themselves are very different, but for our
modeling activity , they are expected to have a similar effect on the
outcome. In our encoding, therefore, we can assign similar numerical
values to these. The value is simply the average value of the target. If we
replace the category with the average target value, then we have a
different value for each level in a single column and the difference
between the levels is also retained.
  
Note: Another option here was clubbing the categories. However because
the job categories are logically very different, we do not club them using
our domain understanding. Additionally , there could be some other
information in the dataset related to the job category that we do not want
to lose out on.
 
 
Let us implement target encoding for the job feature. For this, we could
either create our own simple mappings or use the category_encoders
library in Python. We recommend using the library as it has a host of other
approaches that you can employ with ease using sklearn-styled syntax.
You can install the package using the pip command:
 
pip install category_encoders
 
More details about the package can be found at
https://pypi.or g/project/category-encoders/.
 
Let us begin by importing and instantiating a target encoder from the
category_encoders package using the following code:
 
from category_encoders.tar get_encoder import TargetEncoder
 
enc_tar get = TargetEncoder()
 
To ﬁt the that is, calculate the average outcome levels, and to extract these
values, we will use the ﬁt_transform method of the We will extract these
values into a new column that we will create on the df_train dataset. The
method needs two inputs the categorical column to encode, and the target
values. Use the following code:
 
df_train['job_encoded'] = enc_tar get.ﬁt_transform(df_train.job,
df_train.deposit)
 
This simple command should do the job. To verify if this was done
correctly , we can use the following groupby command and see the value
assigned to each level. Use the following code:
 
df_train.groupby(['job','job_encoded']).size()
 
The output should be:
 
 
Figure 3.13: Result from the target encoding
 
We can see that for each job category , the job_encoded ﬁeld has a single
value. It is easy to verify that these are the same values that we saw in the
exploratory plot earlier . Target encoding was as simple as that. Now , we
need to apply this encoding to the test data. Note that we are ﬁtting on the
train, but only transforming on the test data. Use the following code:
 
df_test['job_encoded'] = enc_tar get.transform(df_test.job)
  
Caution: Because Target encoding directly uses the target values for
creating a feature, there is a risk of overﬁtting and therefore a lack of
generalization to new data. It is therefore advised that some smoothing or
regularization be applied when using target encoding. This can be done
via some variations of the target encoding like ‘Leave One Fold Out‘ or
with noise addition. Or, can be done via explicit regularization while
modeling (more on this in future chapters).
  
With that, the target encoding is complete for the job ﬁeld. The original
column job can be dropped from both train and test datasets. Using other
encoders is just as simple with similar syntax.
 
Let us now handle the outcome categorical ﬁeld. We saw earlier in Figure
3.12 that this ﬁeld has 4 levels. Revisiting the ﬁgure, we observe that the
success rates for the categories vary, but one category that stands out from
the rest is success. Customers who had previously responded positively to
a campaign were much more likely to respond positively to a new
campaign. This makes logical sense from a domain perspective. The other
categories have values like each other . For our task, we can see that one
category is special.
 
A natural strategy could be to condense the levels from 4 to 2. The two
levels would be a success and all Realize that if we are reducing to two
levels, we need not create 2 new categories. Instead, we could create an
indicator The feature would hold a value of 1 to indicate success and 0
otherwise. We can achieve this by a simple mapping, as we did for the
deposit column in Exercise
 
Let us now approach the ﬁeld We see that there are 3 levels, and all have
different success rates. No category stands out. One hot encoding/dummy
encoding is a good choice for such cases. Essentially , we create indicator
variables for each of the columns. Therefore, there would be three
indicator variables one for each of married, single, and divorced. Let us
use the OneHotEncoder method from category_encoders for this purpose.
Begin by importing and instantiating the using the following code:
 
from category_encoders.one_hot import OneHotEncoder
 
enc_onehot = OneHotEncoder(return_df=T rue, use_cat_names=T rue)
 
Specifying use_cat_names ensures that the indicator variable names are
descriptive and contain the level name as a preﬁx. Specifying return_df
ensures that a dataframe is returned for easy joining with the main dataset.
Now , let us ﬁt and transform the df_train dataset using the marital feature
using the following code and print out the ﬁrst 5 records of the result:
 
dumms = enc_onehot.ﬁt_transform(df_train.marital)
 
dumms.head()
 
The output should be as follows:
 
 
Figure 3.14: Columns from one hot encoding
 
We can see that, as expected, 3 columns were created by the encoder , one
for each level. It is important to note there is redundancy in these 3 new
columns, all 3 columns are not needed to infer the marital status. For
instance, if indicators for both married and divorced are 0, then the
original value must be single. We can therefore drop one of these. Let us
go ahead and drop the column for divorced, and append it to the original
dataframe. Let us also drop the original column marital from df_train to
remove all redundancy . Use the following code:
 
df_train = df_train.join(dumms.drop('marital_divorced', axis = 1))
 
df_train.drop('marital', axis=1, inplace=T rue)
 
df_train.head()
 
You should get the following result:

To complete the handling of this ﬁeld, we need to apply this encoding to
the test data as well. To do so use the following code:
 
dumms_test = enc_onehot.transform(df_test.marital)
 
df_test = df_test.join(dumms_test.drop('marital_divorced',axis = 1))
 
df_test.drop('marital',axis=1,inplace=T rue)
 
With that, we have completed the encoding for the ﬁeld marital as well.
So far, we have discussed/handled 3 ﬁelds already . The remaining

We are one step closer to completing the pre-processing of the data and
