somewhat standard step in pre-processing of numerical features, that is,
scaling.
Step 6: Transformation of numeric features
 
Another very common pre-processing step for numerical features is
transformation. The most common forms of transformations are
scaling, standardization, or normalization (non-standard usage). All of
them usually attempt to bring the different numerical features to a
similar value range. Depending on the task at hand, this could be an
essential step or just an optional one.
 
For most supervised machine learning techniques, scaling the features
does not drastically inﬂuence the model accuracy . This is especially
true for smaller datasets where the background calculations in ﬁtting
the model can be done quickly regardless of the different value ranges.
This is not the case for situations involving large datasets and
modeling techniques like deep neural networks. The optimisation
algorithms employed by these techniques are sensitive to scale in the
sense that reaching an optimum is much faster if features are on a
similar scale. Such situations prefer that the features are 0-centered
(have a mean close to 0) and values close by on either side. When all
features are 0-centered and have a similar range of values, the
optimisation algorithms are expected to work fastest. Therefore, for
efﬁciency , it is a good idea to scale the features.
 
Another beneﬁt of scaling for supervised machine learning is the ease
of interpretation. For a linear or logistic regression model, for example,
it is much easier to interpret and compare the impact of each feature if
the scales are the same. In the absence of scaling, practitioners resort
to calculating standardized coefﬁcients to compare them. This step is
not needed if you scale the features before ﬁtting the model.
 
Some popularly applied transformations are:
 
Standard Makes the ﬁeld 0 mean, unit standard deviation (Z scores).
 
Min-Max Limits to a value between 0 and 1.
 
Other Converts the distribution to approximate Gaussian (or even
uniform) using Quantile/Power/Log transforms.
  
Note: For a larger list of transformations implemented in sklearn, you
can refer to the ofﬁcial sklearn documentation: https://scikit-
learn.or g/stable/modules/classes.html#module-sklearn.preprocessing
  
For most situations, it is sufﬁcient to constrain the different features to
similar values. Standard scaling replaces values effectively with Z
scores, making it 0 mean and unit standard deviation. This would also
mean that most data points would be in the range [-3,3] as values
outside them have low probabilities. Such a transformation beneﬁts
deep neural networks particularly owing to the nature of optimization
algorithms employed.
 
For our marketing model for Azra Inc., let us perform transformation
for the two numerical features age and balance in the next exercise.
Exercise 3.6: Transforming numerical variables:
 
In this exercise, we will perform transformations on the two numerical
features in the dataset for marketing for Azra Inc., age, and balance. Note
that we do not wish to change the way the values are distributed (that is,
no power/log/quantile transforms) but merely change the range of the
values. We will use the standard scaling approach for this purpose. Let us
begin the transformations.
 
First, plot the two variables on box plots to understand their distribution

vars_num = ['age', 'balance']

Note: On applying the scaler to the test dataset, the values might not be
exactly as expected. If standard scaling was used, the mean of the columns
in the test data might not be exactly 0 and the standard deviation might
not be exactly 1. This is because the mean and standard deviation of the
train data could be slightly different from the test data (it usually is). This
is perfectly ﬁne and doesn’ t cause any signiﬁcant drop in model
performance.
 
  
Print the ﬁrst 5 records of the train dataset to have a look at it after all the
pre-processing:
 
df_train.head()
 
The records get printed as follows:
 
 
Figure 3.22: Data after the pre-processing
 
This exercise concludes the eighth and ﬁnal step in our data pre-
processing pipeline. The data is now ready to be supplied to a model and
the entire model ﬁtting process can now begin.

Conclusion
 
In this chapter , we focused on data pre-processing, a core step in
building a machine learning solution, that comes in between Business
Understanding and Model building in the CRISP-DM framework.
There are several steps involved in data pre-processing. This process is
rarely standardized and can/should not be automated. The primary
reason is that there are numerous domain, data, and task speciﬁcs that
must be accounted for in the process.
 
Azra Inc. bank needed a machine learning solution for their marketing
campaign. Through our numerous pre-processing steps, we helped
them go from somewhat raw data to a much more reﬁned and tuned
dataset that will help build a high-performing predictive model. To
begin, we ﬁxed formats, identiﬁed issues and ﬁxed the simple ones
right away . A train-test split is essential before proceeding further with
detailed analysis and data processing. We do not want data leakage and
analyzing the train dataset alone is a good way to ensure this. Outlier
treatment, numerical transformations, categorical feature handling and
other feature engineering decisions were all made using the train data.
The decisions were then only applied to the test set.
 
With redundancies removed, additional features generated (all
informed by data analysis), along with the incorporation of our domain
knowledge, we are far better off than where we began. We have
optimized the dataset that goes into the model. This completes one
major step towards a more optimized machine learning/ AI model.
 
In the next chapter , we will learn how to assess the performance of the
predictive model that we build on this pre-processed data. We will
learn the best way to assess the generalized performance of our model.
If things are not right, debug the model and ﬁgure out improvement
areas.

CHAPTER 4
Model Evaluation and Debugging
Introduction
 
As a data scientist in a large, multinational fast fashion giant, Azra
Inc., you have been working on a model to show users the most helpful
product reviews. Products can gather several reviews on the platform,
and popular items have thousands of them. The goal is to identify
reviews the user ﬁnds most helpful and display them at the top when
the product page loads. You choose a classiﬁcation-based approach -
predicting whether the user will ﬁnd the review helpful. The review
with the highest predicted probability of being helpful gets ranked at
the top. What do you suppose is a good model for this situation?
Additionally , you may have identiﬁed the right technique and aimed to
improve the model. How do you diagnose poor model performance
and identify corrective actions? Model debugging is also important for
a practitioner .
 
How would you evaluate the various competing models? Is the low
latency (faster predictions) model with decent accuracy better than a
high accuracy model with higher latency? Is an interpretable model
more important than a black box model? Numerous considerations go
into deﬁning and choosing the best model and pure accuracy is seldom
the sole criterion. In this chapter , we will understand these
considerations in detail. We will also learn a principled way to
diagnose and models to identify the cause of poor performance and
improve our models. We will go through real-world examples to
practice and solidify these learnings.
Structure
 
This chapter will cover the following topics:
 
•Ad click prediction case study
 
•Model evaluation considerations
 
•Model evaluation metrics
 
∘Metrics for classiﬁcation
 
∘Metrics for regression
 
•Model evaluation schemes
 
•Model debugging
 
∘Overﬁtting and underﬁtting
 
∘Handling overﬁtting
Objectives
 
In this chapter , we will learn about model evaluation in the context of
solving business problems. We will understand the various
considerations that make one model better than another . We will learn
about metrics for model evaluation, classiﬁcation, and regression
problems. Not all errors are equally problematic, and we will
understand several metrics that are beyond simple accuracy . We will
also learn a principled way to diagnose and debug models to identify
the cause of poor performance. Thereafter , we will discuss several
ways to handle underﬁtting and overﬁtting. At the end of the chapter ,
we will go through real-world examples to practice and solidify these
learnings.
Ad click prediction case study
 
Azra Inc. is a multi-national fast fashion giant with millions of daily
active users visiting the mobile app. The home page of the app is prime
digital real estate and brands are keen on advertising their
brand/selection to gain maximum visibility . On-platform advertisement
on e-commerce platforms is extremely popular and a great revenue
stream for the platforms. Azra Inc., though, needs to make sure the ads
are relevant to the customer . Irrelevant ads dilute customer experience
and impact customer retention. Azra Inc. therefore needs to identify
the ads relevant to the customer .
 
A popular approach is to display only those ads that have a higher
chance of being clicked by the customer . An ad being clicked is a
reasonable indicator of relevance; customers are less likely to click on
irrelevant ads. Like most major platforms, Azra Inc. wishes to deploy a
click prediction model that will predict whether a user will click on a
given Such a model can be used to rank ads and the company can
surface only those that have a high chance of being clicked. The input
to such models usually consists of (but not limited to) demographic
information of the customer , information about the context (device
type, OS, and so on), and information about the ad itself (item
category , brand, and so on).
 
Azra Inc. has collected historical data containing some demographic
information about the customer , their estimated salary , and whether
they clicked on the ad that was displayed. Your task as a data scientist
is to create the ad click prediction model using this data. That is, given
user demographic data, predict if they will click on the ad. Let us
understand the data that we have at hand.

evaluation considerations
 
Revisit the review ranking situation (as mentioned in the Introduction
section) for the data scientist at Azra Inc. How would you deﬁne a good
model? The models must generalize well to the unseen data which is a
basic requirement in all situations, and therefore something other than
what we need to focus on. Let us discuss the other considerations.
 
We know the ranking is user-speciﬁc and a thousand reviews (as
mentioned in the Introduction section) may need to be ranked. The
reviews need to be ranked as soon as the page is loaded. The scoring and
ranking need to be extremely quick. This means that speed (low latency)
is an extremely important criterion - a model that takes several seconds to
rank these thousand reviews is simply unacceptable. The good model
must be quick.
 
Another consideration is that user preferences and behaviors keep
changing frequently , and what interests the user today might not be
relevant on the next visit. This means that frequent retraining of the model
is a requirement and you may need to retrain the model daily . Therefore, a
good model must be easy and quick to retrain. Model interpretability is
another consideration - in this case, the model is an internal model
employed for a (relatively) less risky task (compared to disease diagnosis
or loan approvals). For such a situation, a highly interpretable model that
allows auditing for fairness is optional. Therefore, while interpretability
would be nice, it is optional for a good model in this situation.
 
Coming to accuracy - does the situation have very steep accuracy
requirements? That is, does the most helpful review always need to be
ranked ﬁrst? We display several reviews at once. It is okay if the most
helpful review is ranked second or third. The user will potentially read
multiple reviews, so the top review does not have to be the winner . We
have a low accuracy requirement, and a good model can have acceptable
accuracy .
 
Putting it all together , we now have an idea of a good model for our
situation: a low-latency model with acceptable accuracy that is easy and
quick to retrain.
 
In the example, accuracy was discussed at the end. Indeed, accuracy is
only one of the criteria for evaluating a model. In general, there are
several considerations in evaluating a model. Refer to the following
ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure General considerations in model evaluation
 
The following list is the main considerations:
 
•Training time : Higher training time often equates to higher costs and
might make frequent retraining prohibitively time-consuming and
expensive. For example, models needing to be retrained once a month can
afford signiﬁcantly higher training times than those needing daily
retraining.
 
•Inference time : This is when the model predicts a record (referred to as
the latency of the model). In online, near real-time systems, inference
times have hard constraints that must be respected to be useful (for
example inference time of at most ﬁve milliseconds). A higher latency can
be acceptable in ofﬂine scoring systems with fewer predictions.
  
Pause and ponder: In a real-time situation, where inference time needs to
be extremely short, which modeling technique might be more appropriate
- a K Nearest Neighbor model, or a decision tree model? Which of these
approaches is expected to have faster inference times?
  
•Performance : We refer to how well the model predicts the outcome. For
classiﬁcation problems, this could be plain accuracy , precision, recall, or
one of the many available measures (discussed in the next section). This
could be R2, MAE, MAPE, and so on for regression problems.
  
Note: Choosing from the various model performance metrics itself has
several considerations. Certain situations have very speciﬁc requirements
on model performance that dictate the choice of the metric, and we will
learn these in the next section.
  
•Explainability : Sit uations where model bias can have serious
consequences necessitate that the model be interpretable and can be
audited for fairness, this is a necessity for several regulatory
environments. Additionally , it is critical for explanatory models (the
purpose is to explain behavior). However , there are several situations
where explainability is not a concern.
 
•Data requirements : A linear regression model and a deep neural network
surely have different data requirements. The former can do with a hundred
records, but the latter usually needs thousands at the least. Low data size
is typical in several domains necessitating fewer data-hungry models. On
the other hand, platforms with millions of customers typically have
millions of data points in Terabytes of data that the most complex models
can work with.
 
•Context-speciﬁc constraints : Constraints can also emer ge from the
speciﬁc context and models that do not respect these constraints can be
useless. For example, if a situation needs predictions to have conﬁdence
intervals, then only a few models can even be considered.
 
To summarize, there are multiple considerations in model evaluation and
accuracy is merely one of them. We must respect the constraints of the
system in which the model is to be employed. The model is useless
otherwise. A model depends on the situation, and it is up to the data
scientist, to use the domain understanding and judgment to choose the
best model. In the next section, we will learn model evaluation metrics in
detail to help us concretely evaluate model performance. Before we get
there, to make things more concrete, let us prepare a baseline model to
predict ad clicks.

evaluation metrics
 
Model performance evaluation to assess how well the model predicts
the outcome - is a core criterion for evaluating the goodness of a
model. While this is not the only criterion in general, as we saw in the
previous section, this becomes the deciding factor in several situations.
It is therefore imperative for a data science practitioner to be proﬁcient
in model performance evaluation by skillfully identifying:
 
•The right metrics for assessment
 
•The evaluation scheme to reliably estimate the generalized
performance of the model
 
The various evaluation schemes will be discussed in a bit. This section
will focus on the right metrics for assessment, which has various
considerations, as you will learn. Let us begin by appreciating the
machine learning problem; there are numerous metrics to evaluate
model performance. We will list and deﬁne some metrics for
classiﬁcation and regression problems and calculate them for the click
prediction case study . Let us begin with metrics for classiﬁcation.
for classiﬁcation problems
 
In problems, the task is correctly predicting the outcome/ label/
category that the item belongs to. The outcome can have two or more
categories. For binary classiﬁcation problems, the outcome has two
possible values. The outcome of our click prediction case study can be
clicked or not clicked. Likewise, the outcome for a spam detection
model would be either spam or not spam. Indeed, binary classiﬁcation
problems are everywhere- predicting customer churn, device failure,
ad clicks, loan default, lead conversion, and so on.
 
Multi-class classiﬁcation problems have more than two classes to
predict.
 
We have already seen and employed accuracy in Chapter 3, Data
Collection and Accuracy measures the proportion of cases that were
correctly classiﬁed that is the model predicted their true category/label.
If 90 of the 100 records were correctly predicted, we say the model has
90% accuracy . This is extremely useful and one of the most commonly
used metrics.
 
It is important to note that accuracy does not care about the labels. It
does not treat positives as different from negatives and getting a
positive example right is considered as good as getting a negative
example right. As a corollary , it considers getting a positive example
wrong as problematic as getting a negative one wrong. We therefore
need to think beyond accuracy and consider metrics that closely match
our business requirements and make our model more suitable for the
problem at hand. Let us look at some options.
  
Pause and ponder: Think about a model to detect HIV. Is not detecting
HIV as problematic as a false positive that is a non-HIV  case identiﬁed
as an HIV case? If there is a difference, which type of error do you
think is more important to avoid?
matrix
 
We saw that plain old accuracy does not care about class and looks at
the overall correctness of the model. Often, we care about one outcome
and getting it right more than the other . Coming back to the question
around HIV detection - we asked if identifying one outcome is more
important than the other . You may have identiﬁed that this question
needs to be more concrete. Let us formulate this better by
understanding the types of errors that can occur in this situation.
 
For binary classiﬁcation problems, there are two real outcomes and
two predicted outcomes. This means that four possible situations can
occur for each prediction/record which a confusion matrix can
conveniently express. Table 4.1 shows a general-purpose confusion
matrix. We have actual outcomes in the rows, while the columns
contain the predictions. Table 4.1 uses positive/negative labels for the
outcomes, but the confusion matrix works the same if the outcomes are
1/0, True/False and so on.
 
on.
on.
on. on.  on.
on. on.  on.
 
Table Confusion Matrix showing the possible cases
 
The four cases are through A and D and are easy to understand. A and
D are cases that the model got right:
 
•True positives (A): The positive outcome was correctly predicted as
positive.
 
•True negatives (D): The negative outcome was correctly predicted as
negative.
 
While cases B and C are errors made by the model:
 
•False negatives (B): The positive outcome was incorrectly predicted
as negative.
 
•False positives (C): The negative outcome was incorrectly predicted
as positive.
 
To make things more concrete, look at the conceptual confusion matrix
for our ad click prediction case study in Table The rows represent the
truth, actual click or no click, while the columns contain the model
predictions. The four cases contextualized to the case study , mean:
 
•True positives: Clicks correctly predicted by the model
 
•True negatives: Non-clicks correctly predicted by the model
 
•False positives: Non-clicks incorrectly predicted as clicks
 
•False negatives: Clicks incorrectly predicted as non-clicks

Table Confusion Matrix for the Click Prediction Case Study
 
Revisiting the question from earlier: Are all error types equally
problematic in all situations? Taking the case of HIV detection think
about the consequences of each error type. A False positive, a healthy
patient incorrectly identiﬁed as HIV positive, is typically followed by
another conﬁrmatory test to rule out the error . While it may cause
emotional trauma to the patient for a while, the error is usually
identiﬁed. Now , consider a false negative an HIV positive case not
detected. This patient would go out unaware of their true condition,
risking their lives and potentially of others around them. The
consequences of a false negative can be dire. A model for HIV
detection must therefore focus on detecting all true cases, even if it
means getting a few more false positives. A confusion matrix is a
perfect tool to check this breakdown and identify these cases. Let us
now create the confusion matrix for the ad click prediction case study
and evaluate the performance of the various models.

We created confusion matrices for the three models to get a detailed
breakdown of model performance (on the train dataset). From the
confusion matrices, we could also see which class is getting misclassiﬁed
more than the other . It is easy to derive the extent of true positives, false
negatives, and so on. from the confusion matrix, making it a useful tool
for assessing model performance. We will now take a look at some more
metrics which can be used for speciﬁc ways of evaluation and can
programmatically be calculated easily and used for model comparison.
Precision, Recall, and F1 Score
 
As discussed earlier , in many situations, we care about one class more
than the other . This is particularly true when the objective is to detect an
event. For a loan default prediction exercise, for example, most loans are
not defaulted, and detecting a default is more interesting than identifying
non-defaults. Likewise, the actual fraud cases are more interesting in a
fraud detection situation than the regular , non-fraud cases. Note that this is
also true for the ad prediction case study where we are more interested in
identifying true clicks. This is true of many practical situations. We,
therefore, need metrics that focus on model performance in one of the
classes. For the ad click prediction case, we would be interested in metrics
that help us understand the performance around true clicks better . While
these metrics can be applied to the positive and negative classes, they are
employed for the positive classes in most situations. We deﬁne the metrics
in Table Let us understand these metrics better with some concrete
examples.
 
examples.
examples. examples. examples. examples. examples. examples.
examples.
examples. examples. examples. examples. examples.
examples. examples. examples. examples. examples. examples.
 
Precision, Recall, and F1 score
 
Precision score answers the question, what proportion of the predicted
positives are positive? For a fraud detection situation, this would indicate
what proportion of the fraudulent cases we identiﬁed as fraud. Naturally ,
we would want this to be as high as possible; we would not want the
model to identify genuine transactions as fraudulent. For the ad click
prediction situation, a low precision would mean that many cases we
predicted as clicks were not clicks.
 
Recall score answers the question, what proportion of the positives did we
detect? This is particularly relevant when dealing with fraud, where the
recall score tells us the proportion of fraud cases the model detects. In
event detection situations (for example disease detection tests), we would
like the recall to be as high as possible. A high recall score would mean
we could detect the most clicks for the ad click prediction case.
 
F1 score tries to balance the precision and recall scores. But why is this
balance needed? Because there is a trade-of f between precision and recall.
Increasing the recall of the model often comes at the cost of precision that
is when we attempt to increase the proportion of positives we detect, we
often also increase the number of false positives that is cases that are not
positives but predicted as so. Likewise, increasing precision leads to a
reduction in recall. The F1 score therefore is a needed metric that
measures the balance between precision and recall. Unlike the arithmetic
mean, the harmonic mean is more inclined towards the lower of the two
values. This means we will obtain a low score even if one value is
extremely high and the other is low. To get a high F1 score, both precision
and recall need to be high. The F1 score effectively captures the tradeof f
between precision and recall and is a great metric when both precision and
recall are important.
 
As you may have ﬁgured, these metrics can all be derived from the
confusion matrix with a little calculation. However , this is optional as all
these metrics are very conveniently derived with a single module
classiﬁcation_report in sklearn. We can use the classiﬁcation_report
method as shown in the following code for the ad click prediction
example for the SVC model:
 
svc.predict(X_train)))
 
The preceding single line command provides the very detailed output in
Figure The command calculates precision, recall, and F1 score for both
classes. As discussed, we are interested in these metrics for the positive
class. The second row of the output corresponds to the positive class and
contains the precision, recall, and F1 score. For our example, recall (0.44)
is much lower than the precision. Accordingly , the F1 score is at 0.58,
more towards the lower value (Recall) as expected. The overall accuracy
is available in the third row in the output as 0.78. Refer to Figure
 
 
Figure Metrics from the classiﬁcation_report
  
Note: While the classiﬁcation report provides a lot of detailed metrics, you
can only employ this method if you are interested in any speciﬁc metric.
To get only precision, recall or f1 score for the model, you can use the
`precision_score`, `recall_score`, `f1_score` methods from
sklearn.metrics.
  
To calculate only precision, recall or F1 score, you could use the
associated methods in Let us calculate accuracy and recall values for the
three models on the train dataset. Start with importing the functions from
sklearn using the following code:
 
from sklearn.metrics import accuracy_score, recall_score
 
Next, generate and store predictions from the decision tree model on the
train dataset into a numpy array and use the accuracy_score and
recall_score methods as shown in the following code:
 
y_train_pred = dt.predict(X_train)
 
accuracy_score(y_train, y_train_pred))
 
recall_score(y_train, y_train_pred))
 
You should get the following output:
 
DT Accuracy: 0.9678571428571429
 
DT Recall: 0.9479166666666666
 
Similarly , calculate the training accuracy and recall for the RandomForest
model using the following code:
 
y_train_pred = rf.predict(X_train)
 
accuracy_score(y_train, y_train_pred))
 
recall_score(y_train, y_train_pred))
 
You should get the following output:
 
RF Accuracy: 0.9821428571428571
 
RF Recall: 0.96875
 
Finally , print the training accuracy and recall for the SVC model:
 
y_train_pred = svc.predict(X_train)
 
accuracy_score(y_train, y_train_pred))
 
recall_score(y_train, y_train_pred))
 
The output should be as follows:
 
SVC Accuracy: 0.7785714285714286
 
SVC Recall: 0.4375
 
We observe that the accuracy and recall are the highest for the
RandomForest model, followed closely by the decision tree model, with
the SVC model lagging.
 
Precision, recall, and F1 score are extremely useful metrics for the
positive class. These are essential for a data scientist to understand and be
proﬁcient in situations where one class is more interesting and extremely
common. But there are also several situations where the model should
balance performance in both classes and show high skill in differentiating
between the classes. The ROC curve and the area under it are must-learn
metrics for such situations. Let us learn and implement them.
A
rea Under ROC curve (AUC)
 
Area under the Receiver Operator Characteristic curve is another
extremely useful and popular metric. The name is a mouthful, but the
intuition is straightforward. The area under the ROC curve measures how
well the model balances false positives and negatives. Figure 4.9
illustrates the idea of the ROC curve and how it can be used:
 
 
Figure 4.9: ROC Curve ¹
 
The False Positive Rate makes the X axis, while the Y axis represents the
True Positive Rate The false positive rate (False positives/ Total actual
negatives) measures the proportion of negatives the model got wrong.
True positive rate (True positives/T otal actual positives) measures the
proportion of positives correctly predicted. TPR therefore focuses on the
positives and FPR on the negatives. The curve is generated using different
thresholds from 0 to 1 and plotting the point on the graph for the TPR and
FPR obtained. The ROC curve therefore shows how skilled the model is
that is how well the model differentiates the classes.
 
A perfect model would not have any False Positives (FPR of 0) and would
have a 100% True positive rate. As shown in Figure the perfect classiﬁer
would be represented by the dot at the upper left corner as it would have 0
FPR and 100% TPR. A model with no skill, that is, a model which is as
good as randomly assigning the classes (say using a coin ﬂip) would have
equal TPR and FPR at all thresholds. The diagonal dotted line represents
this in Figure A way to summarize model performance is therefore the
area under this curve.
 
The no skill, random classiﬁer will have a 50% area under the curve as it
represents the diagonal line. A perfect classiﬁer will have 100% area under
the curve as it has 100% TPR and 0% FPR and immediately reaches the
top left corner . Most models will be between the no skill and the perfect
classiﬁer that is the area under the ROC curve for most models will be
between 0.5 and 1. This is visually represented in Figure As the curve gets
more towards the upper left corner , the model’ s skill is higher and
therefore the area under the ROC curve.
  
Note: For a more detailed explanation of the ROC curve, its interpretation
and how it relates mathematically to other concepts, you can refer to the
Wikipedia page - Receiver operating characteristic - Wikipedia
  
Let us plot the ROC curve and calculate the AUC (area under the ROC
curve) for our ad click prediction case study for the SVC model. Use the
following code to import the plotting module from sklearn and plot the
ROC curve on the training data. The from_estimator method of
RocCurveDisplay needs the trained classiﬁer , the features and the true
labels using which the ROC curve can be generated. Refer to the
following code:
 
from sklearn.metrics import RocCurveDisplay as roc
 
roc.from_estimator(svc, X_train, y_train)
 
plt.show()
 
You should get the following output:
 
 
Figure ROC Curve for SVC model
 
The ROC Curve for our SVC model (on the training data) is doing a
decent job. As the graph indicates, the AUC is 0.8, signiﬁcantly better
than a no-skill classiﬁer (0.5). Note that you can only plot the entire curve
if interested in the AUC. The AUC can be calculated using the
roa_auc_score method in The roc_auc_score method needs the class
probabilities to calculate the TPR and FPR at different thresholds. We
extract the probabilities from the SVCs’  method. Let us use it in the
following code:
 
from sklearn.metrics import roc_auc_score
 
y_pred_train_probs =
 
roc_auc_score(y_train, y_pred_train_probs)
 
The AUC printed is 0.7967 which matches the value displayed on the
ROC curve (rounded to two decimals). The roc_auc_score can be used to
compare multiple models for performance directly .
 
To summarize, the ROC curve and the AUC are extremely informative
and useful methods when both classes are important to balance
performance on. The AUC summary metric can help compare the
performance of multiple models and help select the best one. To complete
our discussion on classiﬁcation performance metrics, we will now take a
look at the area under the PR curve, a great choice when balancing
precision and recall is required.
under PR curve
 
Area under PR curve is similar to the ROC curve (which balances TPR
and FPR) and the precision-recall curve can be used to see how a model
balances precision and recall. As discussed earlier , there is a tradeof f
between precision and recall, and an improvement in recall often comes at
the cost of reduced precision and vice versa. The PR curve therefore
shows how well a model makes this tradeof f.
 
As shown in Figure the X axis of the PR curve represents the recall, while
the Y axis represents the precision of the model. To generate the plot, the
values of precision and recall are noted and plotted for different
probability thresholds. The perfect classiﬁer would have 100% precision
and 100% recall, represented by a point at the upper -right corner . A
random classiﬁer would have precision like the event rate (% positives)
and a recall that would decrease linearly with the threshold, representing
the dotted line in Figure
 
 
Figure Precision Recall Curve ²
 
The area under the PR curve varies between 0 and 1 and summarizes the
trade-of f between precision and recall made by the model. Let us plot the
PR curve and check the area under it for our ad click prediction case study
for the SVC model on the training data. We will use the
PrecisionRecallDisplay module from as shown in the following code:
 
from sklearn.metrics import PrecisionRecallDisplay as prc
 
prc.from_estimator(svc, X_train, y_train)
 
plt.show()
  
Note: Similar to the roc_auc_score method, you can extract only the area
under the PR curve using the pr_auc_score.
  
This results in the PR curve as shown in Figure The area under the PR
curve is 0.72 as displayed in the plot. This is signiﬁcantly higher than a no
skill classiﬁer (which will have a 0 area), and we perform well on the train
data. Since this is on the training data, the model’ s true performance is
unknown and can be assessed only on unseen data:
 
 
Figure PR Curve for Ad Click SVC model (train data)
 
To summarize, the PR curve is another extremely useful model evaluation
measure that captures the tradeof f between precision and recall. This is
useful when one class is in focus, which is extremely common to note that
in situations with heavy class imbalance (For example, fraud detection,
where the event rate is typically very low) the PR curve is a more reliable
indicator of performance than the ROC curve. We will discuss this in
more detail in Chapter 6, Imbalanced Machine
 
We discussed classiﬁcation metrics in a great amount of detail. Let us now
turn our attention to regression problems. We will look at the most
common and useful metrics for evaluating model performance for
regression tasks.
for regression
 
In regression problems, the target/outcome is a number (as opposed to
a category for classiﬁcation problems). A classic example of a
regression task is predicting the sales of a product on an e-commerce
platform. Likewise, tasks like stock price prediction, product rating
prediction, delivery time prediction, ride arrival time prediction, and
temperature prediction are all regression tasks.
 
Therefore, the notion of error in regression tasks is how far the
predicted value is from the true value. For instance, how far the
predicted sales were from the actual sales of the product. If y_true is
the actual value and y_pred is the prediction for a record, then in the
simplest form the error for this record could be deﬁned as:
 
 
This term is also called residual. The error aggregated over all the
records gives us the overall model error or goodness of ﬁt. There are
various ways in which this error term can be aggregated over all the
records, and normalized in multiple ways, resulting in several metrics
for evaluating regression models. Let us look at a few of them,
beginning with the popular R squared.
 
The R squared for a regression model is deﬁned as:
 
 
Where is the sum of squares of the residuals that is sum of squares of
the errors and is the total variance of the outcome. The R squared
metric therefore represents the proportion of variance in outcome that
the model explains. A perfect model would have 0 error and predict the
outcome perfectly would be 0) and R squared would be 1. A poor
model that does not explain the variance in the outcome has a value
closer to 0. The R squared is a normalized metric and does not depend
on the number of records and is therefore a widely used metric for
regression. R squared’  s drawback is that it uses squared error terms,
and a single large error value can signiﬁcantly impact its value. We
therefore also must look at metrics that are more robust to outliers in
error values.
 
Median absolute percentage error is a very robust alternative. The
MAPE is deﬁned as:
 
 
The MAPE uses the absolute value of the error and normalizes it by
the true value of the instance. This ensures that the value for each
instance is between 0 and 1. Next, because the median of this value is
taken, it is not inﬂuenced by extremely high or extremely low values.
Another beneﬁt of the MAPE metric is that it is highly interpretable as
how bad the typical error is relative to the data and provides an
intuitive understanding of the model’ s goodness.
Model evaluation schemes
 
In most real-world situations, a model is trained on historical data to use it
on newer data. For instance, a loan default prediction model trained on
historical data is useful only if it can be used to help identify future
default cases. Indeed, the true assessment of the goodness of a model is its
performance on data that has not been seen before. This is the reason for
the train test split scheme. A part of the data is reserved as the test is set
for ﬁnal validation, as illustrated in Figure The 80-20 split in the ﬁgure is
only an example and a completely different split can rightfully be chosen
by the data scientist if they see ﬁt for the situation.
 
 
Figure Using holdout set for validation
 
The single holdout validation set approach has its merits, but also a few
drawbacks. First, the test set needs to be used only at the end of the
development process. This makes it difﬁcult to estimate generalized
performance at any intermediate step. Second, the assessment on the test
set is based on a single sample and the estimate is therefore less reliable.
In smaller datasets, this estimate could be based on very few records
making the estimate unreliable, as you might expect.
 
A better approach would be a cross-validation approach; a popular
example of which is the k-fold cross-validation technique. Figure 4.14
illustrates the working of a k-fold cross-validation scheme, taking the
example of 5 folds. The data is divided into 5 folds. Each time, one-fold
acts as the validation fold while the rest of the folks form the training data
for the model. For the ﬁrst row, the last 20% records of the data become
the validation set. The model is trained on the remaining 4 folds of 80% of
data. Next, block 4 becomes the validation set and the remaining 4 folds
constitute the training data. In this manner , a fold is held out each time as
a validation set while the other blocks are used for training. At the end of
this process, each fold has once become a validation set. Each time the
validation set was unseen by the model. The average of performance on
the 5 folds is the ﬁnal estimate of the generalized performance of the
model. An average of over 5 folds is reasonably expected to be a more
robust estimation. Refer to Figure
 
 
Figure 4.14: Example of a 5-fold cross-validation scheme
  
Note: For hyper -parameter tuning, we will use a slightly modiﬁed K-fold
scheme. We will see this in detail in Chapter 6, Hyperparameter Tuning.
   
Note: The number of folds to use is different and depends on the amount
of data you have at hand, the time you can spend on this assessment and
the number you as an individual need to trust the numbers. 3-5 folds are
used commonly .
  
Let us now see model evaluation using cross-validation in action on the ad
click prediction models we prepared.

Model
 
The astute reader would have noticed a big difference between the
training and the models’  cross-validated performance. This is
particularly true for the decision tree and random forest models. Table
4.4 lists the training and cross-validated recall for the three models:
 
models:
models:
models:
models:
 
Table Difference between training and cross-validated recall
 
Focusing on the decision tree model to understand the behavior , we see
that the performance is high on seen data (what it trained on) and much
lower on unseen data. We realize the decision tree model’ s
performance on the train data does not generalize well to unseen data,
and this is a classic case of overﬁtting. In the next section, we
understand this behavior better and learn how to handle this.
Overﬁtting and underﬁtting
 
In the previous section, we studied the training vs. cross-validated
performance of the three models used in the ad click prediction case
study . Table 4.4 showed a big difference in the performance of the
decision tree model: a high recall score on seen data, and a much lower
score on unseen data. On the other hand, the SVC model has a much
lower recall on both seen and unseen data.
 
These are very good examples of overﬁtting and underﬁtting
respectively . In both cases, the model behavior could be better . We
want a model that does well on seen and unseen data. Overﬁtting and
underﬁtting are therefore two extremes, but sides of the same coin a
model that does not learn patterns in the data well. Let us understand
these concepts, what causes them and how to handle them.
Overﬁtting
 
Revisiting the performance of the decision tree model, the numbers from
Table 4.2 tell us the following:
 
•Does the tree perform well on the training data? Yes
 
•Does the tree perform well on unseen data? No
 
This is classic overﬁtting behavior , as we mentioned earlier when the
machine learning model performs too well on the training data but does
not generalize well. Why does this happen? Recall that the objective and
utility of training a model is usually to make predictions on unseen data.
 
Using the training data, we wish for the model to learn the patterns and
relationships hidden in the data that help us make predictions. Noise in the
(training) data is a reality of the world and we have artefacts in the data,
some deviations from the true patterns. Consider a very ﬂexible and
complex model that is so ﬂexible that it learns even the noise. That is a
model that learns noise as a pattern. Such a model is expected to perform
extremely well on the training data. Indeed, complex tree-based models
(like boosted trees in XGBoost, GBMs, and so on) and neural networks
can learn each point in the dataset, giving you even close to 100%
accuracy scores. Figure 4.16 illustrates how an overﬁtting model might
work:
 
 
Figure Underﬁtting, Overﬁtting, a good ﬁt ³
 
However , is an overﬁtting model expected to do well on unseen data? Do
we expect the same noise in the unseen data? Of course not. The models
are expected to perform poorly on new data. This is exactly what
happened with the decision tree model in the ad click prediction case
study: ~95% recall on the training data but around 79% recall on the test
data. The model could have been better because it understood the training
data too well (including its noise) and needed a better understanding of the
overall patterns.
 
With an understanding of overﬁtting, let us now turn towards the other
case of a poor ﬁt - underﬁtting:
Underﬁtting
 
Now consider the performance of the SVC model on the ad click
prediction case study . Revisiting Table
 
•Does the SVC perform well on the training data? No
 
•Does the SVC perform well on unseen data? No
 
A natural inference from this information could be that the SVC model
is not ﬂexible enough to capture the patterns in the data. The model is
likely behaving like the leftmost plot in Figure The underﬁt case is
where the boundary is not ﬂexible enough to separate the two classes.
Underﬁtting can be due to a few reasons:
 
•The modeling technique is not ﬂexible enough (think linear regression
vs. XGBoost).
 
•The set parameters of the technique do not allow for ﬂexibility (For
example, k in KNN methods, or shallow vs. deep decision tree, more
on this in Chapter 6, Hyper -parameter Tuning ).
 
•Input features are not informative enough.
 
•Too much noise in the data that is not enough patterns or signals
(related to point 2; about ﬂexibility).
 
•The model has not been trained long enough (this is particularly true
for techniques like neural networks that are often trained incrementally
in batches).
 
Handling underﬁtting therefore can be done by addressing each of the
points appropriately . This is often done by using a complex model,
adding more informative features, or sometimes simply training the
model for a longer duration. Before we start ﬁxing these issues in our
models, we need to be able to detect overﬁtting or underﬁtting.
Training/learning curves are an excellent tool for this purpose. Let us
understand them better and learn how to use them in our ad click
prediction case study .
Validation curves and goodness of ﬁt
 
We learnt that model complexity/ﬂexibility is a key factor for performance
and has a big bearing on the model’ s ﬁt. A high complexity model is often
the main reason for overﬁtting and low complexity is often the reason for
underﬁtting.
 
Complexity in the model can come from:
 
•Highly ﬂexible modelling technique (think linear regression vs. neural
network)
 
•Model parameter choices (shallow vs. deep decision trees)
 
•High number of features
 
•Overtrained models (relevant for techniques that learn in batches, for
example, neural networks or boosted trees)
 
A simple yet extremely powerful way to identify the
overﬁtting/underﬁtting of the model is through validation curves
(variations are also called learning curves). This common variation is
shown in Figure The X-axis represents model complexity , and the Y-axis
represents model accuracy (any metric like recall, accuracy , and so on).
Two curves are plotted on these axes: the training and validation errors.
This simple view tells us a lot about the learning situation.
 
 
Figure Model complexity vs. performance
  
Note: Model complexity on the X-axis can be represented in many ways.
It could be the number of training batches (For example, neural networks),
or parameters that deﬁne ﬂexibility (depth of the tree for tree-based
methods). It could also be the number of features.
  
As the complexity of the model increases, the model gets more ﬂexible
and after a point begins to learn the noise components. This situation can
be detected by looking at the validation accuracy , representing the
generalized performance on unseen data. As long as the model learns
genuine patterns, its training and validation accuracy improve. This is
because these genuine patterns also work well on the validation data.
When the model begins to learn the data too well, including the noise, we
see that the training and validation curves start diver ging. Further training
only increases the gap between these curves. Therefore, a sweet spot is a
level of complexity where the performance is good on both training and
validation data. Finding this optimizes the ﬁt of the model.
 
A similar understanding can be derived from another variation of the
validation curves. Instead of plotting accuracy , we could plot the loss of
the model on the training and the validation data. Note that the key
difference is that the error or the deﬁnition of loss can also be linked to the
underlying optimization that the model does (often utilizing loss
functions). In principle and practice, this curve gives us similar results to
the previous version (accuracy on the Y axis). In an overﬁtting situation,
the model keeps reducing errors in the training data. Still, the validation
error is stable or keeps increasing as the noise inﬂuences the model and
fails to generalize. Refer to Figure
 
 
Figure Model complexity vs. error rates
 
To make this understanding more concrete and test these ideas for
ourselves, let us create such validation curves for the ad click prediction
models we prepared for Azra Inc. and evaluate when we started to overﬁt
the data.

Note: Max depth is an important setting that controls the tree’s ﬂexibility

generally need to tune multiple such hyper -parameters to optimize the
model. We will explore this topic in detail in Chapter 6, Hyperparameter
Tuning.
  
Now that we understand how to identify overﬁtting, the next natural
question is – how do we handle overﬁtting? Let us now look at a few
ways to do so.
Handling overﬁtting
 
In a previous section, we saw some of the reasons for overﬁtting.
While the reasons could be aplenty , the result is always an overly
complex and ﬂexible model. Handling overﬁtting, logically , is about
simplifying the model. There are multiple ways to achieve this.
Feature selection
 
One of the simplest ways models become complex is by adding way
more features than needed. This could happen even for classically less
ﬂexible models like linear regression. The impact is much more
pronounced for more ﬂexible models like boosted trees or neural
networks. By nature, a linear regression with 3 features is much
simpler than one with 200 features.
 
Data scientists often collect more features than they ﬁnally use in the
model. Having more features and eliminating them is better than
having very few and creating them individually in a tedious and
stretched project. But how many features and which of these should be
included in the model? First, dumping all the possible features is
seldom a good idea. This is particularly true of models that need to be
maintained. Maintaining a pipeline for 200 features is much more
complex than a pipeline for 10. How do we go about choosing then?
 
The most practical and intuitive approaches are around
recursive/iterative/stepwise feature selection. In principle, three
approaches are used:
 
•Stepwise feature addition (forward) : Start with one feature and add
only those features that provide a meaningful performance
improvement. This is continued until there is no beneﬁt of adding any
more features.
 
•Stepwise feature elimination (backward) : Start with all the features in
the model and keep eliminating features one by one, each time
dropping the feature that added the least information to the model. This
continues until dropping any further feature leads to a signiﬁcant drop
in performance.
 
•Recursive feature elimination (mixed) : A combined approach that
adds and drops features. At each step, the approach evaluates if a
feature can be dropped without losing signiﬁcant performance. Then.
the model checks whether adding any leftover features provides any
performance boost. This continues until we arrive at an optimal feature
set.
 
Note that all these approaches can be combined, and indeed are better ,
with cross-validation to determine if we are impacting performance.
  
Note: A useful implementation of Recursive Feature Elimination is
available in sklearn as RFE under the feature selection module
(sklearn.feature_selection). More details and some examples can be
found in the ofﬁcial
Hyper -parameter control
 
We have already seen that certain model settings control its
complexity/ﬂexibility . We saw how the setting max_depth is a hyper -
parameter for a decision tree and that changing this can signiﬁcantly
improve how well the model generalizes. It was a good example of the
extent of impact hyper -parameters can have. Let us ﬁrst see the impact
of changing max depth of the decision tree and see if we can achieve
more generalizable results.
 
Let us begin by instantiating a DecisionT reeClassiﬁer from sklearn
with a max_depth value of 4. Use the following code:

Comparing the 90% validation accuracy at depth 4 with 86% accuracy
at depth 10, it is clear that controlling hyper -parameters can
signiﬁcantly help with overﬁtting and improve the generalized
performance of the model.
 
Most modeling techniques have their own set of hyper -parameters. The
humble decision tree itself has several hyper -parameters deﬁning the
maximum depth of the tree, features to evaluate at each split, minimum
samples allowed in a leaf after the split, minimum leaves before a split,
the information criterion and many more. When the decision tree is
employed in an XGBoost, the hyper -parameters increase. There are a
reasonable number of hyper -parameters for deep learning models,
which we will see in the later chapters. Therefore, evaluating these
settings ensures we have an optimal model.
  
Note: Hyper -parameter tuning is a core topic in model optimization
and we will study this in detail in this book. Chapter 6, Hyper -
parameter Tuning focuses on choosing hyper -parameters for classical
machine learning models. Chapter 8, Optimizing Deep Learning
Models teaches getting the most out of the deep learning models.
Regularization
 
Regularization is a general machine learning concept that applies well
to classical and deep learning techniques. The term regularization has
more popularity due to its usage in deep learning models. The term
shrinkage has been around in classical machine learning techniques for
decades. The model is regularized by shrinking the
parameters/coefﬁcients of the predictors in the model.
 
For example, a linear regression model could be complex with many
predictors. Regularization would mean reducing the coefﬁcients in
magnitude for the predictors. Note that this does not necessarily drop
predictors, but rather subdue the impact of the predictors. The effect is
reduced ﬂexibility in the model which effectively helps reduce
overﬁtting. Figure 4.20 illustrates this impact. Regularizing the
coefﬁcients makes the highly ﬂuctuating and ﬂexible curve much less
ﬂexible. The result is a less overﬁtting model.
 
 
Figure Regularization of coefﬁcients; source: Wikipedia
 
Regularization of linear regression models gives us techniques like
ridge regression and lasso both of which regularize in slightly different
ways. While ridge regression suppresses the values of all coefﬁcients,
lasso can reduce some coefﬁcients to 0. Lasso, therefore, also helps
with feature selection. If you want a compromise between the
approaches, you could also use the ElasticNet approach which is
simply a weighted combination between ridge and lasso.
  
Note: Ridge and Lasso regression are less popular but are well
supported in sklearn. The methods work well with other sklearn
utilities making them easy to use. More details on the ideas of
regularization are available at Regularization (mathematics) -
  
In the context of deep learning models, there are several regularization
methods. The most straightforward way is to add a penalty for higher
weights. A model with a lot of high weights will be less preferred,
resulting in the weights being subdued exactly the way linear
regression coefﬁcients are subdued. Additionally , some penalties allow
regularization to work exactly like ridge and lasso work for regression.
In Chapter 8, Optimizing Deep Learning we will study regularization
in greater detail.
Data augmentation
 
Data augmentation techniques are primarily used in deep learning for
images and textual data. However , they can also be employed with
classical Machine Learning approaches for tabular data. Data
augmentation can be considered another form of regularization for
highly complex models. Highly complex models’  overﬁt small datasets
very easily and increasing the data size should help mitigate the
problem.
 
However , getting more data is impractical sometimes. A smart
workaround is to generate data variations to provide more examples
for the model to learn from. Consider an image classiﬁcation model
developed to detect dogs in pictures. To create more training examples,
you must make minor alterations to the image. For instance, you could
rotate the image by a few degrees, zoom into the image a bit, and ﬂip it
horizontally and vertically . In all cases, you are not changing the
image’ s subject and the fact that it contains a dog. Effectively , you can
generate several samples from one for the same label. In this way, data
augmentation helps you get more from your data by:
 
•Creating more variety in the data for the model to learn from; in many
cases, it provides that much-needed variation
 
•Regularizing and making the model generalize better
 
Data augmentation is a useful tool in your arsenal as a data scientist.
We will study this in detail in later chapters.
Optimal training (early stopping)
 
For modeling techniques that either learn in batches or from data
sequentially , stopping at the right time can prevent overﬁtting. For
neural networks, this would mean the right number of epochs. For
other techniques, this could mean setting the right number of learning
iterations. Taking the example of neural networks, an epoch is said to
be passed when the entire data has gone through the network once for
training. Neural networks are typically trained over several epochs in
dozens, hundreds, or even thousands. This increases the training time
and signiﬁcantly maximizes the tendency to overﬁt.
 
A practical and effective strategy to prevent overﬁtting and choosing
the optimal number of epochs is to use early stopping. The idea is
simple while training the neural network, keep monitoring the
validation loss/accuracy . If the validation loss does not decrease (or the
accuracy does not increase) for a few epochs, the model will stop
training further . The rationale should be obvious now. If the training
score is increasing, but the validation score is not, we are overﬁtting.
This approach is very effective and is employed commonly in practice
with great success.
  
Note: A detailed exploration of techniques for overﬁtting neural
networks and how to use early stopping is covered in Chapter 8,
Optimizing Deep Learning Models.
Conclusion
 
In this chapter , we saw a principled way of comparing models. We
began with the idea that accuracy is among many considerations in
choosing models. We saw metrics like precision, recall, ROC curves,
PR curves, F1 scores, and more in action. We learned that they can
help ensure the model performance is aligned with the business
objectives in different situations.
 
We then saw that because models need to be employed on unseen data,
we need that they generalize well. We saw the pitfalls of assessing the
training data saw different evaluation schemes to estimate generalized
performance and implemented the K-fold cross-validation scheme for
different models.
 
In the process, we realized that overﬁtting is a common problem that
plagues most machine learning projects. We learnt about overﬁtting
and underﬁtting and ways to diagnose them using learning/validation
curves where we saw the models’  performance vs. complexity . We
discussed multiple ways and implemented some ways in the Ad click
prediction case study . This completes the very important chapter on
model evaluation.
 
In the next chapter , we will discuss and overcome another extremely
common challenge in several real-world machine learning problems –
imbalanced classes.
 
1 source: roc curve
 
2 Source: Precision-Recall curve and AUC-PR - Hasty .ai
 
3 Source: What is Overﬁtting? | IBM

CHAPTER 5
Imbalanced Machine Learning
Introduction
 
In the chapters so far, when dealing with binary classiﬁcation, the
classes we saw had a somewhat equal distribution (close to 1:1) which
was convenient. However , there are several real-world situations in
which you will ﬁnd many more records belonging to one class than the
other . Fraud detection, that is, identifying fraudulent transactions is a
classic example of such a situation where you typically have less than
0.1% (or fewer) that are fraudulent. This is an example of highly
imbalanced classes with a ratio between the non-fraud and fraud
classes around 999:1 (for 0.1% of fraud cases). It is important to note
that such situations are not rare. Some common tasks where the
imbalance is usual are:
 
•Fraud detection (<0.1% to <1%, banking, e-commerce)
 
•Spam detection (<1%)
 
•Disease (for example, cancer) prediction (<1% usually)
 
•Device failure prediction (varies, but <1% usually)
 
•Customer conversion prediction (<5% on top e-commerce sites)
 
•Email campaign click prediction (<1% open the email)
 
•Anomaly detection (usually <1%)
 
•Outlier detection (usually <1%)
 
If you have noticed, most of these are about predicting or detecting an
event of interest (for example, the presence of a disease). The event
occurs rather rarely and thus has a low event rate (records where the
event occurred/total records). In all the tasks mentioned above,
detecting one outcome is more important. How does this play out in
the whole data science process? Well, there are two main key
challenges from such low event rates (imbalanced classes):
 
•Model evaluation is tricky
 
•Getting the model to handle imbalance is tricky
 
In this chapter , we will understand and see these two problems playing
out for ourselves. Beginning with model evaluation, we will see how
standard metrics like accuracy are not sufﬁcient and a more involved
evaluation is needed to make the model useful. We will explore key
alternative metrics and examine the difference between them on the
same task. After we understand a better way of evaluation, we will
tackle the next challenge which is handling the imbalance. We will
learn and implement techniques of varying complexities, right from
the simplest sampling approaches to more sophisticated approaches
that use complex algorithms for careful data selection. We will
implement all these hands-on using Credit Card Fraud Detection as our
problem statement.
 
Let us begin by understanding the most important aspect ﬁrst: the
difﬁculty in model evaluation. But before that, let us understand the
business problem that we are solving and familiarize ourselves with
the data at hand.
Structure
 
In this chapter , we will discuss the following topics:
 
•Understanding the business problem
 
•Difﬁculty in model evaluation
 
•Evaluation metrics for imbalanced classes
 
∘F1 score
 
∘Precision-Recall curve
 
∘Summary of model evaluation
 
•Handling imbalance
 
∘Adjusting the cost function
 
∘Data Balancing
 
•Undersampling
 
∘Model evaluation with rebalancing
 
∘Using Pipelines for evaluating re-balanced data
 
∘Tomek Links
 
∘Undersampling methods: edited nearest neighbour
 
•Oversampling
 
∘SMOTE
 
∘ADASYN
 
•Combination of undersampling and oversampling
 
•Comparing the approaches
Objectives
 
After reading this chapter , you will be able to tackle one of the most
common and challenging problems in machine learning/artiﬁcial
intelligence. You will understand how imbalance impacts our
evaluation of the model and be able to diagnose issues due to
imbalance. You will identify better metrics for evaluation. You will
also be able to use multiple approaches to tackle imbalance. You will
also be adept at using under/over sampling techniques to balance the
dataset better for a more optimized machine learning solution.
Understanding the business problem
 
Aiza, Inc. is a leading insurance company offering products in the life,
health, property , and liability insurance categories. The company wants
to expand to vehicle insurance for 4-wheelers. Its existing
policyholders are a natural target segment, given that they already own
insurance policies, it is reasonable to assume they are more likely to
buy the new offering. Additionally , the company has enough details
about the customers to target them effectively .
 
Aiza, Inc. recently ran a large marketing campaign, reaching out to
about 37 thousand of its existing policyholders. The data from the
campaign coupled with some demographic information about the
customers and some knowledge of their vehicles can potentially
provide good insights into which customers are more likely to convert.
Training a Machine Learning model that predicts whether a customer
will convert or not can be extremely useful in optimizing future
campaigns by limiting the target to those having a higher chance of
converting.
 
Campaign data responses are typically imbalanced, and our data is no
exception. In this chapter , we will use this as our case study and help
build a high-performance model for identifying better leads and
learning how to handle imbalanced classes along the way. First, let us
load, understand, and prepare the data.
Exercise 5.1: Understanding and staging the data
 
In this exercise, we will use various simple analyses to understand the
data and the business problem better . Let us begin.

With this, we performed the basic pre-processing of the data. We
acknowledge that the classes are signiﬁcantly imbalanced. Let us now
understand how the imbalance is
Difﬁculty in model evaluation
 
In Chapter Model Evaluation and we discussed that one of the most
unexpected ways in which a model breaks or a modeling process
breaks is when the evaluation is not done as per the objective and the
characteristics of the data. This is particularly problematic with
imbalanced datasets or situations where one class is much more
prevalent than the other . Working with imbalanced datasets requires a
lot of careful consideration and a good understanding of the business
objectives and the application of the model to arrive at a good
evaluation approach. Regular metrics like classiﬁcation accuracy are
rarely enough. As a quick recap, consider a case where you are
creating a model for fraud detection. Let us say only about 0.1% of the
transactions in the data are fraudulent transactions. Now , let us say you
have a model which predicts everything as not fraud. What would be
the accuracy of this model? The model would still have a 99.9%
accuracy .
  
Pause and ponder: Let us say you are evaluating a model to detect a
serious disease like HIV. Which of the following errors is more
serious? A false positive (a person not having HIV, getting diagnosed
with HIV), or a false negative (a person having HIV and not getting
detected).
   
Hint: Think of the consequences of each error . An unaware HIV-
infected patient or a healthy person who gets scared by the result and
can go for a re-test?
  
While this performance sounds great on paper , the model is completely
useless for the purpose. This is because, in our situation, we are not
very concerned about getting the non-fraud cases right. Rather , we are
interested in detecting cases of fraud. We are more interested in the
minority class. The metrics, therefore, need to be chosen accordingly .
Accuracy as a metric is unreliable because it does not distinguish
between the minority and majority classes.
 
By now you must have realized that the solution is simple, at least
conceptually . We need asymmetric metrics to allow us to weigh one
class more than the other . Let us ﬁrst see for ourselves if accuracy is
suitable for our insurance case study in the next exercise.
Exercise 5.2: Accuracy for imbalanced classes
 
We will now build and evaluate a Machine Learning model using
accuracy as our metric. First, we will perform the train-test split on the
dataset, followed by scaling the numeric features. After preprocessing is
complete, we will train a Logistic Regression model on the data.
Alongside seeing the accuracy of the model, we will perform an involved
evaluation by analyzing the performance of the individual classes.Please
bear in mind that our classes are imbalanced and we, therefore, need to
perform a stratiﬁed split to ensure that the proportions of the classes are
maintained in the derived datasets. Perform the following steps.
