This view conﬁrms that the precision is around 49% and recall is around
11% for the conversions.
 
The model isn’t performing well even on the train set. This model’ s
performance is not sufﬁcient. It should be clear that accuracy is not a good
metric when evaluating performance in imbalanced classes. We always
need to perform a more detailed analysis and choose our success metric
according to our situation. Let us discuss some potential metrics for such
imbalanced situations.
Evaluation metrics for imbalanced classes
 
We did a detailed discussion of accuracy , precision, and recall in
Chapter 4, Model Evaluation and The previous hands-on activity made
it clear that we need better metrics than accuracy . Precision and Recall
are much better alternatives for such cases. There is a tradeof f between
precision and recall and may need to prioritize one over the other for
our business problem. While these are good alternatives, they are not
our only options. Let us evaluate some more key metrics.
  
Pause and ponder: Going back to the example of evaluating a model to
detect HIV. Which metric is more important? Precision or recall?
   
Hint: You would want to detect all possible true HIV cases, even if at
the expense of some false positives.
F1 score
 
We know that there is a tradeof f between precision and recall, higher
precision leads to lower recall and vice versa. While in some situations
we have a clear prioritization between them, choosing is not always
possible. In such situations, we need a metric that captures the model’ s
performance on both precision and recall. An effective metric is the F1
score. The F1 score is the harmonic mean between precision and
recall, as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure F1 score is the harmonic mean of precision and recall
 
As depicted in Figure the harmonic mean between two variables is the
highest when both are high. Even if one of the variables has a low
value, the harmonic mean drops signiﬁcantly , even if the other variable
is close to 1. Consequently , a high F1 score is achieved when both
precision and recall are high, indicating a well-balanced trade-of f
between these two metrics.
 
 
Figure Harmonic mean visualized
 
It is important to note the implicit assumptions and therefore the
situations where the F1 score is suitable to use are as follows:
 
•The F1 score assumes that a false positive is equally bad as a false
negative.
 
•True negatives are uninteresting because we look at precision and
recall, both of which are focused on the positive class.
 
In practice, the F1 score is a very popular metric for imbalanced
classes. The F1 score metric is conveniently available in sklearn
among many other metrics. As a consequence, the metric can be easily
used in other sklearn modules for cross-validation and other extremely
useful utilities that we will encounter later will help us optimize the
model performance to obtain better F1 scores.
 
Warning: Do not use Area under ROC curves!
 
One of the most common mistakes while working with imbalanced
data sets is using the ROC curve. This mistake is so prevalent that
even many experienced data science professionals commit this. We
know people who have more than a decade of experience in building
statistical models make this mistake. Therefore, this can be considered
one of those data science sins that one should be extremely careful
around.
 
Let us plot the ROC curve for our case study to see for ourselves how
misleading it could be. We’ll use the plot ROC curve utility from
sklearn using the following code:
 
from sklearn.metrics import plot_roc_curve
 
plot_roc_curve(logreg, X_train, y_train)
 
plt.show()
 
The ROC curve is plotted as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure ROC curve for the classiﬁer
 
The area under the ROC curve is which is impressive. But we already
have seen that the model is not useful for our purpose. The area under
the ROC curve is therefore misleading. But why is that? Let us
understand.
 
The ROC curve, as seen in Chapter Evaluation and attempts to
measure how well the model can distinguish between the two classes.
A model with high skill will be able to distinguish between the classes
very well, whereas a model with low skill can easily be confused. A
common mistake is to use ROC curves for highly imbalanced
situations. The idea seems to have merit prima facie, if a model
differentiates between two classes well, the imbalance should not be a
problem. But to understand why this is problematic, we need to
understand the metrics that the ROC curve works on True Positive
Rate and False Positive Rate. A true Positive Rate is the same as
Recall, so the Recall part is somehow captured. But the other metric is
concerned more about the proportion of total negatives that were
identiﬁed as positive (False Positive/T otal Negatives). When you have
a severe imbalance, the False Positive Rate will inevitably be very low
as the denominator (Total Negatives) is typically high. A better
alternative would be to use the area under the Precision-Recall curve
an extremely useful metric that we will learn next.
Precision-Recall Curve
 
The PR curve is generated like the ROC curve but using precision and
recall metrics. This is the trade-of f that is often most relevant to us.
Given that the recall metric is precisely about detecting the true
positive cases and the precision metric is about not creating a lot of
false positives, the area under this curve would be far more useful for
evaluating imbalanced situations.
 
Plotting the PR curve is also simple using the
‘plot_precision_recall_curve’  utility from sklearn. Let us plot the curve
for our model for vehicle insurance campaigns using the following
code:
 
plot_precision_recall_curve(logreg, X_train, y_train)
 
plt.show()
 
The curve is plotted as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure PR curve of the classiﬁer
 
As seen in Figure the area under the PR curve is just about 0.46. This
is far lower than the area under the ROC curve (0.87). The PR curve
gives us a much more reliable picture of the model performance. The
reason is that both metrics precision and recall that are used to
generate this curve are of importance for situations like ours.
Summary of model evaluation
 
There are many metrics available for evaluating models, even for
imbalanced situations. The key point is to understand which one is
most relevant to the problem statement. In some cases, a single metric
like the F1 score may sufﬁce. In other cases, a combination of metrics
may be used with one as the primary metric and the other as a
guardrail. This is especially true when the model can have a serious
business impact or for critical applications. If you are in doubt, make
sure to take inputs from the business teams and align the criteria for a
good model.
 
With the ﬁrst challenge of model evaluation addressed, let us turn our
attention to the second biggest challenge in machine learning for
imbalanced classes, making the model handle the imbalance and
thereby improving the model performance. Let us discuss the main
approaches to doing so.
Handling imbalance
 
With a better understanding of a proper way of evaluating the model,
we are now ready to evaluate a single model as well as compare
multiple models. This also now allows us to improvise on a model, or
at least know how one model is better than the other . But the key
question remains how do we improve the model? How do we make the
model pay more attention to a certain class while training? How do we
make the model learn the patterns that we want it to learn? Now , there
are several approaches to this. We will look at the 3 main approaches.
Adjusting the cost function
 
The ﬁrst approach is inﬂuencing the way the machine learning model
‘learns’  that is inﬂuencing the model training itself. Recall from Chapter
1, Optimizing a Machine Learning/ Artiﬁcial Intelligence Solution that
“model training” is, simply put, making the model learn the patterns in the
data while providing an objective or a cost function that the model needs
to minimize. While training, if we can tell the model to pay more attention
to a certain class and that getting the minority class right is more
important than getting the majority class right, then we would be close to
a better solution.
 
For example, if you could tell the model that classifying a fraud case
correctly is 10 times more important than getting a not-fraud case right,
the model would detect more fraud cases. Model training is an
optimization problem after all. All optimization problems have a cost or
objective function. If you can modify the objective function to give more
weight to a certain class, then the model learning can be inﬂuenced very
easily . This is indeed a simple, straightforward, yet powerful ﬁrst
approach to handling imbalance.
 
The logistic regression loss function is shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Regular log loss for classiﬁcation
 
Class weights to weigh one class more than the other is shown in Figure
5.16:
 
 
Figure Log loss with class weights
 
The simplest way is to modify the class weight setting that several
classiﬁcation models in sklearn have. Let us relook at the arguments to the
LogisticRegression module in sklearn using the line of the following
code:
 
?LogisticRegression
 
You will see the arguments for the model in Figure
 
 
Figure The class_weight parameter in LogisticRegression
 
Notice the class weight parameter . By default, it is set to ‘None’, which
means that the cost function weighs both classes equally . To learn more
about the parameter and how to use it, scroll down further in the help pop-
up to look at the ‘Parameters’  section of the docstring of
LogisticRegression. You will see the description of this parameter as
shown in Figure 5.18:
 

 
Figure Description of the class_weight parameter
 
We can specify the weight for each class using a dictionary . This weight
will be used in the adjusted cost function for LogisticRegression. If you
wish to weight the positive class, 10 times more than the negative, you
can specify class weight as {0:1, 1:10} as an example (the weights don’t
need to add up to 1, it is the ratio that matters). If you want to weigh the
classes in inverse proportion to their proportions in the data, use
‘balanced’. If the ratio between the minority and majority classes is 1:5,
using a ‘balanced’  scheme would assign class weights with a 5:1 ratio.
This simple approach is usually effective and a good starting point.
  
Note: While we saw the class_weight parameter in the context of
LogisticRegression, most classiﬁcation methods in sklearn have the
class_weight parameter implemented. For all those classiﬁcation methods,
adjusting the cost function is as simple as modifying the class_weight
parameter .
Exercise 5.3: Adjusting class_weight
 
In this exercise we will assess the impact of adjusting class weights for the
logistic regression model. We will use a stratiﬁed k-fold validation scheme
to get a generalized performance score. Perform the following steps.
 
1. Create an instance of LogisticRegression with class_weight speciﬁed as
`balanced`L:
 
logreg_wt = LogisticRegression(class_weight='balanced',
random_state=42)
 
2. To assess model performance, use the 3-fold scoring scheme and print
out the cross-validation scores as a dataframe. Use the code in the
following commands:
 
from sklearn.model_selection import StratiﬁedKFold
 
from sklearn.model_selection import cross_validate
 
folds = StratiﬁedKFold(random_state=42, n_splits=3, shufﬂe=T rue)
 
cv_scores = cross_validate(logreg_wt,
 
X_train,
 
y_train,
 
cv=folds,
 
scoring=['precision','recall','f1'])
 
pd.DataFrame(cv_scores)
 
The CV scores are printed as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Cross-validation scores
Data balancing
 
Here is an insight that is extremely simple and fundamental: models learn
patterns from the data presented to them. If you want the model to pick up
the patterns relevant for detecting the minority class better , it simply needs
more representation. We often come across situations where the imbalance
in the data is so severe that merely modifying the cost function does not
sufﬁce. In such cases, we need to explore the option of data balancing.
Data balancing means that we reduce the imbalance of the classes in the
data, which means that we see more records, as a percentage, from the
minority class. This will help the model see a little more of the minority
class records and the patterns the model learns are less dominated by the
majority class. We need not always completely balance the classes 50:50,
even 5:1 is a signiﬁcant improvement over 999:1.
 
How do we do this? Well, we have only two options, undersampling and
oversampling, sometimes you may use a combination of the two.
Undersampling refers to taking a subset of the records for the majority
class. As an example, in a dataset with 1 million records, we may have
999,000 records in the majority class and only 1,000 records in the
minority class, a ratio of 999:1. But while preparing the training data,
instead of choosing all 999,000 records, we could choose just 5000. The
5000 records can be chosen using Random under -sampling that is
choosing the records at random, or some other sophisticated strategy (for
example, Near Miss). This would create the ratio or modify the ratio to
5:1. As discussed earlier , 5:1 is still somewhat imbalanced, but is a huge
improvement over 999:1. The two approaches are illustrated in Figure
 
 
Figure Undersampling vs. oversampling
 
The idea of oversampling is on similar lines, while preparing the data set,
we include more records from the minority class. The simplest way of
achieving this is to merely duplicate or create copies of the records from
the minority class, either replicating all records or choosing a random
portion (Random Oversampling) of the records to duplicate. The most
sophisticated approaches don’t just replicate points but generate new data
points that are similar to existing data points. Techniques like SMOTE,
and ADASYN fall into this category . We will understand and implement
these in later sections of this chapter .
 
The result of both undersampling and oversampling is the same; a more
favorable ratio for the minority class. But the practical difference between
the two approaches can be signiﬁcant, while doing an under -sampling of
the majority class you may end up losing some important information or
patterns in the data. The loss of information can lead to extremely poor
performance, especially when there is a severe imbalance in the labels.
Therefore, exercise caution, while performing under -sampling. As a
common-sense guiding principle, always prefer retaining information to
losing information.
 
 
Pause and ponder: Is simple oversampling of the minority class
fundamentally signiﬁcantly different from assigning a higher -class weight
to the minority class? In both cases, we do not provide any new
information and are effectively providing a higher weight to the minority
class.
Undersampling
 
We saw that the idea behind under -sampling is extremely simple,
balance the classes in favour of the minority class by reducing the
records from the majority class. The simplest and most naive method is
simply dropping some or many records from the majority class while
preparing the training dataset. By performing Random under -sampling,
we can choose the proportion of records to drop or retain from the
majority class.
Exercise 5.4: Random undersampling of insurance data
 
To understand how it works, let us see Random Undersampling in action.
We will use the imblearn library for Random Undersampling and all the
subsequently discussed approaches. If you don’t have the library installed,
install it using the following command:
 
!pip install imblearn
 
We will use the handy ﬁt_resample method from imblearn to balance the
data. As you might expect, the ﬁt_resample method identiﬁes the number
of records in the minority class. Next, it randomly selects a matching
number of records from the majority class, resulting in a perfectly
balanced set. Let us perform the undersampling and subsequent analysis.
 
1. Import RandomUnderSampler from the under_sampling module in
imblearn and create an instance of the undersampler . Use the following
code:
 
from imblearn.under_sampling import RandomUnderSampler
 
u_sampler = RandomUnderSampler(random_state=42)
 
2. Resample the dataset and verify the new class distributions using the
following code. Note that we are using new variables to store the balanced
datasets.
 
X_train_under , y_train_under = u_sampler .ﬁt_resample(X_train, y_train)
 
y_train_under .value_counts()
 
The new class frequencies get printed as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Distribution after under -sampling
  
Note: Class balancing must always be done AFTER performing the train-
test split. Any manipulations or rebalancing must be done on the train
dataset alone. Performing balancing before train-test split will make the
test data non-representative of the true data and can render the ﬁnal model
useless.
  
3. Create an instance of LogisticRegression (specify only the random
state) and assess its performance on the balanced dataset using the cross-
validation scheme we have been using so far. Use the following code:
 
logreg = LogisticRegression(random_state=42)
 
cv_scores = cross_validate(logreg,
 
X_train_under ,
 
y_train_under ,
 
cv=folds,
 
scoring=['precision','recall','f1'])
 
pd.DataFrame(cv_scores)
 
The model performance gets printed as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Performance after Random Undersampling
 
This seems like a huge improvement over the previous models. It seems
like we have struck gold with just a simple change, a recall of about 94%
and F1 scores > 82%. Is this the generalized performance we can expect
on new and unseen data? Have we created a great model with just this
change? No, we have not. This a very insidious ‘gotcha’  that you should
be extremely careful about while working with imbalanced datasets. Let
us understand this pitfall better .
Model evaluation with re-balancing
 
After re-balancing the training data, we are ready to build a model and
evaluate its performance. We usually look at the cross-validation score
for this purpose. K-fold cross-validation creates folds from the training
data and measures the performance on the held-out fold. The idea is
that the average score over these validation folds gives us an estimate
of the generalized performance of the model, which we hope is similar
to the test set. However , after you have re-balanced the training data,
the cross-validation score does not serve its purpose.
 
This is because when you create the cross-validation folds after
rebalancing, even the ‘test’  folds have the same, balanced class
distribution. Therefore, the performance seen in the cross-validation
results is based on balanced classes. In the actual data and the unseen
data that the model will encounter in the future, the class distribution is
heavily skewed. The balanced test set is therefore not representative of
true and the performance of the model is not indicative of generalized
performance.
 
The following steps indicate the process we followed:
 
1. Rebalance the training data
 
2. Split into k folds
 
3. Model evaluation on balanced test folds
 
Instead, this is what we need to do:
 
1. Split into k fold
 
2. Rebalance the training folds
 
3. Evaluation of untouched test folds
 
The difference between the two approaches is highlighted in Figures
5.23 and
 
 
Figure Rebalancing before creating validation splits
 

 
Figure Splitting before rebalancing
Using Pipelines for evaluating re-balanced data
 
We now understand that the right order in model evaluation with class re-
balancing is as follows:
 
split into k folds| rebalance the training folds| evaluation on untouched test
folds
 
This can be coded manually using a loop. Manual coding would give you
full control over each step in the process. Note that the steps listed above
are a general outline - you might have task-speciﬁc steps in the process. In
general, the loop would split the data into the folds, then perform all the
necessary steps to rebalance (more than one sometimes) and ﬁt the model
on the training folds. Finally , the evaluation on the validation fold is
performed and the score is stored. As mentioned earlier , this can be coded
manually , but is less convenient compared to the handy Pipeline utility in
the imblearn library . The Pipeline module allows us to chain multiple
steps into a single pipeline that includes the re-balancing steps and can
also include the model. The pipeline module conveniently handles the
looping and fold division part. It allows you to specify the rebalancing
step(s) conveniently . It also allows you to add the predictive model as a
pipeline component. The fold creation, rebalancing, model ﬁtting, and
scoring are all handled very easily by the pipeline module. Let us see how
it works in the following steps:
 
1. First, we import the Pipeline utility from imblearn using the following
code:
 
from imblearn.pipeline import Pipeline
 
2. Next, we instantiate the rebalancer that is the random undersampler and
the LogisticRegression instance. The following code does this:
 
u_sampler = RandomUnderSampler(random_state=42)
 
logreg = LogisticRegression(random_state=42)
 
3. Next, both these steps are combined in a pipeline. The steps in the
pipeline can be speciﬁed as named steps, as shown in the following code:
 
p_steps = [('under', u_sampler),
 
('model', logreg)]
 
pipeline = Pipeline(steps = p_steps)
 
4. The pipeline is now ready and works just like a sklearn model would.
You can assess the cross-validation performance of this pipeline by using
it just like you would use the cross_validate method on the sklearn model:
 
cv_scores = cross_validate(pipeline,
 
X_train,
 
y_train,
 
cv=folds,
 
scoring=['precision','recall','f1'])
 
pd.DataFrame(cv_scores)
 
The output is shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Realistic results on using Pipeline
 
This performance is much more believable and closer to the performance
we have seen so far. The generalized recall is high, but the precision is
poor, leading to F1 scores similar to what we saw when using the
class_weight parameter .
 
The performance is much more reliable and realistic because the pipeline
does exactly what needs to be done. The under sampler is applied only to
the training folds created after the K-fold splits. The test fold is not re-
balanced. While predicting, the under -sampler step doesn’ t do anything (it
just passes the values through). Thanks to the Pipeline module in
imblearn, we now have a much better model evaluation scheme that works
with re-balancing approaches. Let us now turn our attention towards more
re-balancing approaches.
Undersampling methods: Tomek Links
 
The approach of Random undersampling is extremely simple. When
dropping records from the majority class, it treats all points equally . This
isn’t such a good idea. Points belonging to the majority and minority
classes need not be well separated in space. Near the decision boundary ,
we may have points from both classes close together . This could be
confusing for the model, which may not be able to identify a clear
decision boundary because of the ‘unclean’  space. Therefore, points of the
majority class that are very close to the minority class points and vice
versa are ‘nuisance’  points that confuse the model.
 
A smarter undersampling approach would identify that dropping such
nuisance points is more beneﬁcial than dropping points in the majority
class that are far away from the minority class points. Points from the
opposite classes that are nearest neighbours of each other form a Tomek
Link. The Tomek Link is illustrated in Figure Points in Tomek Links can
be considered to be ‘nuisance’  points that confuse the model.
 
 
Figure Tomek Links visualized.
 
Under -sampling approaches using Tomek Links would drop these
nuisance points. Figure 5.27 shows the undersampling using Tomek Links
in action. The initial space has many cases where points of opposite
classes are very close to each other (forming a Tomek Link). The under -
sampler identiﬁes these links and drops points from the majority class
belonging to these links. The result is a much cleaner space with the
opposite classes better separated. Refer to Figure 5.27 for the detailed
working of the technique:
 
 
Figure Clean-up using Tomek Links
 
It is important to note that this approach is not truly a rebalancing
approach. The approach does not guarantee, and indeed in most cases
does not lead to well-balanced classes. This is because it removes only the
records from the Tomek links. Points that do not belong to Tomek Links
are not omitted. Therefore, Tomek Links based under sampler can be
thought of as a cleaning but not a strong under sampler . We will now see
the Tomek Links under sampler in action on the insurance case study .
Exercise 5.5: Tomek Links applied to insurance data
 
In this hands-on exercise, we will perform Tomek Links based on a
sampling of the insurance case study and evaluate model performance. Let
us get started:
 
1. First, import the `TomekLinks` utility from the `under_sampling`
module in imblearn using the following code:
 
from imblearn.under_sampling import TomekLinks
 
2. Next, instantiate the TomekLinks under sampler and using the
ﬁt_resample method, rebalance the training data as in the code:
 
u_sampler = TomekLinks()
 
X_train_under , y_train_under = u_sampler .ﬁt_resample(X_train, y_train)
 
3. To verify the extent of re-balancing on applying the over sampler ,
display the class frequencies before and after rebalancing. Use the
following code:
 
y_train.value_counts()
 
y_train_under .value_counts()
 
The output is shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Distribution before and after under -sampling
 
Notice from the output that very few points (1,309) have been dropped
from the majority class. We still have a lot of imbalances in the classes.
Let us see if the model performance is good. To assess the performance of
this under sampling scheme, we will use the imblearn pipeline approach
as we did for random under sampling.
 
4. Create a pipeline with TomekLinks under Sampler and Logistic
Regression as the steps:
 
u_sampler = TomekLinks(n_jobs=-1)
 
logreg = LogisticRegression(random_state=42)
 
p_steps = [('under', u_sampler),
 
('model', logreg)]
 
pipeline = Pipeline(steps = p_steps)
 
5. Evaluate the model using the cross-validation scheme we have been
using so far and display the results as a Pandas dataframe using the
following code:
 
cv_scores = cross_validate(pipeline,
 
X_train,
 
y_train,
 
cv=folds,
 
scoring=['precision','recall','f1'])
 
pd.DataFrame(cv_scores)
 
The model performance gets printed as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Cross-validation scores
 
The performance is very poor compared to the random over-sampler we
saw previously . Note that the performance is still an improvement over the
completely imbalanced data. This should not be surprising considering
that we understand that Tomek Links is not rebalancing to a signiﬁcant
extent. Regardless, undersampling using Tomek Links is an extremely
important conceptual approach and we will explore its full potential in a
subsequent section in this chapter . For now, we evaluate another powerful
undersampling approach, Edited Nearest Neighbour -based under -
sampling.
Undersampling methods: edited nearest neighbour
 
We saw the Tomek Links under the sampling approach is a cleaning
approach that drops points that form a Tomek Link when a point from
the majority class is the closest neighbour to one from the minority
class. We saw that this drops very few points and in our case study , it
did not affect the imbalance in a signiﬁcant manner . The Edited nearest
neighbour approach is a variation of this approach. For each point, the
approach forms a neighbourhood of the K nearest neighbours (default
value of 3 in sklearn implementation) and attempts to clean this
neighbourhood by dropping the disagreeing point. For example, if a
neighbourhood contains mostly the minority class, then the majority
point class from this neighbourhood will be dropped. Such an
approach can potentially drop more points and clean the space better ,
leading to potentially better balance in the data. Again, this too is a
cleaning approach, and we cannot be sure of the extent of rebalancing
of the data. Let us see how well it works for our insurance case study .
Exercise 5.6: Edited nearest neighbour undersampling
 
In this hands-on exercise, we will perform Edited Nearest Neighbours
based on sampling on the insurance dataset. We will use the
EditedNearestNeighbours utility from the under_sampling module in
imblearn, similar to the way we used TomekLinks and evaluated model
performance.Perform the following steps.
 
1. Import the EditedNearestNeighbours utility , instantiate, and apply it to
the train data. Display the class frequencies before and after the under
sampling:
 
from imblearn.under_sampling import EditedNearestNeighbours
 
u_sampler = EditedNearestNeighbours(n_jobs=-1)
 
X_train_under , y_train_under = u_sampler .ﬁt_resample(X_train, y_train)
 
y_train.value_counts()
 
y_train_under .value_counts()
 
The frequencies get output as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Class distribution before and after under -sampling
 
Note that this time we have a higher number of points dropped (4,865).
This is an improvement from TomekLinks and the balance is slightly
better , but we still have an imbalance and this is somewhat expected as
Edited Nearest Neighbour is a cleaning method. Let us see how this
approach fares on the data.
 
2. Create a pipeline with Edited Nearest Neighbour and Logistic
Regression and evaluate its performance using the following code:
 
p_steps = [('under', u_sampler),
 
('model', logreg)]
 
pipeline = Pipeline(steps = p_steps)
 
cv_scores = cross_validate(pipeline,
 
X_train,
 
y_train,
 
cv=folds,
 
scoring=['precision','recall','f1'])
 
pd.DataFrame(cv_scores)
 
The performance output is shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Cross-validation scores
 
The recall is now more than 80% and the F1 score is above 56%. This is
an improvement in terms of precision and the best result we achieved so
far on the F1 score. The Edited Nearest Neighbour approach cleaned up
the data better than Tomek Links and improved model performance.
 
We have seen a few variations of the under -sampling approach by now.
Random undersampling was the simplest approach that dropped a
signiﬁcant chunk of the majority class points and can balance the classes
perfectly . It treats all majority points equally and was thus a naïve
approach. Data clean-up approaches like Tomek Links and Edited Nearest
Neighbour clean up the space instead. The approaches attempt to clean up
spaces where points from the majority and minority classes are close to
each other and drop the points to make the region less mixed. These
approaches do not guarantee class balance. Indeed, on our data, the Tomek
Links approach dropped very few majority class points and the model
performance was poor. The Edited Nearest Neighbour method dropped
more points and gave us better model performance. Note that in all the
under -sampling approaches, we are losing data points and therefore
information contained in the data. With this summary , let us now look at
the other major approach to balancing the classes of oversampling.
Oversampling
 
Under -sampling attempted to balance the classes better by omitting points
from the majority class. Oversampling also has the same objective, but the
approach is to increase the records of the minority class. There are various
ways to achieve this. The simplest and most naive approach is to merely
duplicate or create copies of the records from the minority class. You can
do this as much as you want to achieve better balance. One such approach
is Random Oversampling where you randomly choose a certain
proportion of the records to duplicate. The beneﬁt of over-sampling is that
unlike under sampling, we do not lose any information from the data. We
will soon see better ways of over-sampling. But let us ﬁrst implement
Random Oversampling and assess the performance of the model
ourselves.
 
An important decision that we need to make when performing
oversampling is the extent of the balance that we want to achieve. We can
look at the ratio of the minority to the majority class to measure the
imbalance. In our problem statement, the ratio is 4,248/21,832 which is
0.195. We surely want to increase this ratio to achieve a greater balance.
In situations where we have extreme ratios, to begin with, say 1:1,000,
rebalancing to a 1:1 ratio would mean replicating the minority class 1,000
times. As your intuition might tell you, this isn’t a great idea. As
mentioned earlier , a ratio of 1:10 is a good deal better than 1:1,000. The
necessary extent of rebalance varies by case and the data scientist should
aim to get the best performance without replicating the data too much. For
our case study , we will rebalance to a ratio of 0.75 that is records of the
minority class/records of the majority class would be 0.75 (3:4). The
oversampling algorithms in the imblearn package can be supplied via the
‘sampling_strategy’  parameter . If unspeciﬁed, the default value is 1 which
is a complete rebalance of the classes. Let’s see all of this in action in the
following points:
 
1. We ﬁrst import the RandomOverSampler utility from the
imblearn.over_sampling module using the following code:
 
from imblearn.over_sampling import RandomOverSampler
 
2. Next, create an instance ‘o_sampler ’ of the RandomOverSampler
specifying 0.75 as `sampling_strategy’  and 42 as `random_state`:
 
o_sampler = RandomOverSampler(random_state=42,
sampling_strategy=0.75)
 
3. Now , use the ﬁt_resample method of the sampler , supplying X_train
and y_train to it. The method returns resampled versions of X_train and
y_train - collect them in X_train_over and y_train_over variables using
the following code :
 
X_train_over , y_train_over = o_sampler .ﬁt_resample(X_train, y_train)
 
4. Check the counts for each class in Y_train using the value_count
method using the following command:
 
y_train_over .value_counts()
 
You will see the frequencies in Figure
 
 
Figure Updated distribution of response
 
The number of records with class 0 does not change, but it has
signiﬁcantly increased for class 1 (it was 4,284 earlier). The ratio between
the classes is now 16,374/21,832, exactly 0.75, just as we speciﬁed using
the ‘sampling_strategy’  parameter .
 
5. Now , create an instance of LogisticRegression with 42 as
`random_state` and create a pipeline with the oversampler followed by
Logistic Regression:
 
logreg = LogisticRegression(random_state=42)
 
p_steps = [('over', o_sampler),
 
('model', logreg)]
 
pipeline = Pipeline(steps = p_steps)
 
With the pipeline speciﬁed, now we will evaluate the performance of the
approach by using the 3-fold cross-validation scheme we have been using
so far. Print the cross-validation performance in terms of precision, recall,
and F1 score:
 
cv_scores = cross_validate(pipeline,
 
X_train,
 
y_train,
 
cv=folds,
 
scoring=['precision','recall','f1'])
 
pd.DataFrame(cv_scores)
 
 
Figure Cross-validation scores
 
We observe that the precision of the pipeline is not the best, but recall is
decent overall. The overall F1 score also seems to be acceptable. The
oversampling strategy seems to fare decently overall. But is this the best
oversampling approach we have?
  
Note: Reiterating that we do not need a perfect 1:1 balance between
classes. Especially for cases with extreme imbalance (class ratio of, say,
1:1,000 ratio), a perfect rebalancing would mean oversampling the
minority class 1,000 times. Regardless of the technique you employ , it is
likely that you are not adding signiﬁcant useful information.
   
Pause and ponder: If you increase the `sampling_strategy` value to 1, how
do you expect the recall to change? Would you expect it to increase or
decrease? And why?
  
It is important to note that when performing Random Oversampling, you
are not adding any new information to the dataset. You could argue that
this approach is not different from simply adjusting the class weights in
the model. The additional observations we created were simply dumb
copies of existing records. A smarter approach would be to use the
patterns in the data to generate new records. Data generation approaches
like SMOTE and ADASYN do just that. Let us now understand these
approaches and see for ourselves if they help us with the model
performance.
Synthetic Minority Oversampling Technique
 
Synthetic Minority Oversampling Technique The idea behind the SMOTE
technique is elegant and powerful. Figure 5.34 illustrates the workings of
the technique. Instead of creating duplicates of all records or randomly
selected records from the minority Class, the technique generates
synthetic observations representing the minority class by interpolating the
points. More concretely , there are 3 steps in the SMOTE algorithm:
 
1. Identify a point from the minority class
 
2. Find its nearest neighbours from the minority class
 
3. Create a synthetic observation along the line between the point and the
nearest neighbours
 
For each point in the minority class, the SMOTE algorithm looks at a few
of its nearest neighbours (defaults to 5 in the imblearn implementation). A
link is created between the point and its neighbours and the new synthetic
points lie on these links. By design, SMOTE aims to create synthetic
records of the minority class that are ‘in-between’  other points of the
minority class. The number of points created depends on the extent of the
rebalancing speciﬁed. See Figure 5.34 which illustrates the working of
SMOTE:
 
 
Figure The SMOTE technique in action
 
As you can imagine, the separation between the classes isn’t always as
clean as in the illustration. We may (and usually do) have points belonging
to the minority class right between the majority class points. This can lead
to situations where you have newly generated minority class points, even
though such a point didn’ t exist in the original data. This leads to ‘noise’
in the data, as we are now seeing data that are not helpful towards our
objective. For larger datasets, however , this could be a beneﬁt. The
additional points effectively make the decision boundary larger,
effectively leading to regularization. When using larger datasets and high-
variance classiﬁers, SMOTE can therefore provide a performance boost. A
data scientist working with SMOTE should always be cognizant of the
consequences of the nature of the algorithm.
  
Note: The SMOTE technique interpolates a signiﬁcant number of points
between marginal outliers and inliers, leading to noisy samples. This issue
can be solved by cleaning the space resulting from over-sampling, which
we will explore in an upcoming section on combining over and under -
sampling approaches.
Exercise 5.7: Synthetic Data Generation with SMOTE
 
In this hands-on exercise, we will use SMOTE to generate synthetic
samples using the minority class points to balance the data. We will use
the SMOTE utility from the over-sampling module in imblearn. Perform
the following steps.
 
1. Import the SMOTE utility and create an instance of the over-sampler
specifying 0.75 as sampling_strategy . Use the following code:
 
from imblearn.over_sampling import SMOTE
 
o_sampler = SMOTE(random_state=42, sampling_strategy=0.75)
 
2. Create a pipeline with SMOTE and Logistic Regression and evaluate
the model performance using the 3-fold cross-validation scheme we have
been using so far:
 
logreg = LogisticRegression(random_state=42)
 
p_steps = [('over', o_sampler),
 
('model', logreg)]
 
pipeline = Pipeline(steps = p_steps)
 
cv_scores = cross_validate(pipeline,
 
X_train,
 
y_train,
 
cv=folds,
 
scoring=['precision','recall','f1'])
 
pd.DataFrame(cv_scores)
 
The performance gets printed as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Cross-validation scores
 
We notice that the performance is very similar to the performance of the
random oversampling approach. A detailed diagnosis is not prudent at this
stage, but we do suspect that because of its nature, the SMOTE technique
could be creating noisy points that are not helping the model in any way.
Let us now discuss another approach that overcomes the drawbacks of
SMOTE.
ADASYN - Adaptive Synthesis
 
Recall that the issue with SMOTE is that for the minority class points
very close to majority class points, it creates a lot of noise points that
can make classiﬁcation more difﬁcult for the model. ADASYN solves
this by effectively de-prioritizing such points. ADASYN creates more
synthetic points for minority class points that have fewer majority
class points around them. The points generated will be the least for
minority class points that lie in between majority class points thus the
‘adaptive’  in the name.
 
Theoretically , ADASYN should perform better than SMOTE. Let us
see for ourselves if that is practically true for our vehicle insurance
case study .
Exercise 5.8: Synthetic Data Generation with ADASYN
 
In this hands-on exercise, we will use ADASYN to generate synthetic
samples. We will use the ADASYN utility from the over_sampling
module in imblearn. Let us start by importing ADASYN and creating an
instance of the over sampler (sampling strategy at 0.75) using the
following code:
 
from imblearn.over_sampling import ADASYN
 
o_sampler = ADASYN(random_state=42, sampling_strategy=0.75)
 
Now , create a pipeline with ADASYN followed by Logistic Regression
and evaluate the model performance using the 3-fold evaluation scheme
we have been using so far:
 
logreg = LogisticRegression(random_state=42)
 
p_steps = [('over', o_sampler),
 
('model', logreg)]
 
pipeline = Pipeline(steps = p_steps)
 
cv_scores = cross_validate(pipeline,
 
X_train,
 
y_train,
 
cv=folds,
 
scoring=['precision','recall','f1'])
 
pd.DataFrame(cv_scores)
 
The result gets printed as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Cross-validation scores
 
As seen in Figure there was no improvement in the F1 score. However ,
there was an improvement in the recall score. Note that the higher recall
comes at the cost of reduced precision. This score is an improvement in
terms of recall.
 
Let us now discuss another aspect of handling imbalance: the extent of
rebalancing. Let us look at the considerations and explore smarter ways of
rebalancing.
 
Extent of rebalancing
 
Do you always need to rebalance the classes 1:1? Most real-world
situations do not have a perfect balance between the classes, so do we
always need to rebalance them? The answer to this depends on the data as
well as the machine learning algorithm. If the observations are easily
separable using the available features, techniques like Logistic Regression
and SVC with linear kernels may work ﬁne without extreme rebalancing.
In other cases, algorithms like Boosting and RandomForest (with
sufﬁcient depth) can also work well without the classes being equally
distributed. Of course, it is not practical to know Apriori which approach
will work better . We, therefore, need to evaluate what works best for our
situation. We used 0.75 as the ratio of minority to majority class in the
examples above and it seemed to be working well for our situation. It is
always a good idea to try different extents of rebalancing.
  
Note: The ratio of minority to majority class observations is a user choice
that affects the performance of the model. It is therefore a hyper -
parameter . We will learn how to tune hyper -parameters in Chapter 7,
Hyper -parameter Tuning.
Combination of under and oversampling
 
Under -sampling of the majority class may drop a signiﬁcant number of
records and therefore information from the data. On the other hand,
oversampling of the minority class in certain cases may not add
signiﬁcant value and might lead to mere replication of the data already
contained. Additionally , we discussed that oversampling methods like
SMOTE and ADASYN are effective, but there is a potential problem
of ‘noise’, where the techniques generate some noisy samples when
they interpolate. One way to ﬁx this is by “cleaning the space”.
 
Under -sampling methods like Tomek Links (or Edited Nearest
Neighbours) can help us clean the space by dropping these noisy
records. The idea is fundamentally simple, add a cleaning step to the
pipeline after the SMOTE step. SMOTE would generate synthetic
samples from the minority class (albeit with some noise) and the
Tomek Links method reduces this noise. We could manually build
pipelines composed of these components. For example, we could chain
a SMOTE and ENN to create a combo pipeline ourselves. However ,
very conveniently for us, the pipelines have been implemented in the
imblearn package. This means that a combination sampler like
SMOTE Tomek or SMOTEENN can be used just like any other
sampler . Let us see it in action in the following exercise.
Exercise 5.9: Using a combination of over and undersampling
 
In this exercise, you will evaluate the combination of over and under -
sampling for rebalancing the data. First, you will use SMOTE + Tomek
Links create a pipeline, and evaluate the cross-validation performance of
the model. Then, you use SMOTE + Edited Nearest Neighbours for
rebalancing and compare the performance of the two approaches. Perform
the following steps:
 
1. Import the SMOTEENN and SMOTET omek utilities from
imblearn.combine module :
 
from imblearn.combine import SMOTEENN, SMOTET omek
 
2. Create an instance of Logistic regression, specify 42 as `random_state`:
 
logreg = LogisticRegression(random_state=42)
 
3. Create an instance of SMOTET omek specifying 0.75 as
`sampling_strategy`:
 
combo_sampler = SMOTET omek(random_state=42,
sampling_strategy=0.75)
 
4. Create a pipeline of SMOTET omek and Logistic Regression, calculate
and print the cross-validation scores (Precision, Recall and F1 score as
metrics) as a dataframe:
 
p_steps = [('combo', combo_sampler),
 
('model', logreg)]
 
pipeline = Pipeline(steps = p_steps)
 
cv_scores = cross_validate(pipeline,
 
X_train,
 
y_train,
 
cv=folds,
 
scoring=['precision','recall','f1'])
 
pd.DataFrame(cv_scores)
 
 
Figure Cross-validation scores
 
5. Create an instance of SMOTEENN specifying 0.75 as
`sampling_strategy`:
 
combo_sampler = SMOTEENN(random_state=42,
sampling_strategy=0.75)
 
6. Create a pipeline of SMOTEENN and Logistic Regression, calculate,
and print the cross-validation scores (Precision, Recall, and F1 score as
metrics) as a dataframe:
 
p_steps = [('combo', combo_sampler),
 
('model', logreg)]
 
pipeline = Pipeline(steps = p_steps)cv_scores = cross_validate(pipeline,
 
X_train,
 
y_train,
 
cv=folds,
 
scoring=['precision','recall','f1'])
 
pd.DataFrame(cv_scores)
 
You should get results as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Cross-validation scores
 
What do you observe? Which works better?
 
We see that the results from both approaches are very similar and
practically equal. We do not have a clear winner . Also, note that the
overall performance of the combination approach is decent and gives us
among the best recall and F1 scores so far.
Comparing the approaches
 
We saw a variety of approaches for handling imbalance, from the simplest
approach of adjusting the cost function to using a sequence of
oversampling and clean-up techniques. We saw that some of these
approaches worked better than the others in our vehicle insurance case
study . But so far, we have been looking at the cross-validation scores. Let
us now see which approaches work best on the unseen test data, the true
test of the approaches.
 
We begin by creating a list containing the different techniques we want to
evaluate. Based on the cross-validation results, we saw that the following
approaches gave among the best results:
 
•Random Over Sampling (over sampling)
 
•Edited Nearest Neighbours (under sampling)
 
•ADASYN (over sampling)
 
•SMOTEENN (over sampling + under sampling)
 
We create a `sampler_list` containing instances of these 4 samplers. We
will specify 0.75 as the sampling_strategy for RandomOverSampler ,
ADASYN, and SMOTEENN. Use the following code:
 
sampler_list = [EditedNearestNeighbours(n_jobs=-1),
 
RandomOverSampler(random_state=42, sampling_strategy=0.75),
 
ADASYN(random_state=42, sampling_strategy=0.75),
 
SMOTEENN(random_state=42, sampling_strategy=0.75)]
 
Instantiate a Logistic Regression classiﬁer . Also import the utilities
precision_score, recall_Score, and f1_score from sklearn.metrics. We will
calculate these metrics for each sampler:
 
logreg = LogisticRegression(random_state=42)
 
from sklearn.metrics import precision_score, recall_score, f1_score
 
For each sampler , we will create a pipeline (sampler + logistic regression)
and train on the train set. We will then predict the test classes and
calculate and print the precision, recall and F1 score on the test set for
each sampler . Use the following code:
 
for sampler in sampler_list:
 
p_steps = [('sampler', sampler),
 
('model', logreg)]
 
pipeline = Pipeline(steps = p_steps)
 
pipeline.ﬁt(X_train, y_train)
 
print(sampler)
 
y_pred = pipeline.predict(X_test)
 
print('Precision:', precision_score(y_test, y_pred).round(2),
 
'Recall:', recall_score(y_test, y_pred).round(2),
 
'F1 Score:', f1_score(y_test, y_pred).round(2),
 
'\n')
 
The performance of the sampling approaches is printed as shown in
Figure
 
 
Figure test performance of the methods
 
From the results, we observe that if F1 were our only criterion, the Edited
Nearest Neighbour approach would be the clear winner . However , we
need to get good recall as well. Note that the results are close, and most
approaches achieve a similar recall. The SMOTEENN approach has an F1
score of 0.54, a good recall of 0.9, and a precision is 0.39. This result
seems to be the most balanced. For our vehicle insurance case study , we
will therefore go ahead with SMOTEENN.
Conclusion
 
Imbalanced classes in Machine Learning are a common situation,
many real-world situations have a skewed balance of the classes. This
is particularly common when the object is to ‘detect’  an event, a
purchase on an eCommerce platform, or a disease. We ﬁrst discussed
the difﬁculty in the model evaluation due to imbalance. We saw that
we needn’ t limit ourselves to simple accuracy and have a variety of
metrics available. A well-thought-out choice of metric goes a long way
in setting us up for success.
 
Handling class imbalance by making a high-performance machine
learning model that handles the imbalance is not straightforward.
There are several approaches which broadly fall into two categories,
sampling of the majority class and over-sampling of the minority class.
While both aim to achieve a more balanced class representation, the
approaches can be wildly different. Under -sampling approaches can
either randomly drop points from the data (Random Under Sampling)
or perform involved clean-up (Tomek Links, Edited Nearest
Neighbour) of the data to make the classiﬁcation easier for the
modeling technique. Under -sampling drops points and therefore
potentially loses some information contained in the dataset.
 
In contrast, over-sampling does not lose any data. It derives newer data
points from the existing minority class points. This derivation could be
simple replication, as in the case of Random Over Sampling. Or, as in
the case of SMOTE and ADASYN, we could generate newer points
similar to the minority class points. We noted that these methods tend
to generate noisy data. The combination approach – using
oversampling followed by cleanup of the data is also a promising
option. We saw all these approaches in action in our vehicle insurance
case study .
 
Imbalanced classes are frequent in real-world problems. Imbalanced
Machine Learning is therefore an essential skill for a data scientist to
excel in today’ s world. Apart from a good understanding of the
problem statement and the domain, solving imbalanced problems also
requires a good understanding of machine learning techniques. With
this chapter , you have learned the essential skills and implemented
them. Well done!
 
In the next chapter , we will learn about a very direct way of optimizing
the model: modifying the hyper -parameters of the model. Hyper -
parameter tuning is one of the most powerful methods of getting the
most optimized ML/AI skill that any data scientist must master .

Authors:
 
https://discord.bpbonline.com
 

CHAPTER 6
Hyper -parameter Tuning
Introduction
 
You are a data scientist in the marketing team at a bank. The bank is
gearing up for its next major marketing campaign. The product to be
promoted is not new, and there have been marketing campaigns in the
past. Surely , the past campaign’ s data can be used to improve the
upcoming campaign. A good assumption is that the kind of customers
who responded positively to the previous campaign will respond
positively again. Therefore, you could analyze the data to identify the
customer segments that are likely to respond positively . The
constraints are that the output/model should be interpretable by non-
technical marketers. The number of segments for the marketing team
to act on should be limited. High accuracy is not an important
requirement.
  
Pause and ponder: Which modeling technique(s) do you think works
best in such a situation? Make a list of techniques suitable for the task
and rank them in order of your preference.
   
Hint: Go through the constraints again. Interpretability disqualiﬁes
many high-performance models.
  
After much discussion with your team, you chose to use Decision
Trees. But how do you inﬂuence the Decision Tree model to make it
respect your constraints? Controlling the depth seems like a good
option! But how do we decide the best depth? Very low depth may
lead to a model too simple and low in performance to be useful. How
do you strike a balance?
 
In another situation, the only requirement could be to get the
maximum possible accuracy , without having to worry about
interpretability . The only constraint could be the speed of making
predictions. Let us say that you’re planning to use tree-based models
(Decision Trees, Random Forests, Boosting, etc.). You need to make
choices again - what should be the maximum depth of the tree? What
depth gives you the best result without overﬁtting the data?
 
Yet another situation could be where the model doesn’ t need to be
interpretable, but there is an upper limit on the time it needs to make
predictions. In such a situation, you would look for a model that gives
you the highest accuracy and does not exceed the allowed prediction
time.
 
In all the aforementioned situations, the task is similar - you wish to
ﬁnd the value of a ‘setting’  that gives you the best/optimal result while
respecting the constraints. This process of searching for the best setting
is called ‘hyper -parameter tuning’. Let us understand ‘hyper -
parameters’  and what it means to ‘tune’  them.
Structure
 
In this chapter , we will cover the following topics:
 
•Parameters and hyper -parameters
 
•Importance of hyper -parameters
 
•Hyper -parameter tuning and approaches
 
•Manual looping
 
•GridSearchCV  - Grid Search with Cross-V alidation
 
•Limitations of exhaustive methods
 
•RandomizedSearchCV
 
•Grid Search versus Randomized Search
 
•Choosing a hyper -parameter tuning approach
 
•Choosing the ‘best’  model
 
•Training on the entire data and making predictions
 
•The complete process
Objectives
 
After reading this chapter , you will be able to optimize the model to
get the best generalized performance by way of ﬁnding the best set of
“settings” for the model, i.e., hyper -parameters. You will be able to
assess the impact of hyper -parameters on model performance and be
able to choose the “best” model. You will also learn some
considerations in deﬁning “best”. You will learn multiple automated
approaches to searching for the best hyper -parameters – from
exhaustive methods like grid search to approaches like Randomized
search that work better for larger datasets and hyper -parameter
combinations. You will then be able to use these hyper -parameters to
train on the entire dataset, get the best generalized performance and
start making predictions on unseen data.
Parameters and hyper -parameters
 
Hyper -parameters are parameters that are ‘external’  to the model and
control the behavior and learning of the model. By ‘external’  we mean
that the model does not learn these parameters. Instead, these need to
be provided to the modeling algorithm by the user. They are not to be
confused with ‘model parameters.’
 
Let us understand ‘model parameters’  ﬁrst. Let us take the example of
a linear regression model. The output of a linear regression model are
the coefﬁcients corresponding to the predictors. The coefﬁcients are
what deﬁne the model and learning these coefﬁcients is what training
is all about. These coefﬁcients are internal to the model and are learned
by the model during the training process. Hyper -parameters, by
deﬁnition, are not learned by the model.
 
To make the idea more concrete, let us take the example of a decision
tree. A very important characteristic of a decision tree is its depth, that
is, the number of levels in the tree, or the maximum number of
decisions/questions allowed to get to a prediction. A tree with a depth
of just 1 will ask just one question to make a prediction - a single
question to decide whether , say, an individual will default on a loan or
not. This decision stump is the simplest of models. Another extreme
could be a decision tree with a depth of 15 - meaning that it could ask
15 questions to get to a prediction and is therefore far more complex.
Modeling packages have a standard ‘setting’  for Decision Trees which
is ‘maximum depth’  - the maximum depth that the tree is allowed to
grow to. The decision tree does not learn this and is to be provided by
the user. Depending on the speciﬁed ‘maximum depth’, the resulting
tree and therefore the model can be very different. The maximum
depth of the tree is, therefore, a
  
Pause and ponder: We are basing the deﬁnition on one key feature -
hyper -parameters need to be speciﬁed by the user. But what if this can
be automated? What if I have a code that automatically ﬁnds the best
depth for a Decision Tree? Does it then become a parameter?
   
Hint: Hyper -parameters are external to the model and inﬂuence the
model training.
  
Each algorithm has its own set of different hyper -parameters. For a
KNN classiﬁer , the number of neighbours is an important hyper -
parameter that the user must specify . Similarly , for a K-means
clustering, the number of clusters needs to be speciﬁed by the user. For
Deep Neural Networks, the activation functions are among the many
hyper -parameters. For SVM, the type of Kernel needs to be speciﬁed.
These, along with some other key hyper -parameters, control how the
algorithm learns and inﬂuences the resulting model.
 
For most standard packages, you can easily ﬁnd the list of hyper -
parameters that can be speciﬁed by the user for that algorithm. For
example, for a Decision Tree Classiﬁer in SciKit Learn, you can ﬁnd
the available hyper -parameters by using the following code:
 
from sklearn.tree import DecisionT reeClassiﬁer
 
?DecisionT reeClassiﬁer
 
You will see the signature of the class which includes the parameters
the user can provide:
 
 
Figure Hyper -parameters in a decision tree
 
All the above are hyper -parameters that you can specify . We see that
for an algorithm as simple as a Decision Tree, you can have several
hyper -parameters that control how the tree model grows. Moving to
tree-based ensemble methods only increases the hyper -parameters.
 
Task 1: Go ahead and check out the hyper -parameters for any other
algorithm that you are curious about. If you can’t think of any
algorithm, go ahead with LogisticRegression. Which hyper -parameters
do you think are most important? Which of these have you already
adjusted in the previous chapters?
Importance of hyper -parameters
 
We mentioned that hyper -parameters impact how the model learns and the
resulting model can be very different for different choices of hyper -
parameters. Let us continue with the example of the Decision Tree as it is
extremely suitable and makes for a great example of the importance of
hyper -parameters. Instead of discussing theoretically , we will demonstrate
this signiﬁcance.
 
Let us ﬁrst make a decision tree with the maximum depth speciﬁed as 2.
The parameter to specify while creating an instance of the
DecisionT reeClassiﬁer is ‘max_depth’. We won’ t specify any other hyper -
parameter . Use the following code:
 
from sklearn.tree import DecisionT reeClassiﬁer
 
dt = DecisionT reeClassiﬁer(max_depth=2)
 
dt.ﬁt(X_train, y_train)
 
With that, the ﬁrst decision tree with a maximum depth of 2 has been
trained. To understand the tree and see what it looks like, we can use the
plot_tree method in sklearn. You can do so using the following code:
 
from sklearn.tree import plot_tree
 
plt.ﬁgure(ﬁgsize=[10,6])
 
plot_tree(dt, feature_names=X_train.columns, ﬁlled=T rue, rounded=T rue)
 
plt.show()
 
You should get a tree as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Decision Tree for Depth 2
 
The Decision tree in Figure 6.2 has two levels, i.e., it asks at most two
questions to get to a leaf (that contains the prediction). This tree is simple
to read and it’s easy to see how a decision is being made. If you have
difﬁculty understanding the tree in Figure make sure to read the following
optional explanation. Note that understanding the tree itself is not required
for this chapter and that you can proceed even if the interpretation of the
tree isn’t crystal clear to you.
 
Optional: Interpreting a decision tree
 
The decision tree can be read and interpreted like a ﬂow chart, a rather
simple one. The arrows indicate the direction you need to move. The
arrows are from top to bottom, which means that you begin at the top
(root node) and traverse to the bottom (leaf nodes). A question is asked at
each node. If the answer to the question is yes, then you move to the left
in the next level. A ‘No’ means you move to the right.
 
Consider the path leading to the orange box (second from left in the last
row) in Figure The ﬁrst question that is asked at the top (the root node) is
if the previous outcome success is <= 0.5 (that is, 0, meaning a failure).
Let us say the answer is ‘Yes’, that is, the previous outcome was a failure.
You, therefore, move to the left in the next level. The next question asked
is if contact_unknown <= 0.5 (that is, 0, is the contact known?). If the
answer to this question is no (i.e., contact is not known), then you move to
the right to the next level. This leads to the leaf node where no further
question is asked. In this node, you can see that most of the records
belong to the ﬁrst class, i.e., class 0, the customers with whom the
campaign did not work. These are customers who meet both the
conditions:
 
1. The previous campaign was a failure.
 
2. Contact is unknown.
 
It makes sense that these customers have a very low chance of success.
 
The way you read the tree above is the standard way in which you can
read any decision tree. As you can see, for small trees, it is extremely easy
to look at each path and understand exactly how the model works.
 
If you need more explanation and examples, head over to the following
link:
 
https://www .ibm.com/topics/decision-trees
 
Now , let us train another Decision Tree; this time with no depth control. It
would be an interesting experiment to ‘see’ this tree with much higher
depth. Do not specify any max_depth value, which will allow the tree to
grow to its fullest. Train the model and plot the tree like we did for the
previous tree. Note that if the tree has high depth, it may take a while to
display the plot:
 
dt = DecisionT reeClassiﬁer(random_state=42)
 
dt.ﬁt(X_train, y_train)
 
plt.ﬁgure(ﬁgsize=[15,10])
 
plot_tree(dt, feature_names=X_train.columns, ﬁlled=T rue, rounded=T rue)
 
plt.show()
 
 
Figure Decision Tree with no depth control
 
Clearly , the second tree is far more complex than the ﬁrst. In this plot, the
tree isn’t even readable. You’ll be excused if you do not want to put the
effort into understanding this tree - neither did we.
 
Let us now compare the performances of the models on the train and test
sets. For the ﬁrst model, let us calculate the accuracy score on the train
and test sets using the following code and make note of the scores:
 
y_train_pred = dt_2.predict(X_train)
 
print("T rain accuracy:", accuracy_score(y_train, y_train_pred))
 
y_test_pred = dt_2.predict(X_test)
 
print("T est accuracy:", accuracy_score(y_test, y_test_pred))
 
The train and test accuracy should be around 0.609 and 0.593,
respectively . The simple model has similar performance on both train and
test sets. Let us now see how the complex model with no depth control
fares:
 
y_train_pred = dt.predict(X_train)
 
print("T rain accuracy:", accuracy_score(y_train, y_train_pred))
 
y_test_pred = dt.predict(X_test)
 
print("T est accuracy:", accuracy_score(y_test, y_test_pred))
 
The train accuracy should be around 0.999 - nearly perfect predictions.
The test performance, however , is drastically lower at around 0.63.
Clearly , this model is overﬁtting!
