overﬁtting! overﬁtting! overﬁtting! overﬁtting! overﬁtting!
 
Table Comparing the two decision trees
 
Not only do the models look very different, but their performances are
also strikingly different. A simple comparison between the two trees is
shown in Table The key takeaway is that the choice of hyper -parameters
can result in very different models. We employed only one hyper -
parameter - this was the only difference between the two training
attempts. In other situations, you may need to set multiple hyper -
parameters leading to potentially greater differences in the resulting
models. Hyper -parameters are therefore extremely important and can have
a signiﬁcant impact on the resulting model. It is, therefore, essential that
we understand the approaches to ﬁnding the right set of hyper -parameters,
a process commonly referred to as ‘hyper -parameter tuning’. The trees are
visualized in Figure
 
 
Figure Decision trees on our dataset for different values of max_depth.
Hyper -parameter tuning
 
By now we have seen that hyper -parameters greatly impact the
resulting model. A poor choice of hyper -parameters is often the reason
for the underwhelming performance of a model. Even if you have
good data and if you are employing an extremely powerful algorithm,
a bad set of hyper -parameters can lead to a poor model that just does
not utilize the information in the data or the potential of the algorithm.
The other way to look at this is -
 
Choosing the right set of hyper -parameters gets you the maximum out
of the data and the modeling algorithm.
 
Determining the right set of hyper -parameters can be considered a
search problem. Going back to the Decision Tree example - a very low
depth (2) gave a very simple model that was too simple, whereas a
high depth (no depth control) model was unnecessarily complex and
overﬁtting. Somewhere between these two extremes is the optimal
model - that has good generalized performance. Hyper -parameter
tuning in this example would be ﬁnding the optimal value of maximum
depth for the decision tree model.
 
In general, we work with multiple hyper -parameters. Hyper -parameter
tuning can therefore be thought of as ﬁnding the right combination of
hyper -parameters to get optimal model performance. As with search
problems in general, there are multiple approaches to hyper -parameter
tuning. These approaches could be manual or automated, could be
exhaustive searches or smarter ones that quickly zone into the optimal
values. However , the essence of these approaches is the same and can
be simpliﬁed to a few key steps. For a given dataset, estimator
(modeling algorithm), success metric is as follows:
 
1. Perform a k-fold split on the training data.
 
2. For a candidate set of hyper -parameters (i.e., a particular
combination), obtain the cross-val score using the scoring metric
speciﬁed.
 
3. Store the candidate hyper -parameter combination, its cross-
validation scores, training scores, and timing details.
 
4. Choose the ‘best estimator ’ as one that provides the ‘best’
performance.
  
Note: It is important to recall at this juncture that the deﬁnition of
‘best’  depends on the situation and there are several considerations in
declaring an estimator the best. We touched upon this idea in Chapter
4, Model Evaluation and We will discuss this further in this chapter in
the section Choosing the ‘best’  model.
  
The scoring metric is also speciﬁed by the user. Recall from the
discussion in Chapter 4, Model Evaluation and that this is an important
choice that deﬁnes the utility of your model and must be aligned with
the overall objective of the project.
  
Note: Notice that we use cross-validation scores for model evaluation,
and not training scores or the test scores. We have discussed model
evaluation extensively in Chapter 4, Model Evaluation and and saw
that cross-validation score is a great way to estimate the generalized
performance of the model on unseen data. For this reason, most hyper -
parameter tuning algorithms use the cross val score for evaluating a
candidate hyper -parameter set.
  
The preceding steps are general steps in any hyper -parameter tuning
approach. Extensive research has gone into solving this search
problem as quickly as possible, using minimal resources, by way of
smartly identifying the high-potential candidate combinations. This has
spun multiple hyper -parameter tuning algorithms. We will discuss
some prominent approaches. But ﬁrst, we will perform this search
ourselves manually in the simplest possible manner - manual looping.
Manual looping over hyper -parameters
 
This ﬁrst approach is simple enough for us to code ourselves with ease.
We will manually loop through the candidate hyper -parameter
combinations, calculate, and store the cross-validation scores. We can
then pick the best based on the performance. Let us begin!
 
The general approach for manual looping is very simple and can be
described as a sequence of 4 simple steps:
 
1. Store the candidate values of the hyper -parameter in a list.
 
2. Loop over the list and for each candidate value, ﬁt the model.
 
3. Calculate and store the cross-validation score.
 
4. Choose the ‘best estimator ’ as one that provides the ‘best’
performance.
Perform your own manual hyper -parameter tuning
 
We will continue using the same dataset and use the Decision Tree
modeling algorithm. We will pick one hyper -parameter - max_depth - for
our implementation. We will loop through the values 1 through 15, in
increments of 1 (stored in a list) and store the cross-validation score
using a 4-fold cross-validation scheme.
  
Note: We need to be mindful of the number of models ﬁts we are
planning to execute. In the preceding example, we will be trying 15
values of the hyper -parameter , performing a 4-fold cross-validation each
time. This would mean 15 times 4, i.e., 60 model ﬁts. This is a small
number for this example but can get out of hand very easily with a higher
number of hyper -parameters.
  
Begin with deﬁning a list ‘depth_val’  that contains the range of values
from 1 through 15 using the range function. Also, deﬁne an empty list
that will contain the mean cross-validation score for each candidate, as
shown here:
 
depth_vals = range(1,16)
 
candidate_score = []
 
Next, we will loop through the values of depth. For each depth value, we
will instantiate a DecisionT reeClassiﬁer with that depth. Then, calculate
the mean cross val score with a 4-fold CV scheme, and append it to the
candidate_score list. We do this using the following code:
 
for depth in depth_vals:
 
dt = DecisionT reeClassiﬁer(max_depth=depth, random_state=42)
 
cv_scores = cross_val_score(estimator=dt, X=X_train, y=y_train, cv=4,
scoring='accuracy')
 
candidate_score.append(cv_scores.mean())
 
The candidate_score list will now contain the score for each depth value.
To analyze the results visually , we’ll plot the score against each depth
value using the following code:
 
plt.ﬁgure(ﬁgsize=[10,6])
 
plt.plot(depth_vals, candidate_score)
 
plt.xlabel("Max Depth")
 
plt.ylabel("Cross Validation Accuracy")
 
plt.show()
 
We get the graph shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Accuracy vs. Max depth
 
The graph of accuracy vs. max depth in Figure 6.5 shows that the
accuracy is maximized at slightly less than 70% for depth 9. This is
where the model gets us the best-generalized performance and is the
optimal value of the hyper -parameter .
 
The manual approach we used is very useful because it gives us a lot of
control. We needn’ t restrict ourselves to storing just the mean accuracy -
we could add more metrics and custom metrics too. We could also add
more details - about the training time, perhaps, or the median accuracy
from the different folds, or even the training accuracy . You get the drift.
We have total control over the code we write and can customize it
heavily if needed. This approach is very useful and we encourage you to
try out variations of the same that include more details on the
performance.
 
While very promising, the manual looping approach has a couple of
limitations:
 
•Adding more details and extending it to multiple hyper -parameters
combinations could be cumbersome to code manually .
 
•The method employs brute force and is dumb in the sense that it must
go through all the combinations.
 
Despite its drawbacks, in some cases where you need a very high level of
customization, this approach may be your only option. But in general, we
now have standard libraries which cover most of the typical use cases. In
that sense, you do not need to re-invent anything and can employ the
methods available in popular packages like Scikit Learn. These are very
simple to use, very convenient, and store a lot of details that you would
ordinarily require. Let us begin understanding the ﬁrst of these
automated approaches available in Scikit Learn - GridSearchCV .
GridSearchCV  - Grid Search with Cross-V alidation
 
What GridSearchCV  search does is not very different from the manual
looping approach. But it does so in an automated fashion that greatly
reduces our coding efforts and helps save signiﬁcant time. If you need
to tune multiple hyper -parameters, GridSearchCV  makes the code
much simpler . Note that this is still an exhaustive, simple approach that
tries every possible combination of the speciﬁc hyper -parameters.
 
Let us now use the convenient GridSearchCV  method for hyper -
parameter tuning for the same Bank Marketing example. We’ll assess
the same candidates for using GridSearch and discover for ourselves
its beneﬁts and drawbacks.
 
First, let us understand the parameters needed for GridSearchCV .
Begin with importing it from sklearn using the following command:
 
from sklearn.model_selection import GridSearchCV
 
Use the following command to invoke a good description of the
method and usage examples:
 
?GridSearchCV
 
The parameters should be printed as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Parameters for GridSearchCV
 
Not all parameters are equally important to us. You need the following
key parameters:
 
•estimator : The predictor you want to use.
 
•param_grid : The hyper -parameters and their values you want to try,
passed as a dictionary .
 
•cv : To specify the cross-validation scheme you want to use; you can
supply an integer which will be considered the number of folds in the
k-fold scheme. E.g. use 4 for 4-fold cross-validation.
 
Additionally , you can use the following parameters to further
customize the process:
 
•scoring : The metric to assess performance on. You can choose
accuracy , precision, recall, or any of the standard metrics available in
sklearn. If scoring isn’t speciﬁed, the default measure of the estimator
is used, which is ‘accuracy’  for most classiﬁers.
 
•n_jobs : To utilize multi-core setup for parallel processing, if
available. If -1 is supplied, all available cores can be utilized for
maximum execution speed.
 
•verbose : The level of detail in the progress updates. If 0, no progress
is displayed. The higher the number , the greater the detail of the
progress printed out to you. A value of 2 gives enough information for
most situations.
 
•return_train_score : True would return the performance on the
training folds as well. By default, it is False and only the validation set
performance is returned.
Using GridSearchCV  for hyper -parameter tuning
 
Let us now use GridSearchCV  to replicate the example we did with
manual looping. Let us begin with instantiating the estimator:
 
dt = DecisionT reeClassiﬁer(random_state=42)
 
Note that we did not specify any parameter to the classiﬁer except for This
will ensure that the random_state is ﬁxed to what we want. In case there is
another parameter you want to ﬁx, you can do so by specifying it in the
instantiation command. For example, if you used
DecisionT reeClassiﬁer(random_state=42, then both random_state and
max_features would be ﬁxed at 42 and 4, respectively . The remaining
parameters will be their default values or be tuned through GridSearchCV .
 
The hyper -parameters to be tuned and their values to try are speciﬁed
using a ‘parameter grid’ - a dictionary . For our case, we want to try values
1 to 15 for the ‘max_depth’  hyper -parameter . The ‘parameter grid’
dictionary will be as follows:
 
hyper_params = {max_depth: range(1, 16)}
 
We will also use a 4-fold cross-validation scheme, so ‘cv’ would be 4. We
will set verbose to 2 to get some basic progress status. To return the train
score, we will set ‘return_train_score’  to True and set n_jobs to -1 to
utilize all cores. We will use ‘accuracy’  to score:
 
model_cv = GridSearchCV(estimator=dt,
 
param_grid=hyper_params,
 
scoring='accuracy',
 
cv=4,
 
n_jobs=-1,
 
verbose=2,
 
return_train_score=T rue)
 
The preceding command speciﬁes the settings we want to and returns a
‘GridSearchCV’  object into ‘model_cv’. To try out the different hyper -
parameters on the training data, we need to ‘ﬁt’ this on the data. The
syntax is the same as ﬁtting any sklearn model on training data:
 
model_cv .ﬁt(X_train, y_train)
 
This will ‘ﬁt’ on the data and try out the different combinations of hyper -
parameters and store performance information for each combination.
Because we used verbose = 2, the progress will be displayed on the
screen, as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Output from ﬁt command
 
There are 15 candidate hyper -parameter combinations. Since we’re using
a 4-fold CV scheme, 4 ﬁts were done for each candidate, totaling 60 ﬁts.
This is very useful as sometimes when you’re trying multiple hyper -
parameters, the number of combinations gets out of hand quickly and this
serves as a good reminder .
 
With the hyper -parameters tried, let us see which value of max_depth
gave us the best cross-validation accuracy . We can use the
‘best_estimator_’  and the ‘best_score_’  attributes of model_cv to ﬁnd the
best estimator and its cross-validation performance:
 
print(model_cv .best_estimator_)
 
print(model_cv .best_score_)
 
The output should be as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Best estimator and score
 
The best value of max_depth seems to be 9 which is exactly the result we
got from manual looping. The cross-validation accuracy is also very close
to what we got from manual looping. This shows that GridSearchCV  does
whatever we do using manual looping, just in a cleaner , modular manner .
 
So far, we just looked at the best estimator and its cross-validation score.
GridSearchCV  stores far more information than that in the ‘cv_results_’
attribute. It contains rich information about the performance on each
validation fold, the scoring time, the ﬁt time and more, to give you a very
detailed understanding of the performance beyond just the overall score.
Let us extract this information into a Pandas dataframe and explore it a
bit, using the following code:
 
cv_df = pd.DataFrame(model_cv .cv_results_)
 
print(cv_df.shape)
 
cv_df.head()
 
You’ll see the output as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Shape and ﬁrst 5 records of the dataset
 
There are 15 rows in the dataset - one for each parameter
value/combination tried. There are 19 columns giving you rich detail
about each ﬁt. Go ahead and explore the dataset and see for yourself the
details available. You will see that it does a lot of work for us while
requiring minimal coding skills for us. We will use this detailed
information shortly to see the utility of having all this in addition to the
highest accuracy .
GridSearchCV  with multiple hyper -parameters
 
The true utility of GridSearchCV  is apparent when tuning multiple hyper -
parameters at once. The hyper -parameters and their values to be tried are
supplied as a dictionary to ‘param_grid’  - this is all the change you need to
do in code to work with several hyper -parameters.
 
Let us continue with the Decision Tree example and tune several hyper -
parameters at once. We will tune - and Use the following code to set up
the parameter grid:
 
hyper_params = {'max_depth': [3, 5, 7, 9, 11, 13, 15],
 
'max_features': [3, 4, 5, 6, 7],
 
'min_samples_leaf': [5, 10, 15, 20, 35, 50]
 
 }
 
It should be easy to understand the combinations we are trying here. We
are trying multiple depth values - 3 to 15 in increments of 2. We are trying
max_features from 3 to 7. We are also trying to ﬁnd the best value for
min_samples_leaf among [5, 10, 15, 20, 35, 50]. GridSearchCV  will try
all combinations of these hyper -parameters. How many combinations
would there be? As many as the product of the number of unique values
being tried for each hyper -parameter - 7 x 5 x 6, i.e., 210 combinations.
 
Let us now use GridSearchCV  to ﬁnd the best combination of these 3
hyper -parameters - by supplying the hyper -parameter grid and ﬁtting on
the train data using the following code:
 
model_cv = GridSearchCV(estimator=dt,
 
param_grid=hyper_params,
 
verbose=1,
 
cv=4,
 
n_jobs=-1,
 
return_train_score=T rue)
 
model_cv .ﬁt(X_train, y_train)
 
You should get the following output:
 
 
Figure GridSearchCV  ﬁt output
 
We see that there are 210 candidates, just as we expected, and that there
are a total of 840 ﬁts (since we are using a 4-fold CV scheme). Note that
this number can signiﬁcantly increase with just one more parameter as the
candidates would increase in a multiplicative manner .
 
Now that the search is complete, what is the best hyper -parameter
combination? And what is its performance? Use the following code to ﬁnd
out:
 
print(model_cv .best_estimator_)
 
print(model_cv .best_score_)
 
 
Figure Best DT hyperparameters and score
 
The best estimator is one with the following hyper -parameters:
 
max_depth: 13
 
max_features: 7
 
min_samples_leaf: 15
 
This is the hyper -parameter combination that would give us the best
generalized accuracy .
The effect of each hyper -parameter
 
We saw earlier that the cv_results_ attribute of the trained GridSearchCV
contains detailed performance information for each combination. Since all
combinations of the hyper -parameters have been tried, this data is useful
to us. It can tell us how, on average, the performance of the estimator
changes with the value of the hyper -parameter . This can easily be plotted
to give us a great visual summary of the effect of the hyper -parameter on
the performance.
 
First, we load the results into a Pandas dataframe, inspect it, and
understand what details we have to work with:
 
cv_df = pd.DataFrame(model_cv .cv_results_)
 
print(cv_df.shape)
 
cv_df.columns
 
You’ll see in the following output that there are 210 rows in the
dataframe, corresponding to the 210 candidates/combinations:
 
 
Figure Columns available in the CV results
 
Let us take a look at the top 3 records to understand the values contained
in the columns beginning with ‘param’:
 
 
Figure First 3 records from the CV results
 
There are many columns in the dataframe. We are interested in the
parameter values, mean_train_score (training accuracy), and
mean_test_score (cross-validation accuracy) column. The hyper -
parameter values for the candidate tried are conveniently available in the
columns beginning with ‘param’. We can use this to see the average train
and cross-validation accuracy at a certain value of the hyper -parameter
and observe how the accuracy changes with the values tried. You can do
so using the following code:
 
res = cv_df.groupby('param_max_depth')['mean_train_score',
'mean_test_score'].mean()
 
res.plot(ﬁgsize=[8,5])
 
plt.show()
 
You should get the following output:
 

 
Figure Avg cross val score for max_depth candidates
 
Take a moment to understand this plot. You have the training accuracy and
cross-validation accuracy for each value of the hyper -parameter . What do
you infer from the plot?
 
As the max_depth increases, the training accuracy as well as the cross-
validation accuracy increase. The cross-validation accuracy increases
signiﬁcantly at ﬁrst but almost ﬂattens at around 0.67. The train score
increases at a much faster rate and continues to do so.
  
Pause and ponder: Look at the plot in Figure Is the model overﬁtting? If
yes, at what depth does it start to overﬁt?
   
Hint: Be mindful of the scale of the Y axis before concluding.
  
Let us see the impact of This is also an important parameter that affects
the tree growth and ensures the leaf nodes aren’ t smaller than the speciﬁed
value. Use the following code:
 
res = cv_df.groupby('param_min_samples_leaf')['mean_train_score',
'mean_test_score'].mean()
 
res.plot(ﬁgsize=[8,5])
 
plt.show()
 
You should get the following output:
 
 
Figure Effect of min_samples_leaf hyper -parameter
 
The effect of the min_samples_leaf hyper -parameter is rather interesting.
As the value increases, the training accuracy seems to be dropping
signiﬁcantly . The test score however is stable between 0.65 and 0.655 - all
values are performing the same on the test set. So, it looks like lowering
the value is only leading to overﬁtting and is not helping with the
generalized model performance.
 
Task 2: Perform a similar analysis for the hyper -parameter
‘max_features’. What pattern do you observe and how do you explain it?
What do you infer from the analysis?
 
Thus, with some simple commands, you can understand the impact of
each hyper -parameter on the model performance. This was possible
because of the detailed information that GridSearchCV  stores for each
run, requiring minimal code from us. Very useful indeed! This seems like
a great method and indeed it is. But it is not perfect. We must study its
drawbacks as well, as we will do in the next section.
Limitations of exhaustive methods (looping, GridSearchCV)
 
The manual looping method is great if you need to customize metrics and
want perfect control over the processing. The GridSearchCV  method is
also great because it simpliﬁes the code tremendously and gives you rich
performance information by default. Both these methods are of the
‘exhaustive’  type, i.e., they need to go through every candidate in a dumb
fashion to identify the best. These approaches work ﬁne for quick-to-train
algorithms like Decision Trees, Logistic Regression, etc. and small-sized
datasets where you could perform hundreds of ﬁts in a few minutes. But
when you have large datasets and are employing computationally heavy
algorithms, you need to look further . Let us understand the limitations of
these exhaustive methods ﬁrst.
 
We will continue with the Bank marketing example. The dataset is rather
small (about 8K observations) and we expect quick run times. For this
small dataset, we will employ the RandomForest algorithm. We will tune
the same parameters we did earlier while keeping the number of
estimators ﬁxed at 100. Note that the number of estimators is an important
hyper -parameter and should be tuned, but we’ll omit it for simplicity . For
this simple setup, we will note the execution time (%%time cell magic in
Jupyter). First, let us import RandomForestClassiﬁer and instantiate:
 
from sklearn.ensemble import RandomForestClassiﬁer
 
rf = RandomForestClassiﬁer(n_estimators=100, random_state=42,
n_jobs=-1)
 
We speciﬁed the number of estimators ﬁxed random_state for
reproducible results, and speciﬁed n_jobs as -1 to use all possible cores
for quicker execution. We will use the same param_grid we used for our
Decision Tree model. Tune the hyper -parameters and observe the timing
using the following code:
 
hyper_params = {'max_depth': [3, 5, 7, 9, 11, 13, 15],
 
'max_features': [3, 4, 5, 6, 7],
 
'min_samples_leaf': [5, 10, 15, 20, 35, 50]
 
 }
 
rf_grids = GridSearchCV(estimator=rf,
 
param_grid=hyper_params,
 
verbose=1,
 
cv=4,
 
n_jobs=-1,
 
return_train_score=T rue)
 
%%time
 
rf_grids.ﬁt(X_train, y_train)
 
You should get the following output:
 
 
Figure Output of the ﬁt command
  
Note: The run time here is indicative and of course, varies with the system
you are using. Nevertheless, the relative duration of tasks should be
similar . So, if you’re comparing the timings of different approaches, the
inference should be the same.
  
The number of ﬁts remains the same as earlier , as we didn’ t change the
parameter grid or the cross-validation scheme. The %%time cell magic
tells us that the cell took more than 2 to run. Now that the search is
complete, let us see the best hyper -parameter combination it found and the
cross-validation accuracy of the best estimator:
 
print(rf_grids.best_estimator_)
 
print(rf_grids.best_score_)
 
You will get the following output:
 
 
Figure Best RF estimator and score
 
The hyper -parameter combination is different from what we got earlier .
This is not surprising because the model is now very different. The
accuracy of the best estimator is signiﬁcantly higher at about 72%.
  
Pause and ponder: The best hyper -parameter set for Random Forest is
somewhat different from the Decision Tree. Which difference in hyper -
parameter seems most signiﬁcant to you? Also, the accuracy is
signiﬁcantly higher now. Is this expected?
  
Let us discuss the timing a bit. 2 minutes does seem like a lot. But
consider this - we used a rather small dataset (~8K records) and limited
hyper -parameters. What if we used the same setup (same hyper -
parameters) on a dataset with 200K records? Surely , the timing would be
several times higher . We didn’ t even try a different number of estimators -
it is not uncommon to try several hundred estimators. What if we also try
more values of n_estimators and other hyper -parameters on a dataset
having, say, 500K records? The run time estimate would be in hours, or
even days.
 
A training time of days is not very practical for many industry scenarios.
This is a limitation of the exhaustive approach (even after parallelizing
and using all processing cores).
 
Key takeaway: Exhaustive methods are great for small datasets as they are
thorough. However , they require a lot of time and computation which
could make them impractical in situations with large datasets and many
hyper -parameters.
 
Exhaustive methods are great because you can be as thorough as you want
and can be conﬁdent that all possible speciﬁed values have been tried.
With manual looping, you have far more control over the processing and
the information you store. GridSearchCV  is another solution that gives
you richer information using minimal code. But both these approaches
struggle with large datasets. While useful, both these approaches were
dumb in the sense that they tried every possible combination. These work
very hard but we need approaches that work Smarter as well. We need
approaches that, instead of going through all the possible candidates,
allow us to identify high-potential candidates and zone in into them
quickly . One such approach is RandomizedSearchCV . Let us understand
and explore this approach.
RandomizedSearchCV
 
The approach used by RandomizedSearchCV  is different. Instead of going
over all hyper -parameter values, sampling is done to try out a ﬁxed
number of times. Consider a situation in which you are tuning multiple
hyper -parameters and the number of candidates (unique combinations) is
2,000. GridSearchCV  would try all 2,000 candidates. But if you use
RandomizedSearchCV  and specify that you want to try at most 50
combinations, RandomizedSearchCV  honours it. This is the ﬁrst major
difference and you can see that this can reduce the run time signiﬁcantly .
But how does RandomizedSearchCV  select those 50 from the 2,000
candidates?
 
Here is where the ‘Randomized’  in the name comes from.
RandomizedSearchCV  randomly selects the 50 (or any speciﬁed number)
combinations. There’ s another difference in the way the values are tried.
Grid Search tries only the values that are available in the grid. For
continuous hyper -parameters (for example, learning rate of Boosting), this
isn’t ideal because you need to provide discrete steps or very limited
values. Whereas with RandomizedSearchCV , you can specify that the
hyper -parameter is continuous and can even use a distribution to pick the
values. This makes the search much better for continuous hyper -
parameters. The difference in the approaches is illustrated in Figure
 
 
Figure Grid Search vs. Randomized Search
 
Figure 6.18 shows a situation with two hyper -parameters. GridSearchCV
searches only values from the grid. Whereas RandomizedSearchCV  can
choose randomly from the search space and potentially values ‘off the
grid’.
  
Pause and ponder: Do you think there are beneﬁts of Randomized Search
(other than speed)? Do you expect Randomized Search to work as well as
Grid Search? If yes, under what conditions?
   
Hint: Randomized Search is faster but at the cost of thoroughness. But it
can explore values different from Grid Search.
  
Let us see RandomizedSearchCV  in action on our data and see for
ourselves what kind of results we get. To begin, import
RandomizedSearchCV  from sklearn.model_selection and instantiate the
RandomForestClassiﬁer estimator as in the following code. Use the same
hyper -parameter grid that we used for GridSearchCV :
 
from sklearn.model_selection import RandomizedSearchCV
 
rf = RandomForestClassiﬁer(n_estimators=100, random_state=42,
n_jobs=-1)
 
hyper_params = {'max_depth': [3, 5, 7, 9, 11, 13, 15],
 
'max_features': [3, 4, 5, 6, 7],
 
'min_samples_leaf': [5, 10, 15, 20, 35, 50]
 
 }
 
Next, instantiate RandomSearchCV  and supply to it the estimator , the
hyper -parameter grid, a 4-fold cross-validation scheme using the code
below . The syntax for RandomizedSearchCV  is very close to that of
GridSearchCV :
 
rf_randoms = RandomizedSearchCV(estimator=rf,
 
param_distributions=hyper_params,
 
cv=4,
 
n_iter=10,
 
verbose=1,
 
n_jobs=-1)
  
Note: In the preceding example, we speciﬁed values by manually creating
lists. It is also possible to provide ranges and even distributions. For
example, you could provide a uniform distribution using scipy .stats (or
any other distribution). For continuous hyper -parameters, using
distributions allows you to search for more possible values.
  
Notice that we have param_distributions for RandomizedSearchCV  where
we had For GridSearchCV . As mentioned, this is because you can also
supply distributions instead of a list of values. Also note the value of
`n_iter`. This tells RandomizedSearchCV  to try not more than 10
combinations. Of course, this is something you can change. But to begin,
let us use this small value and see what outcome we get. Let us tune these
hyper -parameters, the same way we did earlier , using the ‘ﬁt’ method. Use
%%time cell magic to record the execution time.
 
%%time
 
rf_randoms.ﬁt(X_train, y_train)
 
The output should be as below:
 
 
Figure Output of RandomizedSearch
 
You can see that we are trying 10 candidates - as expected because we
speciﬁed n_iter as 10. But look at the time it took - just 4 seconds!
GridSearchCV  took about 140 seconds. The difference in execution time
should not be very surprising because we’re trying very few combinations
in comparison to GridSearch (210). We got the speed we wanted. But is
the result any good and is the best hyper -parameter set close? Let us ﬁnd
out by extracting the best estimator and its accuracy:
 
print(rf_grids.best_estimator_)
 
print(rf_grids.best_score_)
 
The output you get is as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Best estimator and score
 
Compare this with the best estimator and accuracy from Grid Search. You
will see that it is the same result. We got the same result in a fraction of
the time! Did we simply get lucky? Perhaps. But this raises an important
question - which of the two approaches performs better , or how do we
choose between them? We will answer this in the next section.
Grid Search versus Randomized Search
 
Grid Search and Randomized Search are very different approaches and
comparing them boils down to comparing the merits of one approach over
the other - exhaustive search versus randomized search.
 
We reiterate that each method has its advantages, but you also need to
know about and beneﬁt from the extensive research that has been done on
this topic.
 
In 2012, Bergstra and Bengio extensively studied the performance of both
methods on various datasets. They also employed various algorithms
ranging from extremely simple to complex algorithms like Deep Belief
Nets. They showed that Random Search performs as well as Grid Search
in most cases, even beating it in some cases. This may seem
counterintuitive at ﬁrst, because we have good reasons to be pragmatic,
such as:
 
•Grid Search is thorough, while Random Search is not.
 
•Random Search is much faster , we expect to lose some performance to
achieve this speed.
 
It turns out, there are a few reasons for the good performance on Random
Search.
 
The ﬁrst is that in real-world situations, especially with a high number of
predictors and a fair volume of data - there exist multiple local minima.
Meaning that there isn’t necessarily a unique, golden solution that
outperforms all the rest. Rather , many good solutions are close to each
other in performance. With the random sampling that Random Search
does, with enough number of iterations, there is a good chance that you
encounter one of these ‘good’  solutions.
 
The second reason is that not all hyper -parameters are equally important
to tune. Grid Search was very exhaustive in evaluating all the
combinations. Many of these combinations were simply wasteful because
they were variations on unimportant hyper -parameters. So, even if
Random Search searches fewer combinations, it doesn’ t necessarily lose
many valuable combinations.
 
The third major reason is that Random Search can explore more and do a
ﬁner search. This is especially true when you provide distributions for
continuous hyper -parameters. Grid Search searches ‘on the grid’ and is
therefore limited in what it can try. If as a user you made the search
intervals too granular , Grid Search would not perform well. Consider
Figure which is an example from the 2012 paper by Bergstra and Bengio.
The example beautifully illustrates this. In the data, the performance is
extremely sensitive to the important hyper -parameter in a small interval
where the true maximum lies. The Grid Search intervals could be broad
and miss this. Randomized Search, on the other hand, is more exploratory
and has a higher chance of discovering the true maxima because it is not
forced to follow the wide, rigid intervals. This is illustrated in Figure
 
 
Figure Grid Search vs. Random Search
Choosing a hyper -parameter search approach
 
We have seen different approaches and it is natural to ask - how do we
choose? Is there a ‘best method’? Here are some tips and
considerations to help you choose the best approach for your situation.
 
If the dataset is small and the number of hyper -parameters is small,
then you can use either Grid Search or Randomized Search. If the
hyper -parameters to search have all integer values, then the Grid
Search option is the most thorough. But if you have many continuous
hyper -parameters, a Random Search with a higher number of iterations
will make the search more exhaustive.
 
If you are using a computation-heavy algorithm on a large dataset, then
Random Search is a more attractive option. Especially if the number of
hyper -parameters is large. In such a case, Random Search may be the
only feasible option. Even if the algorithm is quick but there are many
hyper -parameters, Grid Search may not be feasible as it may need to
try several combinations for a large dataset.
 
If you want highly customized metrics and want to record information
that is not captured by Random Search or Grid Search, you may want
to manually loop over the hyper -parameters. This option gives you the
most control and may be the only feasible one in speciﬁc situations.
  
Note: Another approach gaining popularity is performing Bayesian
optimization over hyper -parameters. The approach does not perform
an exhaustive search. A ﬁxed number of parameter combinations is
sampled from the speciﬁed distributions. The key difference from
random search is that the search is directed using Bayes Theorem. A
handy implementation is available in scikit-optimize as
BayesSearchCV . Read more about it here: https://scikit-
optimize.github.io/stable/modules/generated/skopt.BayesSearchCV .ht
ml
  
Having stated the above, please bear in mind that these are merely
guidelines and may not address all situations. In such a case,
understand the constraints and requirements of your project and
choose. In case you have the time, it is wise to explore both Grid
Search and Random Search approaches and maybe even use them in
succession. Indeed, another good idea could be to use both - maybe
use Random Search for coarse tuning, followed by Grid Search for
ﬁne-tuning. There are no set rules and you can be creative in ﬁnding
out what works best for you.
  
Pause and ponder: Hyper -parameter tuning is essentially a search
activity and there are various search/optimization algorithms to
quickly ﬁnd the maximum/minimum. Can you think of any search
approach that can be used for hyper -parameter tuning?
Choosing the ‘best’  model
 
In this section, we will discuss the notion of ‘best’. We have already
discussed in Chapter 4, Model Evaluation and that the notion of ‘best’
depends on the context and objective. At the beginning of this chapter , we
presented 3 different situations where the objective and constraints were
different. To understand the ideas and the considerations better , let us
revisit the example where we did manual looping and re-examine
the results:
 
 
Figure Manual looping for Bank marketing example
 
Note that while maximum accuracy was achieved when ‘max_depth’  was
9, the other values were quite close. At depth 6 too, the accuracy was
above 69% (it is 69.4%, to be precise). At this stage, you should think if
the slight gain in accuracy is worth the 3 extra levels of decisions and the
added complexity . Furthermore, if the highest accuracy is not paramount
and interpretation is important, then you should seriously consider
choosing a depth 4 where you have around 68% accuracy and much
higher interpretation (compared to depth 9). In the situation that we started
the chapter off with - where interpretation is important and accuracy needs
to be reasonable, depth 4 would be the preferred choice.
  
Note: It is also possible to use multiple metrics and derive a notion of The
scoring argument of the search method accepts a customer scoring
function. A simple example can be found at: https://scikit-
learn.or g/stable/modules/model_evaluation.html#multimetric-scoring.
  
Let us also revisit the Grid Search results for the Decision Tree that we
had stored in a dataframe (cv_df Instead of looking at all the columns, we
will limit ourselves to the hyper -parameters that we tuned and the
‘mean_test_score’. We will look at the top 20 hyper -parameter
combinations by sorting on the mean_test_score column. Use the
following code:
 
sel_cols = ['param_max_depth',
 
'param_max_features',
 
'param_min_samples_leaf',
 
'mean_test_score']
 
cv_df.sort_values(by='mean_test_score', ascending=False)[sel_cols]\
 
.head(20)\
 
.reset_index(drop=T rue)
 
The best 10 performers get printed as in Figure We can see that all the top
20 models are very close in performance. Let us say that we deﬁne ‘best’
as the model which gives us good performance while being the simplest.
Which of the following hyper -parameter combinations is ‘best’?
 
 
Figure The top 20 models from Grid Search
 
The answer changes. When we looked purely at performance, the ﬁrst set
(index 0) was the best. But with the new deﬁnition of ‘best’  that includes
simplicity as a criterion, the 13th combination (index 12) is far more
appealing.
Training on the entire data and making predictions
 
So far, we used a cross-validation scheme to evaluate the hyper -parameter
candidates. Because of this, we never trained a model on the full training
data. Even ‘best_estimator_’  from Grid Search or Random Search was
based on the cross-validation score. For a 4-fold cross-validation scheme,
we use at most 75% of the training data, using the remaining 25% as
validation. Therefore, our estimates of generalized performance are
derived from models that did not see the entire training data. Why not use
the entire training data?
 
At the end of the hyper -parameter tuning activity , we know the best hyper -
parameter combination. The next step is to use these hyper -parameters and
train the model on the entire training set. This way, the best hyper -
parameter combination gets the most data to train the best model.
 
Let us do this for our example. For simplicity , we will deﬁne best as
‘highest accuracy’. We begin by extracting the best estimator from Grid
Search by using the ‘best_estimator_’  attribute. We then train it on the
entire training dataset. Use the following code:
 
dt_best = model_cv .best_estimator_
 
dt_best.ﬁt(X_train, y_train)
 
You should see the following output which conﬁrms that the model has
been trained:
 
 
Figure Decision Tree has been ﬁt
 
Next, we come to the true test of the model - its performance on an unseen
dataset, that is, the test dataset that we had set apart in the beginning. We
will make predictions using the ‘predict’  method of the Decision Tree
classiﬁer and calculate the accuracy score. Use the following code:
 
y_test_pred = dt_best.predict(X_test)
 
print(accuracy_score(y_test, y_test_pred))
 
The output accuracy is about 68%. This is good and not signiﬁcantly
different from the estimates from the cross-validation score. The
performance of the chosen hyper -parameters is indeed generalizable on
unseen datasets.
The complete process
 
This completes the process of hyper -parameter tuning and using the
best resulting model. A high-level summary of the steps involved is as
below . For a dataset and a modeling algorithm:
 
1. Deﬁne the hyper -parameter combinations you want to evaluate.
 
2. Use any hyper -parameter search approach and evaluate the
candidates.
 
3. Use cross-validation scores with metrics of choice to measure
performance.
 
4. Choose the ‘best’  candidates as per your requirements.
 
5. Train on the entire training data with the best hyper -parameters.
 
6. Evaluate true model performance using the test set.
 
It is important to remember that there are many choices that you, the
Data Scientist, need to make. These could be choices on the search
approach to use or the candidates for a given search approach. Also
important is the deﬁnition of ‘best’  for the resulting models.
Conclusion
 
In getting the best out of the data and modeling algorithm, choosing
the right hyper -parameters is an important step. Hyper -parameters are
parameters that are external to the model - these are not learnt by the
algorithm, but can signiﬁcantly impact how it learns and inﬂuences the
resulting model. We saw three approaches to hyper -parameter tuning,
beginning with the simplest approach that gives you the most control
and ﬂexibility - manually looping over the hyper -parameter candidates.
The Grid Search approach is an improvement over manual looping in
the sense that it provides far more information with minimal code.
 
Both these approaches are exhaustive, evaluating every candidate, and
are therefore ‘dumb’. Random Search randomly selects a pre-speciﬁed
number of candidates for evaluation and can handle continuous hyper -
parameters better . We implemented and contrasted all of these
approaches in a banking case study and also learned from research on
the topic. We are now conversant with hyper -parameter tuning, which
is an extremely important step in optimizing our machine-learning
models,
 
Now that we understand how to optimize how a modelling algorithm
learns, let us turn our attention to a common challenge in supervised
machine learning – imbalanced classes. Chapter 5, Imbalanced
Machine will teach you the issues with imbalanced classes and how to
handle them to optimize the machine learning model and derive
maximum impact from the solution.
