Tuning the number of layers
 
“Go wide or go deep?”, is among the most common questions in
deﬁning the network architecture. How many layers should you have?
Should they have the same number of hidden units? How do you
decide the right combinations?
 
Theoretically , a single hidden layer with enough number of hidden
units can be a universal function approximator . In other words, it
should be able to perform well at any task. But the question is, how
many are ‘enough’? While the answer varies with the situation, you
should not be surprised if the answer is in thousands. Training with
thousands of hidden units does not seem optimal.
 
The other approach is going deep i.e., having more hidden layers. The
beneﬁt is that a layer can work on the transformations in the data made
by the previous hidden layer , and such ‘collaboration’  of the layers can
help create these powerful transformations in data that provide high
performance with fewer neurons. Indeed, this beneﬁt of being able to
get higher performance with fewer units is a key appeal of deep neural
networks. Indeed, most state-of-the-art models are deep networks with
multiple layers.
 
How do you arrive at the right architecture? Most practitioners and
experts say that there are no rules. They arrive at their architectures
mostly by:
 
1. Intuition (through their own experience)
 
2. Inspiration (architecture performing well on similar tasks)
 
3. Trial and error
 
Hyper -parameter tuning, by nature, is trial and error . By now we have
seen that this process can be automated. Therefore, it should be no
surprise that identifying the optimal number of layers in a neural
network can also be tuned. Fortunately , Keras Tuner allows us to tune
the number of layers as well as the number of hidden units in each
layer at the same time:
 
model = Sequential()
 
 
for layer in
 
model.add(
 
Dense(
 
units = +
 
 
))
Optimizer
 
So far, we have been using the Adam optimizer . Adam stands for
Adaptive momentum and is a popular choice of activation function for
a couple of reasons:
 
•It conver ges faster than simple Stochastic Gradient Descent.
 
•It is less sensitive to other sub-optimal hyper -parameters, which
means that it does well even if the other hyper -parameters have not
been set well.
 
Adam is usually the safe ﬁrst choice when building the Deep Learning
model. But Adam is not the only option, as we have discussed in
Chapter 7, Optimization
 
It is important to note that all these methods are heavily based on
gradient descent, and you can think of them as modiﬁcations to the
plain-vanilla gradient descent to conver ge faster . What this means is
that if given enough time and with the right hyper -parameters, all of
these should provide you with very similar results. Buy now we
understand that accuracy isn’t the only concern when optimizing a
model. If accuracy is paramount and training time does not matter
much, Stochastic Gradient Descent is a good option. If faster
conver gence is required, at the cost of some minor loss in accuracy ,
then optimizers like Adagrad, Adam, Nadam, and others should be
tried.
 
Choosing the optimal optimization algorithm, i.e., tuning this hyper -
parameter , also can be performed systematically using the approach we
have been using so far. Keras Tuner can be employed to tune the
optimization algorithm, just like any other hyper -parameter .
 
Task: Continuing with the product category classiﬁcation case study ,
use Keras Tuner to ﬁnd the optimal optimization algorithm for the
model. The model should have the following structure - having 1
single dense layer of 192 units with ReLu activation and 20% dropout.
The candidates for the optimizer are: adam, nadam, and sgd.
Other hyper -parameters
 
We discussed some key parameters that in general make a signiﬁcant
impact on model performance. This does not mean that the other
parameters are irrelevant. It just means that the hyper -parameters we
discussed had a higher impact, or showed more variance in
performance from case to case. Some of the remaining hyper -
parameters can also help boost performance if used correctly .
 
Consider the dropout rate - we used 20% dropout throughout the
chapter so far. It was an arbitrary choice. You could very well try
optimizing the dropout rate as well. A high dropout rate would lead to
high regularization and extremely slow model training. A very low
dropout rate does not regularize and the deep learning model would be
prone to overﬁtting.
 
Batch size is another hyper -parameter that you may want to tune. A
higher batch size considers more data points together before each
update and is therefore faster . A lower batch size therefore increases
the training time. The effect of the batch size on performance,
however , is interesting and mostly empirically understood. Reducing
the batch size has the effect of regularization and helps reduce
overﬁtting and therefore generalize better .
Coarse plus ﬁne tuning
 
We have discussed this approach in detail in Chapter Hyper -parameter
but it is worth recalling the lessons from that chapter . The coarse plus
ﬁne tuning approach refers to the idea of doing coarse tuning ﬁrst to
ﬁnd a good ‘range’  of parameters to work in. Then, do a ﬁne tuning by
trying smaller variations in the hyper -parameters to ﬁnd the optimal
set. We saw in Chapter Hyper -parameter that such an approach is very
practical as it helps us get to the optimal set much quicker .
 
This approach is particularly useful for deep neural networks because
of the sheer number of hyper -parameters to tune. Performing a ﬁne-
grained tuning of all relevant parameters is impractical in most
situations. Using coarse tuning on some key hyper -parameters (e.g.
learning rate, activation function, number of units and layers) can
provide an initial, somewhat optimized setup. This setup is a great
starting point for ﬁne tuning some other hyper -parameters that can help
you get the most out of the data and the deep learning technique. This
is one of our key practical tips for tuning deep neural networks. We
will not share some more practical tips for tuning neural networks.
Practical tips for tuning Neural Networks
 
We have seen multiple times that tuning more hyper -parameters can
help identify the best-performing combination that can easily be
missed when tuning very few or even one at a time. A natural question
is: should we always try to tune all hyper -parameters and all at once?
Especially for deep learning, where we see that there are many hyper -
parameters to set.
 
The answer is a clear no. Especially for Deep Learning models. A
simple practical reason is that training Deep Learning models takes a
lot of time. Trying to tune a dozen hyper -parameters at once, or even in
sequence gets impractical in most situations. Indeed, in many cases,
tuning several hyper -parameters may need several days more of
training time. In many industry applications, there isn’t enough return
on the time invested in this endeavor .
  
Pause and ponder: Can you think of some industry applications where
a 0.1% increase in accuracy/other performance metrics makes a big
difference in the utility and beneﬁt from the model? Hint: think of
situations with low event rates (high class-imbalance) or applications
that impact human lives.
  
The other , more pragmatic reason is the philosophy of parsimony , i.e.,
doing more with less. There is no limit to the complexity of a Deep
Learning model; you can arbitrarily add a layer with a hundred more
units if you want to. But do you need to? Does it give you a signiﬁcant
enough beneﬁt in accuracy? Does it make your model unstable? Does
the more complex model increase inference time? We have discussed
such considerations before and we understand by now that accuracy is
not the only criterion for judging the performance of a model.
 
The general guidance is - to start simple and add complexity as you
This is relevant for the model optimization process as well. Start with
“good defaults” for key hyper -parameters. For example, Adam is
considered a safe ﬁrst option for an optimizer . The learning rate of
0.001 has empirically shown to provide good performance with the
Adam optimizer . Unless you have reason to suspect otherwise, Adam
optimizer with a learning rate of 0.001 is a good starting point.
  
Pause and ponder: What are some other ways of identifying good
defaults or good starting points for the hyper -parameters?
  
Often, there are other indications of what good starting points can be.
One such indication can be a published work on a similar data and
task. This applies to model architectures too. If you are working on a
dataset that has been used in research before, ﬁnd the paper that is
closest to your research goal or task and use the architecture from the
paper as your starting point.
 
In summary , we start simple with good defaults and add complexity
only if needed.
Beyond hyper -parameters: other approaches to boost performance
 
In the previous sections, we learned how to optimize the many hyper -
parameters to obtain the best combination - the most straightforward
approach to improving model performance. But let’s not limit
ourselves to a single approach, when we have some other powerful
approaches that can provide signiﬁcant performance boosts. Let us
learn some more approaches.
Regularization
 
The key usage of regularization is to make the model less prone to
overﬁtting, not ﬁt to the noise in the data and therefore generalize
better to unseen data. We have already used dropout – which is a
strong and direct way of regularization, but not the only way. Another
extremely effective method is modifying the loss function.
 
The most common approach to modifying the loss function is adding a
penalty term to the loss function. These are added in such a way that
the higher the number of parameters and their values, the higher the
penalty . A higher penalty term means the loss is higher . Since model
learning is all about minimizing the loss, a higher penalty term makes
higher values of parameters less desirable. Lower values of parameters
get preferred and therefore the model becomes less prone to
overﬁtting. L1 and L2 penalties are the most common approaches.
 
L1 a penalty term proportional to the magnitude of the weight is added
to the loss
 
L2 a square term of the weight/parameter is added to the loss
 
L2 regularization puts more penalty on higher weights and therefore is
a more aggressive regularization. Whether to use L1 or L2 penalty can
be thought of as another hyper -parameter and can easily be tuned using
Keras Tuner or a similar utility .
 
Beyond dropout and L1/L2 penalties, techniques like Label Smoothing
(adds noise to the target values) and Stochastic depth (that drops entire
blocks in the neural networks) can also be effective techniques to make
the model generalize better .
More data
 
Models are learning patterns from data. However deep neural networks
are unique in the sense that they seem to beneﬁt more from a larger
volume of data. The performance of a classical ML model may not
increase merely by adding more data to it. But for deep learning
models, more data means potentially more ﬁner patterns to learn.
Because they typically have a higher number of parameters, they can
potentially learn ﬁner patterns.
 
So, if you have maxed out on performance through hyper -parameter
tuning and regularization, try to arrange for more training data for the
model. The act of adding more available data may just give you that
required performance boost. Even if you can’t get your hands on new
and unique training data, techniques like Data Augmentation can help
you derive more data from the existing samples. Let us discuss that
next.
Data augmentation
 
The case for data augmentation is most obvious in deep learning for
images. Many times, it may not be feasible to get even a thousand more
images to make your model better . But what if you could derive these
images from existing ones?
 
Picture that you are making a model to classify the image as being of a cat
or a dog. Naturally , you have images of various cats and dogs. But your
dataset is rather small, and the model is overﬁtting very quickly . Also, for
certain, rare breeds of dogs like the Bluetick Coonhound you have just
one picture. More pictures will surely help.
 

 
Figure Image of a Bluetick Coonhound
 
Here’ s where data augmentation can help. Imagine if the image were
ﬂipped horizontally , it would still show a Bluetick Coonhound. Slight
rotation, a little bit of left or right shifting, shifting the image up or down
– none of these will change the subject of the image and it would still be a
good image of the dog. And just like that – from one image, you now have
6 images of the rare breed! This is the basic idea of data augmentation.
 
 
Figure 6 images from 1 via. data augmentation
 
Data augmentation works for text applications as well. For sentiment
classiﬁcation setup, you can increase the data size by adding variations
that do not alter the sentiment. For topic modelling tasks, you can add
variations that don’t change the topic but make other minor changes.
There are many ways for you to get creative. Just be careful and not
accidentally obfuscate the characteristics of the data that are essential for
the task.
 
In case we did not make it explicit enough, data augmentation helps you
get more from your data by –
 
•Creating more variety in the data for the model to learn from; in many
cases, it provides that much-needed variation.
 
•Regularizes and makes the model generalize better .
 
Data augmentation is an easy way of getting more out of your data. We
will discuss this in more detail and perform hands-on data augmentation
in Chapter Image
Ensembles
 
The essence of ensemble learning is using multiple, diverse models on
the same dataset and combining their predictions to create an
ensemble. The ensemble model typically outperforms any of the
individual models. There is no reason why the beneﬁts of ensemble
learning do not apply to deep learning methods. Indeed, with the high
variance and tendency to overﬁt that comes with deep learning models,
creating an ensemble is a great idea to get a higher -performing model
that generalizes better .
 
Of course, considering that training deep learning models on large
datasets, an ensemble consisting of multiple deep learning models may
take much longer . But it could be worth the effort if you can afford it.
Alternatively , you needn’ t restrict your ensemble to deep learning
models alone. For example, a text classiﬁcation model with a deep
learning model, an SVM classiﬁer and an XGBoost model may
signiﬁcantly outperform the individual models. Try ensembling if your
situation permits, it may be well worth the effort and might just give
you that extra edge.
Inject Domain knowledge through features
 
Even though Deep Learning promises effective, automatic feature
generation, adding features based on your knowledge of the data and
the domain is a good idea. Consider a situation where you are working
on text classiﬁcation and are aware of certain patterns that are clear
indicators of a particular class. With a few lines of code, you can create
a new feature that indicates the presence of this text pattern. Now
whenever the model sees the class label and ﬁnds the indicator
correlating strongly with it, the model will use this feature to make the
classiﬁcation more accurate and therefore the model performs better .
 
The knowledge of the domain and the data that you bring in is
invaluable and injecting it will only improve the performance of the
model.
Conclusion
 
Neural networks are extremely powerful and highly ﬂexible in the
architectures and the hyper -parameters. The data scientist must make
therefore several choices carefully to get the best result from the data.
We saw some key hyper -parameters in neural networks: activation
function, learning rate, number of hidden units, number of layers, and
optimizer . We also learned how to tune them using Keras optimizer .
We also saw some practical tips for training neural networks.
 
Additionally , we saw that optimization of neural networks isn’t limited
to hyper -parameter tuning. Techniques like data augmentation and
regularization can help prevent over-ﬁtting. We learnt that creating
ensembles from individual models can help obtain higher performance
at the cost of additional complexity . Finally , we learnt that smart
feature engineering that injects domain knowledge can be a
differentiator in the value of the output.
 
In the next chapter , we will continue learning about optimizing deep
learning models. We will focus on image models; discuss their
peculiarities, and special hyper -parameters and provide practical
know-how on how to get a high-performance image model.
Additional reads

Keras Tuner library , see the following link:
 
https://keras.io/guides/keras_tuner/getting_started/

CHAPTER 9
Optimizing Image Models
Introduction
 
From facial recognition for unlocking your phone to powering self-
driving cars, to object detection for video generation, the applications
of image processing models are everywhere. Well-optimized image
processing models make high-accuracy , real-time predictions in
systems all around us (including your mobile phone) and often surpass
human performance on these tasks. In the past decade, we have
witnessed an increasing number of tasks being automated practically
completely using machine learning/artiﬁcial intelligence. Today’ s data
scientist needs to be versatile in handling all types of data and not be
limited to tabular/ structured data. Images are now a valuable
ubiquitous form of data.
 
It is one thing to cook up a complex, one-time, ofﬂine model as proof
of concept. Creating an optimized, scalable machine learning/ artiﬁcial
intelligence solution that makes near real-time predictions is a
completely different ball game. In the context of image
processing/computer vision, the considerations and requirements are
steep. We are generally dealing with data-hungry , complex models (for
example, a deep learning model with 50 layers) with a lot of predictive
power . However , we seldom have precise data for our task/domain.
Therefore, the optimization of an image processing/ computer vision
solution has additional aspects that we need to be careful about.
  
Note: The concepts and techniques discussed in Chapter 8, Optimizing
Deep Learning Models, all apply to image models as well. The hyper -
parameter tuning techniques and hyper -parameters will be omitted in
this chapter . Instead, we will focus on new aspects of model
optimization that are speciﬁc to image models. We encourage you to
try and apply the techniques discussed in Chapter 8, Optimizing Deep
Learning Models to the models that we will create in this chapter .
  
In this chapter , we will learn all about the aforementioned aspects and
learn how to create optimized solutions. Beginning with the idea of
convolutional ﬁlters, we will see how this simple mathematical
operation helps create powerful features for image processing tasks. It
forms the backbone of most modern image-processing models. To
learn how to build effective and high-performance models using the
basic building blocks of convolutional neural networks, we will study
the design patterns in various popular architectures. We will approach
these neural networks as a combination of a convolutional frontend
and a dense backend. From a simple convolutional neural network to
the popular Visual Geometry Group and the powerful Residual
Network architectures, we will code these networks ourselves to
maximize our practical learning.
 
Since we are usually dealing with complex models and low data sizes,
model generalizability is an important aspect of developing solutions
for image processing tasks. We will see ﬁrst-hand how even a simple
model can overﬁt very quickly . We will then learn about multiple
approaches to tackle overﬁtting. Concretely , we will learn about
various dropout approaches, the power of the extremely useful batch
normalization technique, and the extremely valuable technique of data
augmentation. We will have plenty of hands-on implementation of all
these topics. From all our experiments and learning, we will synthesize
some key guidelines for designing high-performance, optimized
machine learning/ artiﬁcial intelligence solutions for image processing
and computer vision.
Structure
 
In this chapter , we will discuss the following topics:
 
Applications of image processing

Objectives
 
After reading the chapter , you will get an understanding of the
optimization of Deep Learning models for Image processing tasks.
This chapter will begin with concepts, architectures and parameters
that are unique to Image models and then demonstrate how to optimize
these using TensorFlow 2.0. A case study will be used throughout the
chapter for the hands-on. We will study the design of various popular
model approaches and implement our own versions of VGG and
ResNet models. You will also learn how to perform regularization
using various approaches - dropout, data augmentation, and the
powerful batch normalization technique. The chapter ends with some
general guidelines for designing deep learning models for image tasks.
Applications of image processing
 
We listed a few popular applications of image processing in the
Introduction section. Image models are everywhere and images as data are
being increasingly used in various applications. For example, consider the
application extracting image features from product images on an e-
commerce platform for ranking. The attractiveness of the image and
relevance of the item can be a part of the ranking system that ranks better
quality products higher to increase the clicks on search results. Such
systems are used by major e-commerce players and are good examples of
using images as product data. An exhaustive list of such applications is
not possible, especially considering the rapid rate at which we are making
advances in this ﬁeld. We can, however , categorize these applications into
a few broad buckets.
 
An extremely useful application of image processing is object that is,
detecting the content/objects in the image. This could be simply
classifying whether an image is that of a cat or not, or a complex
application identifying the different objects in an image along with their
boundaries for a self-driving car. There are broadly three types of object
detection models, as illustrated in Figure
 
 
Figure Types of object detection
  
Image classiﬁcation: The task is to classify whether the image belongs to
one of the predetermined categories. An example application
automatically identiﬁes the product category from the item pictures users
upload on an online marketplace like eBay . The labels you categorize into
could be a few (for example, cat vs. dog), or even thousands (for example,
the ImageNet challenge).
 
Classiﬁcation with localization: Going a step further from image
classiﬁcation, the task additionally involves providing a location of the
object in the image. The output includes bounding boxes for the identiﬁed
object, along with the probability that the object belongs to a particular
class.
 
Detection: The task is to identify the classes of the different objects
present in the image. For each detected object in the image, the model
classiﬁes whether it belongs to one of the predetermined categories. Then,
for each object, the model identiﬁes its location and outputs bounding
boxes. The popular You Only Look Once model is a good example. It is a
deep learning-based model that divides an image into grids and calculates
the probability of an object in each grid.
 
Image segmentation is an important technique that is widely used in
various applications of image processing. The task is to partition the
digital image into various segments/regions. The objects in the image are
identiﬁed (using labels) and each pixel in the image is assigned a label.
This technique breaks down the image into components. The technique is
used as a part of some of the aforementioned object detection models and
is used as an input to the algorithm to produce object locations. For a self-
driving car, locating any pedestrian is a critical task and image
segmentation forms a vital component of the solution.
  
Note: Image segmentation is a whole sub-ﬁeld in computer vision with a
lot of ongoing research. To build perspective, we recommend reading the
Wikipedia page for image segmentation, which provides a holistic
overview: https://en.wikipedia.or g/wiki/Image_segmentation
  
We brieﬂy look at some other prominent applications of image processing
in the following list. For each application, we encourage you to explore
and look for more examples.
 
Facial veriﬁcation: Whenever you unlock your phone by pointing your
phone’ s camera to your face, you use this technology . The task is to
identify whether the new face (sufﬁciently) matches a reference face.
 
Face recognition: The task is to identify whether the face in the picture
belongs to one of the faces in the database. A face recognition-based
access in a restricted area needs to query the input face against the faces in
the database and grants the user access only if there is a match.
 
Neural style transfer: This technique modiﬁes digital images/videos to
adopt the visual style of another image. With this technique, you could
make your picture look similar to the works of Picasso or Figure 9.2
illustrates the technique, where the image of a dog was modiﬁed to mimic
the style of Wassily
 
 
Figure Neural style transfer in
 
We have looked at various examples of image processing tasks and gained
a high-level perspective. We are now ready to discuss the practical details
and steps to create our own solutions. For this purpose, we will work on a
case study throughout this chapter . Let us understand this case study next.
Case study: Auto image classiﬁcation
 
The retail giant Azra Inc. has its popular platform Re-Fashion. The Re-
Fashion platform is a marketplace for users to buy and sell used items.
The items are mostly listed by individuals. Many of these individuals are
not the most tech-savvy and often make mistakes in listing the item in the
right product category (for example, a pair of shoes accidentally listed as a
dress). Incorrect categorizations are not good for the shoppers’  experience
and impact the sellers’  sales as the item visibility is wasted in the wrong
category .
 
Azra Inc. currently employs an army of manual checkers who review
thousands of listings each week. The reviewers review the image and
verify if the category selected by the user matches the true category . The
manual review process takes signiﬁcant time and costs Azra Inc. a sizable
chunk of its proﬁts. Azra Inc. would like to reduce this cost. This problem
is well suited for a machine learning/artiﬁcial intelligence solution. As the
data scientist on the team, you will be taking up this challenge. Your
solution will be an automatic image classiﬁcation system that identiﬁes
the true category of the product, using its image. Before we start building
the solution, we need to explore and understand the data well.
  
Note: We recommend using GPUs or TPUs if possible, for the code in this
chapter . For Google Colab users, this is simply changing the Runtime
type.

following command will clone the repository with the data and place a

Feature detection using convolutions
 
In this section, we will brieﬂy go over some ideas that are largely speciﬁc
to image processing and are the building blocks of the convolutional
neural network architecture. The ﬁrst key idea is using convolutional
ﬁlters for feature generation. In the simplest view , a convolutional ﬁlter is,
well, a ﬁlter. It indicates whether a certain pattern is present in the
underlying picture. This ﬁlter essentially goes over the image and
compares a pattern against the pixels in the image. If the pattern (a series
of numbers in the matrix) is present, the convolution operation produces a
high value. The value is low if there is no pattern match. This concept is
illustrated in Figure 9.5 that follows, which represents the output from a
single ﬁlter. We typically use multiple convolutional ﬁlters in a
convolutional layer to detect more features:
 
 
Figure A simple convolution layer ’s operation
 
This simple idea is extremely powerful. When we chain multiple
convolution layers, we can expect that the second layer learns
combinations of feature detections from the ﬁrst layer . Thus, the second
layer can potentially learn more complex features. Chaining multiple
layers helps us learn more and more complex features. When used in
conjunction with another important idea - pooling - and applied over
multiple layers, it gives the network the power to potentially detect
complex patterns like entire objects (for example, a nose) in the picture.
 
Let us go over pooling next. A pooling layer is commonly inserted
between successive convolution layers in a CNN. The key purpose of the
pooling operation is to reduce the size of the feature vector . This helps in
reducing the computation needed in the model and helps in controlling
overﬁtting to some extent. Figure 9.6 illustrates a simple max pooling
operation with 2x2 ﬁlters and stride 2:
 
 
Figure MaxPooling with a stride
 
The pooling layer uses the four values in the upper left quadrant and
outputs the maximum value which is 6. Likewise, the maximum value of
each quadrant is retained. If we used an average pooling instead, it would
output the average of the four values in each quadrant. The choice
between average and maximum pooling is a hyperparameter . However ,
max pooling is increasingly becoming the more common choice. It is
important to note the dimensions of the output. A 4x4 input was reduced
to a 2x2 output, giving us dimensionality reduction. Note that we can use
multiple ﬁlters in each pooling layer . The pooling layer operates
independently on every ﬁlter output. This is illustrated in
Figure
 
 
Figure Pooling is downsampling
 
Figure 9.7 shows how the pooling layer retains the depth of the output
from the convolutional layer . The depth of the output from the
convolutional layer equals the number of ﬁlters in the layer . Since the
pooling layer operates on each layer independently , the output from the
pooling layer has the same depth, while signiﬁcantly downsampling along
the height and width dimensions. If done correctly , pooling allows us to
reduce dimensionality without losing signiﬁcant information.
  
Note: To learn more details about the operationalization of these concepts
and the mathematics behind them, you can refer to this excellent tutorial
on the Stanford CS231 website: https://cs231n.github.io/convolutional-
networks/
  
These two ideas and how they work together are key to understanding the
effectiveness and working of convolutional neural networks. Let us now
understand the architecture of the traditional convolutional neural network
to make these ideas more concrete.
Traditional Convolutional Neural Networks
 
A convolutional neural network can be designed using three main types of
layers:
 
Convolutional layer ,
 
Pooling layer , and
 
Dense layer
 
The convolutional layer(s) along with the pooling layers(s) form the
frontend of the network. The dense layer(s) form the back of the layer . In
other words, a convolutional network can be thought of as adding a
convolutional frontend to a deep neural network. The idea is illustrated in
Figure which shows the simplest convolutional neural network:
 
 
Figure Traditional CNN architecture
 
As denoted in Figure the convolutional layers form the frontend. The
purpose of this frontend is to learn powerful features and reduce the size
of the feature vector . When using multiple convolutional layers, the initial
layers learn very coarse features, while subsequent layers learn more and
more ﬁne features. The max pooling layers manage the size without losing
information from the feature maps.
 
The dense layers form the classiﬁcation/prediction backend. There are
typically multiple, dense, fully connected layers. The output from the
convolutional frontend is typically a feature vector that the backend
processes to make predictions. The design is straightforward and easy to
understand and implement. The hugely successful VGG class of models is
a popular example of this simple approach.
 
For our case study , we will build a simple convolutional neural network
for our image classiﬁcation task. We will use a simple architecture with
three convolution layers in the frontend and one dense layer (apart from
the prediction layer) in the backend. The architecture is illustrated in
Figure
 
 
Figure Our ﬁrst, simple CNN
 
Let’s start by creating a variable named num_classes to store the total
number of classes in the dataset using the following command. This will
help avoid manually entering the number of classes each time for further
models:
 
num_classes =
 
Next, let us import the necessary utilities from Keras. We will import the
Sequential model and the layers to deﬁne the architecture. Use the
commands that follow:
 
from keras.models import Sequential
 
from keras.layers import Rescaling, Conv2D, MaxPooling2D, Flatten,
Dense
 
We can now deﬁne the model architecture illustrated in Figure First, let us
add the Rescaling layer . This rescaling layer will ensure all pixel values
are between 0 and 1 and resize all images to the same target size (128 x
128):
 
model_conv = Sequential()
 
input_shape=(img_dim, img_dim,
 
Next, let us deﬁne the CNN frontend. We will use three Conv2D layers
with ﬁlters of size 3. We will use ReLu activation and specify ‘same’
padding. The layers will contain 16, 32 and 64 ﬁlters, respectively . Each
layer will be followed by a MaxPooling2D layer . Use the following code
to deﬁne the frontend:
 
 
model_conv .add(MaxPooling2D())
 
 
model_conv .add(MaxPooling2D())
 
 
model_conv .add(MaxPooling2D())
 
Now let us deﬁne the DNN backend. First, we use the Flatten layer to get
all the features into a single vector . We follow this with a fully connected
dense layer of 64 neurons with ReLu activation. The ﬁnal prediction layer
will be a dense layer of 10 neurons, one for each class label, with a
softmax activation. We will use the code that follows:
 
model_conv .add(Flatten())
 
 
model_conv .add(Dense(num_classes,
 
The frontend and backend are deﬁned; we can now compile the model.
We will use the Adam optimizer , which is usually a good default. We will
use the sparse_categorical_crossentropy loss function and print out the
model summary as shown in the following code:
 
 
 
 
The summary gets printed out as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Summary of the simple Convolutional model
 
The summary shows about 1.07M parameters. Notice that most of these
parameters come from the ﬁrst dense layer . We expect (16,384 + 1) x 64 =
1,048,640 parameters, which is what we get. It should be easy to see that
the 16,384 parameters in the ﬂatten layer are simply the product of the
dimensions of the ﬁnal max pooling layer (16 x 16 x 64).
 
Now , we can ﬁt the model on the training data generator and supply the
validation data generator for validation scoring. We will train for 10
epochs. Use the following code:
 
 
history = model_conv .ﬁt(train_ds_gen,
 
validation_data=val_ds_gen,
 
epochs=epochs)
 
The (truncated) output should look like Figure.
 
 
Figure. Truncated output from the training
  
Note: Due to randomization, the values you observe might slightly differ
from ours. The pattern, however , should be consistent.
  
Notice that model accuracies on the training and the validation data are
very different - 97% vs. 66%. This seems to be true for the last few
epochs. To study this diver gence better , we will plot the learning curves -
the accuracy of the training and validation sets for all the epochs. Use the
following code for this purpose:
 
train_acc =
 
val_acc =
 
x =
 
plt.plot(x, train_acc,
 
plt.plot(x, val_acc,
 
plt.legend();
 
plt.show()
 
Alternatively , we can deﬁne the following function for reusability and call
it using the following code:
 
def
 
train_acc =
 
val_acc =
 
x =
 
plt.plot(x, train_acc,
 
plt.plot(x, val_acc,
 
plt.legend()
 
plt.show()
 
Using the function, we can plot the model performance using the
following single-line command. The output must be the same as using the
entire code:
 
plot_perf(history , epochs)
 
The output plot should look like Figure 9.12:
 
 
Figure The Convnet overﬁts in a few epochs
 
We can see that this is a textbook case of overﬁtting. The training
accuracy crosses the validation accuracy very quickly and keeps
increasing while the validation accuracy remains stable at around 60%.
This indicates that the model is not learning any generalized pattern but is
memorizing the training data.
 
Indeed, overﬁtting is a big issue for convolutional networks with multiple
layers and high parameter counts. Avoiding overﬁtting is therefore an
important aspect of building convolutional neural networks. Let us now
learn and implement some ways of avoiding overﬁtting and improving the
general performance of the model.
Parameter regularization
 
We have already discussed adjusting the cost function (L1 or L2
penalty) in Chapter 5, Imbalanced Machine Learning and Chapter 6,
Hyper -parameter We have been using regular , dense dropouts in the
chapters so far. Now , we will learn about a version of dropout more
suited for convolutional neural networks. After that, we will learn
about and implement batch normalization, an extremely useful
technique for building deep neural networks.
Spatial Dropout
 
Using a dropout is a very direct way of regularization that is extremely
effective. In addition to the regular drop where some connections are
simply dropped, there are many interesting approaches developed in
the past few years. SpatialDropout2D is an example that is very useful
for models with convolutions. It drops out entire feature maps instead
of just a single weight, as illustrated in Figure
 
 
Figure. 9.13: Regular (left) vs spatial (right) dropout
 
Spatial dropout is a commonly used technique for convolutional neural
networks. The dropout layer is commonly added right after a pooling
layer . We will use spatial dropout in this chapter . However , there are
some other interesting dropout techniques, not limited to those for
CNNs, worth exploring:
 
Gaussian dropout (injects noise to weights),
 
DropConnect (zeros random weights during each forward pass),
 
DropBlock (drops entire blocks in CNNs),
 
Variational dropout (for recurrent networks, uses the same mask for
each time step)
 
Attention dropout (for transformers/attention models, drops attention
units)
  
Note: Details about the dropout methods listed earlier in their original
papers and code can be found at:
https://paperswithcode.com/methods/category/regularization
  
These were a few prominent examples of approaches to dropout. We
encourage you to explore and learn more about these approaches. Let
us now see dropout in action and assess its impact on the image
classiﬁcation case study for Azra Inc.
Exercise 9.1: Convolution model with dropout
 
In this activity , we will use dropout to regularize our image model. We
need two types of dropouts viz. Dropout and SpatialDropout2D. Dropout
is the regular method we use for dense layers. SpatialDropout will be used
to mask entire feature maps. We will use the spatial dropout layer after
every pooling layer and the regular dropout after the ﬂattening layer . Our
model architecture will otherwise be the same as the previous model. Let
us begin creating our model:
 
Import the dropout layers from Keras using the following command:
 
from keras.layers import Dropout, SpatialDropout2D
 
Instantiate a model, add the rescaling layer and the Conv2D layer ,
followed by MaxPooling2D. Add a SpatialDropout2D layer with 50%

model_dropout = Sequential()
 
input_shape=(img_dim, img_dim,
 
 
model_dropout.add(MaxPooling2D())
 
 
To complete the frontend, add spatial dropout (50%) after the convolution
layer only. Use the code that follows:
 
 
model_dropout.add(MaxPooling2D())
 
 
 
model_dropout.add(MaxPooling2D())
 
Add a regular dropout (50%) after adding the ﬂatten later. Add the dense
layers and compile the model using the Adam optimizer and sparse
categorical cross entropy as the loss function, as shown in the following
code:
 
model_dropout.add(Flatten())
 
model_dropout.add(Dropout(0.5))
 
model_dropout.add(Dense(64, activation='relu'))
 
model_dropout.add(Dense(num_classes, activation='softmax'))

We see that both training and validation accuracies are close together and
growing steadily . The training accuracy is very similar to the validation
accuracy , indicating that the model is not overﬁtting. This exercise clearly
demonstrates the power of dropout in managing overﬁtting. Dropout is not
the only tool in our arsenal, though. Let us now look at another very
important technique in the deep learning world - batch normalization.
Batch normalization
 
Another important and indirect way of regularization is batch
normalization. It is important to note that batch normalization was
originally developed to address the problem of Internal Covariate Shift
that occurs in deep neural networks. The issue, brieﬂy , is that ReLu is
not zero-centered due to which the input distribution shifts over time.
As a result, the deep neural network learns very slowly . Batch
normalization was developed to address this issue and thus make the
model learn faster .
 
This is how the batch normalization technique works:
 
Simply normalize the weights within the network for each batch. At
the input of the layer , the mean and standard deviation of the batch are
measured. The activations of the input are then normalized using them.
As a result, we have zero mean and unit standard deviation for each
batch. This is done during the training process. After the training of the
model is complete, to make predictions, the mean and standard
deviation of the entire training data are used.
  
Note: For details on Internal Covariate Shift and to understand exactly
how batch normalization helps in handling it, refer to the 2015 paper
on batch normalization here: https://arxiv .org/pdf/1502.03167.pdf
  
The primary intent of it was to address the Internal Covariate Shift
issue to speed up training. However , researchers observed that batch
normalization also helps in regularization, helping the model
generalize better . A recent paper explains the phenomenon
theoretically and is a recommended read for those interested in the
mechanism. You can ﬁnd the paper at: arxiv .org/pdf/1809.00846.pdf
 
Let us use batch normalization in our case study and assess if we
observe any regularization. Note that we are not using it for faster
conver gence. However , we strongly encourage you to use batch
normalization for faster conver gence. You might want to use it in
conjunction with other regularization techniques and try tuning the
learning rate. Let us begin.
 
We will need the BatchNormalization layer and the ReLu layer . We
will apply the batch normalization right after applying the
convolutions and before the non-linearity from the ReLu activation
kicks in. For this purpose, we need the activation applied as a separate
ReLu layer . We will not use any dropout in this exercise and the model
architecture otherwise will be the same as in the previous exercises.
Let us start by importing the additional layers from Keras using the
command that follows:
 
from keras.layers import BatchNormalization, ReLU
 
Start deﬁning the frontend by adding the rescaling layer and the
Conv2D layer as before. Then, add the BatchNormalization layer ,
followed by the ReLu layer and the MaxPooling2D using the
following code:
 
model_norm = Sequential()
 
model_norm.add(Rescaling(1./255, input_shape=(img_dim, img_dim,
3)))
 
model_norm.add(Conv2D(16, 3, padding='same'))
 
model_norm.add(BatchNormalization())
 
model_norm.add(ReLU())
 
model_norm.add(MaxPooling2D())
 
Similarly , we add the remaining convolutional layers along with the
batch normalization, ReLu activation and max pooling layers. Use the
following code:
 
model_norm.add(Conv2D(32, 3, padding='same'))
 
model_norm.add(BatchNormalization())
 
model_norm.add(ReLU())
 
model_norm.add(MaxPooling2D())
 
model_norm.add(Conv2D(64, 3, padding='same'))
 
model_norm.add(BatchNormalization())
 
model_norm.add(ReLU())
 
model_norm.add(MaxPooling2D())
 
The frontend deﬁnition is now complete. Let us now deﬁne the DNN
backend. We use the same backend as in the previous exercise.
 
model_norm.add(Flatten())
 
model_norm.add(Dense(64, activation='relu'))
 
model_norm.add(Dense(num_classes, activation='softmax'))
 
Compile the model, train for 10 epochs and plot the learning curves.
Use the following code:
 
model_norm.compile(optimizer='adam',
 
loss='sparse_categorical_crossentropy',
 
metrics=['accuracy'])
 
epochs=10
 
history = model_norm.ﬁt(
 
train_ds_gen,
 
validation_data=val_ds_gen,
 
epochs=epochs
 
)
 
plot_perf(history , 10)
 
The learning curves are obtained as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure. Batch normalization helps with regularization
 
It is important to note that batch normalization is not purely/explicitly
a regularization method. Rather , the technique’ s regularization effect
was an observation by researchers and practitioners. Batch
normalization was intended to make the model learn faster . From
Figure 9.16, we can see that the regularization effect exists, but is not
as strong as dropout.
  
Pause and ponder: We see that there is a gap between the training and
validation accuracy that appears across epochs. This behavior is not
surprising. Can you explain this behavior?
   
Hint: Think about normalization during prediction.
  
Additionally , we see that the model is learning faster than the dropout
model and there is still some difference between the training and
validation accuracy . In practice, we should use batch normalization in
conjunction with other explicit regularization methods like dropout
and data augmentation. We have already learned and used the dropout
technique. Let us now understand and implement an extremely
powerful technique for computer vision tasks, that is, data
augmentation.
Data augmentation
 
Data augmentation artiﬁcially enlar ges the dataset. This is done by
generating new images from the existing ones by applying
transformations to them. Modiﬁed copies of the original image are then
used as additional examples for the model to learn from. Consider the task
of classiﬁcation of an image as a dog or a cat. On rotation, the image still
clearly represents a cat and does not change the label, as illustrated in
Figure
 
 
Figure The label does not change on image transformation
 
However , we should be extremely careful in choosing the transformation
for data augmentation. The label should be invariant to the
transformations. In simpler terms, the transformation should not affect the
class in the label. Common transformations may lead to problems in
speciﬁc situations. Consider the example in Figure. In a digit recognition
task, the rotation transformation can cause the label to change. 9 could
become 6 and vice versa. Therefore, we must carefully review the
transformations and ensure that the label is invariant to all of them.
 
 
Figure An example where the label is not invariant to rotation
 
An exhaustive list of transformations is not possible as different situations
necessitate different transformations. Some of the most popular
transformations are illustrated in Figure For most tasks, ﬂipping, rotation,
random crop and contrast change are safer options and are types of
random spatial transformation. Noise addition, color shift and information
loss are types of pixel transformation.
 
 
Figure Common transformations for data
 
We encourage you to explore some other transformation approaches,
beyond the pixel transformation approaches we just learnt about. Some
notable and interesting approaches are as follows:
 
Feature space augmentation
 
GAN-based augmentation
 
Meta-learning
 
Let us now implement data augmentation on our own to see the kind of
output these transformations can produce. Let us also see how convenient
using data augmentation is with Keras. Most of the commonly used
transformations are readily available as Keras layers. We will use a few
different transformations from Keras. Let us start by importing the layers
module from Keras:
 
from keras import layers
 
Next, we deﬁne a series/sequence of layers to perform various
augmentations. This sequence can be used as a layer itself in any Keras
model, as we will see in the next exercise. The transformations we will
use are - random ﬂip, random rotation, random zoom, random contrast,
and Gaussian noise addition. Use the following code to deﬁne the
augmentation layer:
 
data_augmentation = keras.Sequential(
 
[
 
layers.RandomFlip("horizontal_and_vertical",
 
input_shape=(img_dim, img_dim, 3)),
 
layers.RandomRotation(0.2),
 
layers.RandomZoom(0.3),
 
layers.RandomContrast(0.3),
 
layers.GaussianNoise(0.2)
 
])
 
Once deﬁned, this layer can be applied directly to a batch of images as
well. Let us take an image from the training data generator and apply
various transformations to it. Let us then plot 9 different transformation
combinations on the image. Use the following code:
 
for images, _ in
 
for i in
 
augmented_images = data_augmentation(images)
 
ax = i +
 
 
 
The output is shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Data augmentation derived nine images from one
 
Note from Figure 9.20 that a combination of just a few transformations
gave us numerous variations of a single input image. This is extremely
useful in situations where we have little data, or, for class imbalance
situations where we have few examples of a particular class. We
encourage you to try out more transformations that are available as Keras
layers. Now that we understand how augmentation works and how to
employ it using Keras, let us apply augmentation to our image
classiﬁcation case study for Azra Inc.

This completes our discussion on regularization. We discussed three
diverse approaches for this task and saw the standalone performance of
each approach. We saw that these are simple to implement and can
signiﬁcantly improve model generalizability and help tackle
overﬁtting. Now , let us continue our discussion on another important
aspect of designing our neural network - the architecture.
Popular architectures
 
So far, the architecture we used in the case study was rather arbitrary .
A natural question at this point would be - how do we choose this
architecture? To make good decisions about the architecture, we need
to ﬁrst appreciate that the architecture is a system design choice. An
understanding of some common design patterns employed in common
architectures can be extremely useful for developing your own
perspective on design.
 
Several architectures in the ﬁeld of Convolutional Networks have
gained popularity and spurred research. Some of them are as follows.
These gained popularity by top performance in the ImageNet Large
Scale Visual Recognition Challenge or simply the ImageNet challenge.
  
Note: We will skip some of the older architectures like LeNet and
AlexNet
   
This model won the ImageNet challenge in 2014. The latest Inception
models are successors of the GoogLeNet model. This was the ﬁrst
model to develop the inception model that drastically reduced the
number of parameters in the network. A signiﬁcant feature of the
model was the usage of average pooling at the end of the convolutional
frontend. This helped reduce the number of parameters to about 4M.
By contrast, other competing SOTA models had far more parameters
(AlexNet had 60M+, and VGGNet had about 140M).
 
The model was the runner -up in the challenge in 2014. It was a
relatively deep model, consisting of 16 layers. The model was
extremely simple otherwise. The success of this model demonstrated
the importance of depth in neural networks for performance. We will
learn about this in more detail in the section VGG design VGGNet,
however , had a high number of parameters (140M) and was therefore
considered computationally very expensive then.
 
The 2015 challenge was won by ResNet. The key idea of ResNet was
to use special ‘shortcuts’  in the network that allowed for data to
propagate more freely to subsequent layers. The model also heavily
used batch normalization. ResNet models and their derivations
remained state-of-the-art for a long time. We will learn more about it
in detail and create our own ResNet model.
 
Other than these, many more popular architectures have performed
extremely well on image-processing tasks. It is recommended that you
look up the latest SOTA models and try to understand their working as
well. We encourage you to do so with an emphasis on understanding
the design approaches employed by them to help build your own
perspective on network architecture. For now, let us understand the
VGG model and its design better .
VGG design approach
 
The VGGNet model was an extremely popular SOTA model that was the
runner -up of the 2014 ILSVRC competition. The model’ s notable
contribution was that it showed how important the depth of the
architecture is for high performance. The model was rather simplistic
otherwise, using small receptive ﬁelds and equal-sized convolutional
layers in small blocks. The architecture is illustrated in Figure.
 
 
Figure. The VGG16 model architecture
 
As we can see in Figure the salient features of the VGG architecture are:
 
It uses equal-sized convolutional layers in a block
 
It doubles the number of ﬁlters in subsequent blocks (64, 128, 256 and
then 512).
 
It uses pooling at the end of a convolutional block.
 
It uses a small receptive ﬁeld (3x3).
 
Taking a simpliﬁed, frontend vs. backend perspective, the original VGG16
architecture can be represented by Figure. As you can see, the number of
ﬁlters in each convolutional group doubles from 64 to 512:
 
 
Figure CNN Frontend and DNN backend in the VGG16 architecture
  
Note: We will learn how to use the original VGG16 model, trained on the
ImageNet database, in Chapter 11, Transfer
  
The original VGG architecture was trained on the ImageNet database,
consisting of millions of images belonging to 1,000 categories. We,
however , have considerably fewer training images (just 3,068).
Regardless, let us now learn how to code up our VGG16.
 
We will need to add about 13 convolutional layers, which need a lot of
repetition. To make our code cleaner , we will use a procedure/function to
create a convolutional block with a speciﬁc number of convolutional
layers and ﬁlters. The ﬁlter size will be 3 x 3 in all the layers. The
following code accomplishes this:
 
def num_ﬁlters):
 
for n in
 
model_vgg.add(Conv2D(num_ﬁlters,
 
 
 
 
Note that the max pooling layer is added only at the end of the
convolutional block. Also, notice that we have already speciﬁed the name
of the model object as in the function. This is the name of our version of
the VGG16 model that we will create in the following exercise.

now learn another extremely interesting approach to image model
architectures - the Residual Network
ResNet design approach
 
The VGG16 architecture showed that higher depth can signiﬁcantly
improve model performance. High depth, however , leads to issues that we
have discussed due to which the model does not learn and the
performance degrades. The ResNet architecture was designed to
overcome this problem and to ﬁnd a way for us to go deeper without
performance degradation. The ResNet architecture was successful at this
objective. The key element of the ResNet model is the identity link, also
known as a shortcut. The idea is that the next layer sees not only the
convolutional output from the present layer but also the input data, as
shown in Figure
 
 
Figure 9.27: Learning residuals
 
As shown in Figure the identity link is a simple concept. The two weight
layers represent two convolutional layers and the entire structure in the
preceding ﬁgure can be seen as a residual The input data x is not only
supplied to the convolutional layers but is also copied and added (without
any modiﬁcation) to the output of the second convolutional layer . The last
step in Figure 9.27 denotes the operation to ‘link’  these values. The
‘linking’  operation is, essentially , simply adding the input x and the output
from the second convolutional layer . This simple idea helps because now
the subsequent block uses not only the previous block’ s output, but also
the input, and can choose to propagate it if it helps the model
performance. This simple trick can help us go deeper without losing
relevant information.
 
To make the concept of the identity link more concrete, let us create our
own residual block with the identity link/ shortcut. Note that the following
code will use the Keras functional API. The functional API allows us to
create models with non-linear topology (like our identity links). For more
details and a crash course, refer to: keras.io/guides/functional_api/
 
x_shortcut = x
 
x = Conv2D(64, (3,3), strides=(1,1), padding='same')(x)
 
x = ReLu()(x)
 
x = Conv2D(64, (3,3), strides=(1,1), padding='same')(x)
 
x = ReLu()(x)
 
x = layers.add([x_shortcut, x])
 
The block contains two regular convolutional layers with the same
number of ﬁlters of the same size. This is an important characteristic of
the ResNet architecture. The ﬁnal command in the preceding code uses an
‘add’  layer to simply add the input (to the block) to the output of the
second convolutional layer . This is the part where we effectively create
the link. The ResNet architecture also contains regular convolutional
blocks. The key difference is the absence of the link. Now that the idea of
the identity link and the various blocks in the architecture is clear , let us
take a look at the full architecture of the ResNet34 model, as shown in
Figure
 
 
Figure The ResNet34 architecture with links
 
The architecture in Figure 9.28 may look complex but is rather
straightforward to understand. The blocks with the solid connections
between the input and the output represent residual blocks. The blocks
with the dotted connections represent a regular convolutional block. As
we can see from Figure the architecture has a pattern where it has a
regular convolutional block, followed by multiple residual blocks of the
same layer size. This brings us to the salient features of the ResNet model,
which are as follows:
 
It uses ‘shortcuts’  to allow free propagation of data to subsequent layers.
 
It uses the same number of convolutional layers in all the blocks.
 
It heavily uses batch normalization.
 
It uses a global average pooling at the ﬁnal pooling stage (which
signiﬁcantly reduces dimensionality).
 
It used only a single dense layer for the backend.
  
Note: The subsequent variations of the ResNet architecture brought in
some more techniques (for example, bottleneck). The key ideas, however ,
were retained.
  
To complete our learning of the ResNet architecture, let us now code our
own ResNet34 model from scratch. For better reusability , we deﬁne
functions for creating the residual and the regular convolutional block
with a speciﬁc number of ﬁlters. The following code creates the residual
block:
 
def block_residual(num_ﬁlters, x):
 
x_shortcut = x
 
x = Conv2D(num_ﬁlters, (3,3), strides=(1,1), \
 
padding='same', activation = 'relu')(x)
 
x = Conv2D(num_ﬁlters, (3,3), strides=(1,1), \
 
padding='same', activation = 'relu')(x)
 
x = add([x_shortcut, x])
 
return x
 
We also need a function for the regular convolutional block. The only
difference from the previous function is the absence of the link. The
following code deﬁnes the function:
 
def block_conv(num_ﬁlters, x):
 
x = Conv2D(num_ﬁlters, (3,3), strides=(1,1), \
 
padding='same', activation = 'relu')(x)
 
x = Conv2D(num_ﬁlters, (3,3), strides=(1,1), \
 
padding='same', activation = 'relu')(x)
 
return x
 
With the required deﬁnitions in place, we can start deﬁning the model
architecture. First, we import the necessary utilities from Keras:
 
from keras.layers import Conv2D, MaxPool2D, Dense,
GlobalA veragePooling2D
 
from keras.layers import add, Input
 
from keras import Model
 
We deﬁne the inputs using the Input layer using the following command:
 
inputs = Input(shape=(img_dim, img_dim, 3))
 
Let us now deﬁne the ﬁrst convolutional layer and the max pooling layer
that follows it:
 
x = \
 
 
x =
 
Now , we can use the block_residual function to deﬁne residual blocks to
add three residual blocks of 64 ﬁlters each using the following code:
 
x = block_residual(64, x)
 
x = block_residual(64, x)
 
x = block_residual(64, x)
 
Now use the block_conv function to deﬁne one regular convolutional
block with 128 ﬁlters, using the following commands:
 
x = x)
 
Now add three residual blocks (128 ﬁlters each), 1 convolutional block
(256 ﬁlters), 5 residual blocks (256 ﬁlters), followed by 1 convolutional
block (512 ﬁlters) and ﬁnally 2 residual blocks (512 ﬁlters). Use the
following code:
 
x = block_residual(128, x)
 
x = block_residual(128, x)
 
x = block_residual(128, x)
 
x = block_conv(256, x)
 
x = block_residual(256, x)
 
x = block_residual(256, x)
 
x = block_residual(256, x)
 
x = block_residual(256, x)
 
x = block_residual(256, x)
 
x = block_conv(512, x)
 
x = block_residual(512, x)
 
The convolutional frontend is now complete. We now deﬁne the DNN
backend. Add a GlobalA veragePooling2D layer , followed by a single
dense layer with 10 neurons for prediction and complete the model
deﬁnition:
 
x = GlobalA veragePooling2D()(x)
 
outputs = Dense(num_classes, activation = 'softmax')(x)
 
resnet = Model(inputs, outputs)
 
resnet.summary()
 
The (truncated) output is shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Truncated summary of our ResNet model
 
We can visualize the full model using visualkeras using the following
command:
 
visualkeras.layered_view(resnet, \
 
type_ignore=[Flatten, Dense, Input])
 
You should obtain the output as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Our ResNet34 model visualized
 
Figure 9.30 shows that our model is quite deep, consisting of 33
convolutional layers. It is important to recall that a performant model at
this depth was made feasible largely because of the clever trick of the
identity link. Subsequent variants of the ResNet model used more tricks to
optimize the models even further (ResNet50 v2 is a good example to learn
more about) while retaining this powerful core idea. Let us now
summarize our learning from studying these architectures into some
concrete guidelines that you can use in practice.
Guidelines for designing image models
 
We have discussed and implemented many ideas in this chapter . After
understanding the traditional approach, the VGG approach and the
ResNet approach, and the hands-on learning we did can synthesize a
few guidelines for designing high-performance models. The guidelines
are as follows:
 
Doubling convolutions (inspired by VGG16) in each block followed
by MaxPooling helps in feature reduction.
 
For deeper networks, residual networks using identity links help the
model overcome various difﬁculties like vanishing/ exploding
gradients.
 
Regularization is extremely important. Use the techniques in
conjunction to get good results. Data augmentation is extremely useful.
 
Using batch normalization helps go deeper and with faster
conver gence. It also has the effect of regularization.
 
GlobalA veragePooling is an effective pooling operation, worth
considering for the ﬁnal pooling operation.
 
In practice, we need to be aware that each situation and task is
different and that the understanding of the data and task at hand is
critical. The wise data scientist uses their experience and domain
understanding to augment any general guidelines. This brings us to the
end of the topic.
Conclusion
 
The high performance of image processing models comes largely from
a simple mathematical operation - convolution. Convolutional ﬁlters
are used to detect features. In progressive convolutional layers, the
features learnt are increasingly sophisticated. Using convolutional
layers, and pooling layers and as building blocks, we can build an
excellent feature generation frontend of the convolutional network. A
block of dense layers can serve as the predictive backend of the
network. This frontend vs. backend view enhances our understanding
and thus design of networks.
 
Regularization is an important step in developing deep learning models
for images. We saw that even the simplest of models can overﬁt
quickly if regularization is not used. We learnt three different
techniques for implementing it. First, we saw how simple and effective
it is to use dropout (both dense and spatial dropout). We then learned
about the extremely useful batch normalization technique. It is not
explicitly a regularization technique, but helps models learn better and
has a regularization effect. Data augmentation was another useful
technique that helped generate several images from a single reference
and helped with training data-hungry models.
 
To make the design of our own deep learning model architectures
better , we looked at some prominent architectures. We saw that VGG
was a relatively simple approach that used repeatable convolutional
blocks and achieved high performance by going deep. This approach
of simply going deep, however , had limitations. We then saw how
residual networks helped overcome these limitations by introducing
feature reuse and identity links. The approach showed a way to build
deeper models with higher performance. After all the learning and
application, we looked at some guidelines for developing our own
models.
 
With that, we have learnt how to develop high-performance image
models from scratch. However , this is not the only approach to solving
image problems. Sometimes, developing models from scratch may not
be feasible and we need to leverage existing models/ learning. This
will be the focus of Chapter 11, Transfer where you will learn how to
use SOTA image models for your task. Let us continue our quest for
mastering machine learning/ artiﬁcial intelligence models. We now
look at techniques for handling another extremely common type of
unstructured data, text, in the next chapter , Chapter 10, Optimizing
Text

CHAPTER 10
Optimizing Natural Language Processing Models
Introduction
 
Natural language data is everywhere. For a regular person, speech and
text are the predominant forms of communication and information
consumption. Machine learning/artiﬁcial intelligence solutions,
therefore, are practically everywhere as well. The spam classiﬁcation
model protects us from malicious e-mails. The news recommendation
models consider the content and our preferences to rank the most
relevant articles. The virtual assistant (Siri/Google) understands our
commands to compose and send an email on your behalf. Your favorite
translation app instantly transcribes and translates speech to text in
another language as we speak. We are surrounded by machine
learning/artiﬁcial intelligence models for Natural Language Processing
 
ChatGPT  by OpenAI is by far the most popular chatbot and one of the
most successful artiﬁcial intelligence products ever. It reached 100
million users in just two months of launch. It is extremely impressive
and its natural language capabilities have amazed even practitioners
and researchers. The godfather of deep learning, Geof frey stated that
progress in this ﬁeld is much faster than expected. ChatGPT  has
kicked off the Large Language Models race where all major
organizations are rushing to leverage this powerful technology .
 
Now , NLP is of course at the core of this rapid advancement in
artiﬁcial intelligence. NLP is by no means a new ﬁeld. Indeed,
practitioners today beneﬁt from linguistics work done in the 1930s.
What changed in the past few years is the access to data, better
computing and some innovative new models that have easily exceeded
all past benchmarks. In this chapter , we learn about some keys to
success in machine learning/artiﬁcial intelligence for NLP. We will
learn the various considerations for handling text, learn about novel
architectures for text processing, and learn how to optimize these
models.

Objectives
 
In this chapter , we will learn how to optimize NLP models while
handling the peculiarities of natural language data. Beginning with text
pre-processing, we will further learn about text representation through
embeddings and how we can create our embeddings. We will learn the
two major approaches and their corresponding architectures to
processing sequences, that are, recurrent architectures and transformer
architecture. We will also learn hyper -parameter tuning for any chosen
architecture to get the most out of the model. We will learn some more
tricks to optimize our NLP model, for example, using 1D convolutions
and using pre-trained embeddings. We will work on a hands-on case
study to practice all these topics. By the end of the chapter , you will
have all the know-how to optimize a modern NLP model.
Key tasks in NLP
 
NLP is concerned with helping machines make sense of natural
(human) language – speech and text. NLP is not one big task, but an
umbrella term for numerous smaller tasks. Machine learning/artiﬁcial
intelligence models often specialize in a task and larger solutions
combine these individual models. It is therefore important to
understand the types of tasks in NLP. Let us look at a few salient NLP
tasks:
 
Text Provide a crisp summary of a long text without losing the salient/
key points contained in the text. An example is summarizing news
articles, a thousand-word news article can be shortened into a, say,
100-word summary . This is indeed a popular application of text
summarization.
 
Question Based on the input data provided, the model provides
answers to the questions the user provides (as text, usually). The
training data could also be a large open corpus (for example, the entire
Wikipedia online encyclopedia), or maybe a sizable chunk of the
internet (training data for Large Language Models like ChatGPT’ s
base model).
 
Text generation: Given a prompt by the user, the model creates a text.
OpenAI’ s ChatGPT  is popular for creating high-quality , human-like
output given user prompts. You could ask ChatGPT  to write a rap song
about stubbing your toe against furniture, and it will deliver .
 
Text classiﬁcation: An extremely popular application of natural
language processing is classifying text. Spam detection is a popular
example, hate speech detection is another . Text deduplication,
detection of plagiarism, and news topic classiﬁcation are only some
examples of the varied applications of text classiﬁcation.
 
Translation: As the name suggests, the task is to convert input text
from one language to another target language. For example, let us
assume that we visit Germany as a tourist, and we need to ask for
directions. We can use a translation application to instantly ﬁgure out a
well-structured, (mostly) grammatically correct question in German.
 
Text representation (encoding): Representation is an extremely
important preprocessing step for NLP tasks. A good representation that
captures meaning/ other information in the text has a signiﬁcant impact
on the performance of the end solution. Taking the example of the
Universal Sentence Encoder (by Google, based on the BER T model),
you can use models to ﬁnd a good general-purpose representation for
text.
 
Named entity recognition (NER): NER is the task of identifying key
pieces of information in the text and classiﬁcation into a set of
predeﬁned categories. A sub-task of information extraction, NER is
usually concerned with identifying people, places, organizations, time
expressions (and more) in the input text. When you ask Siri to book a 7
PM appointment with Maria at Chili, NER enables Siri to identify the
place, person and time information contained in the command.
 
Note that these were only some of the popular applications of natural
language processing. An exhaustive list is impractical as with each
passing day, NLP is employed in more and more applications as
research in the ﬁeld continues to bloom. Regardless of the task,
though, the nature of natural language data cannot be ignored. Let us
understand this aspect better .
Importance of sequence processing
 
Natural language is a sequence of terms/words. Words come together
in an order , with dependencies among them, to form a string of words
that expresses a coherent idea. Jumbling the words can make a
meaningful sentence sound gibberish. The ideal machine learning/
artiﬁcial intelligence solution, therefore, can beneﬁt from treating text
data like the sequence they are. Empirically , NLP tasks almost always
beneﬁt from processing the text as a sequence of terms/tokens. Topic
models are a notable exception, where popular approaches disregard
the order in the text. For the tasks listed in the previous section, we
must process sequences and accommodate varying lengths of text.
 
Let us take a moment to reﬂect on how we humans process text in our
minds as we read it. A simple demonstration would be in Table From
top to bottom, a new word is revealed in the sentence. The text
processed in each row contains the text processed so far in that step.
The Sentiment column captures the sentiment association with the text
revealed until that step. In the ﬁrst row, we only have the term The
sentiment associated with it is largely neutral, as reﬂected in the entry
in the Sentiment column. The next term revealed is have and the text
processed sofar becomes I Now this phrase is neutral to positive, but
surely not negative. With the third term being the sentence gets
updated to I have The sentiment for this is negative, as you may agree.
The ﬁnal term revealed is The overall sentence thus becomes, I have
no The sentiment in this statement is positive. Take a look at the
following table:

We can see in the example illustrated in Table 10.1 that with each new
term, our assessment of the sentiment contained in the statement gets
updated. The arrival of the ﬁnal term completely ﬂipped our
assessment from Negative to Considering the nature of speech data, it
is essential to consider not only the current/latest word but also the
relevant words preceding it. The key point here is that the entire
sequence needs to be considered. In the upcoming sections, we will
explore various neural network architectures that are well-suited for
this task. However , before delving into those details, let us ﬁrst
familiarize ourselves with a case study that will provide concrete
examples to grasp these concepts better .
A text classiﬁcation case study
 
Azra Movie Database is an online platform for information on movies,
series and other forms of entertainment content. The overall rating of
the movie (based on votes) is the prominent indicator of the goodness
of a movie that AMDb and other similar platforms use for ranking
movies. AMDb wishes to use a new movie ranking system which uses
not just the overall rating of the title, but also the sentiment in the text
reviews received. For this purpose, AMDb would need to classify the
sentiment in each review as positive or negative. This is a classic text
classiﬁcation task.
 
AMDb believes that the text reviews they receive are extremely
similar in language and content to those received on the Internet Movie
Database Therefore, AMDb plans to use the IMDb reviews dataset to
train the model and use it to classify the sentiment of AMDb reviews.
The IMDb reviews dataset contains 50,000 highly polar movie reviews
along with their sentiment. A beneﬁt of using this dataset is that it is
conveniently available as a TensorFlow dataset through Keras and also
available on the TensorFlow hub. Additionally , the dataset contains
longer sequences (1000-word reviews are not rare) which can help us
better understand the considerations in dealing with longer text. Let us
begin by loading and exploring this data.
 
First, load the imdb module from TensorFlow datasets using the
following command:
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.datasets import imdb
 
With this, the imdb module is loaded. We can now use this to extract
the data with a speciﬁed vocabulary size (the total number of unique
terms in the text). To specify a vocabulary size of 10,000 and then load
the train and test data, use the following commands:
 
size_vocab = 10000
 
(X_train, y_train), (X_test, y_test) =
imdb.load_data(num_words=size_vocab)

Note that you could choose a vocabulary size of 15,000 as well. This is
a hyper -parameter that you must choose. An extremely simple
approach is to drop terms with a frequency lower than a threshold. The
threshold could be derived from your domain understanding, or from
percentiles of term frequencies. Note that the nature of the task plays
an important role in this choice. A couple of papers can help you
explore the topic further –

approaches for text classiﬁcation.
