Machine (Link: is a good read.
 
With this, we have a fair understanding of the data we are working
with. Before we proceed, we need to understand the next major step,
that is, text pre-processing.
Text pre-processing
 
Text data can be riddled with imperfections or domain-speciﬁc
nuances. As you can expect, the language and grammar of textual data
found in legal documents are different from those found in tweets on
Twitter . Emojis and slang are unlikely to be found in legal documents
but are extremely common in social media data. What can be
considered an anomaly in one domain could be a valuable feature in
another . Additionally , the task at hand makes a big difference to what
information we might consider important. Text pre-processing,
therefore, is an extremely important step in data handling that has a
signiﬁcant impact on downstream model performance and therefore
the success of the machine learning/artiﬁcial intelligence solution. A
2017 paper titled On the Role of Text Preprocessing in Neural
Network Architectures: An Evaluation Study on Text Categorization
and Sentiment demonstrated that simple text pre-processing steps can
improve/ deteriorate performance of neural network models
signiﬁcantly .
 
We reiterate that the nature of preprocessing depends on the
data/domain/task at hand. As yet another example, emojis are unlikely
to be relevant for topic classiﬁcation. However , the emoji is an
extremely important feature that can ﬂip our understanding of the
sentiment contained in the text. The main takeaway is that there is no
general, task-agnostic text preprocessing pipeline.
 
However , some clean-up or preprocessing steps are commonly
employed. The ﬁrst important step in pre-processing text data is
breaking the text down into working units. If we are building a
character -level model, our token would be a character . If we are
working with a sentence, then that would be our token. Most
commonly , we work with words as tokens. Splitting the raw input text
into tokens is not a trivial task, but modern NLP utilities have
extremely good performance and can mostly be directly employed
without much manual ﬁne-tuning.
 
Natural language typically has multiple variations of a base word. For
example, the terms and runs all form the base word These are
examples of valid inﬂections that are expected and not errors. Other
types of variations could be spelling errors or even abbreviations.
Mapping these variations back to a base term is called Many clean-up
and pre-processing steps fall under this category .
 
Other clean-up steps involve tasks like the removal of less valuable
terms, normalizing cases, removing some special characters/ tokens
and handling data/domain-speciﬁc nuances. Following are short
descriptions of some of the major steps in text pre-processing:
 
Splitting the raw input text into tokens that form our working units. A
token can be a character , a word, a phrase, a sentence, or even a whole
paragraph.
 
Case In situations where the case of the text does not matter , case
normalization is performed to get all the terms to a consistent case
(lower/ upper). This ensures that Term is treated the same as term by
the model (if needed). If the case matters, this step can be skipped.
 
 
Stemming/lemmatization: Both stemming, and lemmatization are
attempts to canonicalize text. Stemming is a rule-based method to chop
off sufﬁxes of terms to get a stem. The terms driver , driving, and drive
all get mapped to As you may have observed, the result of stemming
need not be a valid English term. Lemmatization is a more
sophisticated, dictionary-based method that considers the part of
speech of the term to convert to the base form. driving and drive would
be lemmatized to a valid term. Wordnet by Princeton University is a
great resource and a lexical database. You can ﬁnd good resources
here: https://wordnet.princeton.edu/
 
Spell Spell correction is an important task in many domains and there
are various approaches for the same. While some modern text
representation methods can tackle spelling variations to some extent, it
is recommended that spell correction be performed. Some popular
approaches are the noisy channel model and phonetic hashing. Noisy
channel model is a general-purpose spell corrector , while phonetic
hashing ﬁxes spelling variations that stem from pronunciation (for
example, Dilli vs.
 
Stop word removal: In natural language, a lot of terms are ﬁller terms
that do not add a lot of information. Often called stop these are
general/functional terms like the, of, and. These might not add value to
certain tasks and can be removed. We need to be careful that the notion
of value depends on the task. Filler words might not be useful for topic
classiﬁcation but are essential for machine translation. Additionally ,
there are contextual stopwords that need handling. For example, in a
dataset containing phone reviews, the term phone might not add a lot
of value as it would be present in a large proportion of reviews. Note
that you could also employ the ideas discussed in the papers referred to
in the previous section to choose vocabulary size.
 
Others: An exhaustive list of miscellaneous text pre-processing and
cleanup tasks is not possible as many of these tasks are
data/domain/task speciﬁc. Abbreviation handling is an example, where
we need to tell the model that Television and TV refer to the same
thing. Tackling domain-speciﬁc encodings is another task, where data
might contain some speciﬁc codes with meaning. For example, Rx in
medical text means prescription/recipe/to take. Handling special
characters and emojis is another such example. Handling collocations,
that is, identifying and marking that Hong Kong is one term, is another
pre-processing task.
 
The wise data scientist considers various aspects and carefully creates
a data pre-processing pipeline suited for the speciﬁc task. It is useful to
have cheat sheets/guides for pre-processing, but the prudent data
scientist must use them merely as guidance and be cautious at each
preprocessing step. Once the cleanup is done, the next important task
is representation. Let us understand this step better and how to
optimize it.
Text representation
 
Feature engineering/data representation is one of the most important
steps in machine learning/ artiﬁcial intelligence. NLP is no exception.
Representation is particularly important in NLP because models can
not directly interpret text and we must convert the sequence of text
into a list of numbers. Our task is to convert a text term like world into
numbers that the machine can understand that is, we need to generate
features from the text. These features eventually go into the model, and
we appreciate by now that garbage in is garbage out.
 
While there could be many creative ways to derive features from text,
we discuss two fundamental and common approaches. One approach is
to use count-based features, also known as, the bag of words approach.
The other approach is to use embeddings. Let us understand each of
these better .
Count-based features
 
Count-based features form one prominent approach. The idea is
extremely simple. Each term in the overall text corpus gets a
corresponding column. If the document in consideration contains the
term, the count of the term in the document is the value in the column.
Overall, there would be as many columns to represent the data, as
words in the entire corpus. Often, this is several thousand. For
example, if a corpus contains 10,000 unique terms, each document/row
will be represented by 10,000 numbers. Among these, only the
columns corresponding to terms present in the document will be non-
zero. The value in the column could be the raw count, or some
normalized count
 
One major drawback of this approach is the sheer number of columns
needed to represent a single row (for example, 10,000) and most
values will be 0. Another bigger drawback is that these features are
insufﬁcient in capturing meaning. Unless the practitioner puts in a
great deal of effort, this representation will not identify that the terms
footpath and pavement have similar meanings. The next approach, that
is, word embeddings, tries to solve both problems and has become the
dominant approach for machine learning for text.
Text embeddings
 
Embeddings are low-dimensional, high-density vectors. We could be
working with 16 dimensional vectors, or even 500, but we rarely have
vectors with higher dimensionality . Compared to 10,000, this is a big
efﬁciency gain. These vectors can be created for each individual word,
for phrases, or entire documents. In theory , a 10,000-word document
can therefore be represented by a 50-dimensional vector i.e., a series of
just 50 numbers. As you may expect, such compact representations
make machine learning for text far more convenient and have
signiﬁcantly helped advance the performance of machine learning/
artiﬁcial intelligence models for NLP.
  
Note: Wikipedia provides a decent overview of the topic of word
embeddings and good resources for further study .
https://en.wikipedia.or g/wiki/W ord_embedding
  
There are various approaches to creating these vectors. For our case
study and most of our discussion, we will create these embeddings
from scratch while building our model for sentiment classiﬁcation.
Other approaches aim at creating embeddings that capture the meaning
of the terms. We will revisit these in the section Using pre-trained Let
us now learn how we can create our own embeddings for a task.
Embedding layer in Keras
 
To create our own embeddings from scratch, we need to learn the
representation for each term that is ﬁt for our task. This is achieved with
the help of an embedding layer in the model. In simple terms, the
embedding layer is simply a lookup. That is, the layer returns the low-
dimensional vector corresponding to the input term. The embeddings layer
is always the ﬁrst layer in any model, as it provides the numeric output
that can be processed by subsequent layers in the neural network. The
working of the layer is illustrated in Figure
 
 
Figure 10.1: The embedding layer
 
The embedding layer can simply be a lookup table/ matrix that returns the
vector corresponding to a certain term/index. These representations can be
initialized randomly . When the model is trained, the layer gets trained like
any other layer , and as the learning progresses, the representation for each
term gets optimized for the task at hand. The embedding layer is the ﬁrst
layer and a regular feature of deep learning models for NLP, regardless of
the architecture that follows. That brings us to the interesting topic of
network architectures for NLP.
Architectures for NLP
 
Text data are always sequences and, in the section, Importance of
sequence we discussed that NLP tasks almost always beneﬁt from
processing the text as a sequence of terms/tokens. We repeat the example
from Table 10.1 in Table
 
 
Table 10.2: Sentiment changes with incoming terms
 
Recall that with each new term coming in, our assessment of the
sentiment contained in the statement gets updated. The arrival of the ﬁnal
term completely ﬂipped our assessment from Negative to The neural
network architecture for such tasks must also be capable of processing the
sequence in the text and not ignoring it. There are two major approaches
for this so far:
 
Storing processed information and updating the assessment with each
incoming term.
 
Processing the entire sequence at once while considering the dependencies
between the terms in the text.
 
Recurrent Neural Networks follow approach 1. They are designed for
processing the text one token at a time storing the information received so
far and updating the assessment. The transformer architecture follows
approach 2. Using certain simple and clever mechanisms, the transformer
architecture looks at the entire sequence while paying attention to certain
parts of it and considering the dependencies between the terms in the text.
We will learn about both approaches in detail in the following sections.
 
In the discussion around architectures for NLP, it is important to
understand how we arrived at the current state-of-the-art models. NLP is
not a new ﬁeld, but deep learning made breakthroughs in NLP tasks only
recently . It is important to understand the order of development of the
models used in NLP to build perspective on the way ahead as well.
Limiting ourselves to notable architectures, the chronological order of
development of popular models is shown in Figure 10.2:
 
 
Figure 10.2: Timeline of the development of popular architectures for text
 
The ﬁrst adaptive forms of RNNs were developed in the 1970s, with
successful application on a deep learning task (more than 1,000 RNN
layers) in 1993 bringing it into the limelight again. The Long Short Term
Memory model was developed in 1997 to overcome some shortcomings
of plain RNNs. Gated Recurrent Units were introduced in 2014 to
simplify LSTMs without losing performance. Around the same time,
RNNs were being increasingly used with attention mechanisms
(introduced in 2014) to improve performance. In 2017, the transformer
architecture was introduced and due to its high performance, soon became
an extremely popular feature of modern NLP models. We will learn about
all these variants and use them in the sentiment classiﬁcation case study .
While doing so, we will compare the different models on multiple aspects
– complexity , size and accuracy – to explore the interplay of these factors.
Let us begin by understanding recurrent architectures.
Recurrent architectures
 
As mentioned earlier , recurrent architectures are designed for sequence
processing. The text can be fed to the neural network one term/token at a
time. The new term is processed by the network, along with the
information from the terms encountered so far. The new term along with
the prior information is used to make a prediction. The main feature which
enables such processing is a loop. RNNs have loops in them which allow
the network to access some hidden information corresponding to data
received in the previous step. Figure 10.3 illustrates the idea of loops:
 
 
Figure 10.3: An unfolded Recurrent Neural Network
 
The Xs in the unfolded view are input tokens at different timestamps.
Consider the timestamp t. The input to the hidden layers is not only the
new term but also the data from which is the hidden state from the
previous timestamp. Both of these are used to generate the output,
Similarly , the next step at time t+1 will use the hidden layer data from
time t. This is how recurrence occurs in an RNN.
  
Note: For details on the workings of the different prominent RNNs, you
can refer to this tutorial. https://arxiv .org/abs/1808.03314
  
Figure 10.4 helps us understand the difference between the architectures
of a regular feedforward neural network and a recurrent neural network.
One natural difference is that the feedforward network reads the entire
input data at once. The key difference in architecture is the loop, which is
simply a way to copy the current hidden state and use it at the next time
stamp. Note that another weight matric is needed for the hidden state data
to be passed to the next hidden state. As you might expect, there are more
parameters in the recurrent neural network due to the additional weights
for the recurrent part. Take a look at the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure Regular Feedforward vs. Recurrent neural
 
To estimate the number of parameters in a recurrent layer , we need to
recall that there are two types of weights, feed-forward weights and
recurrent weights. The total number of parameters would be: (n + 1) x m +
m x m
 
Where n is the input dimension and m is the number of recurrent units.
 
The illustration we saw in Figure 10.4 corresponds to the simplest, plain
vanilla RNN. It forms the basis for other complex RNN variants. Let us
see the simple RNN in action and see how it performs on our review
sentiment classiﬁcation case study .
 
First, we need to ensure reproducibility of the results (to the extent
possible). For this, we will import numpy and tensorﬂow and set the seed
for random number generation for both packages to 42. Use the following
code to accomplish this:
 
import tensorﬂow as tf
 
import numpy as np
 
 
 
With the seeds set, we can now expect largely similar runs between
multiple runs and between multiple machines.
  
Note: Despite setting the seeds for Tensorﬂow and Numpy , your results
might not exactly match ours. This is because there are additional sources
of randomization that are hard to control. Regardless, the results you
obtain should be very similar to ours.
  
A detail at this step is that our input sequences are still of differing
lengths. That is, we have very short reviews in the data (less than 50
words) along with long reviews (more than 1,000 words). For our model
to work properly , we need to ﬁx an upper limit to the number of terms that
need to be processed. For our case study , we will ﬁx this to 200. Setting
the length to 200 would require truncating longer reviews and padding
shorter reviews. This can be done easily using the pad_sequences method
in Keras. Let us import it using the following command:
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.preprocessing import sequence
 
Next, we use the pad_sequence method to get all reviews to 200 terms
using the following code:

X_test_proc = sequence.pad_sequences(X_test, max_len)

Now , from import the Sequential model. Also import the Dense,
SimpleRNN, Dropout and SpatialDropout1D layers. The SimpleRNN
layer is the implementation of the plain vanilla RNN. The Embedding
layer creates the lookup for the token embeddings and must be the ﬁrst
layer in NLP models using embeddings. SpatialDropout1D performs the
same function as a regular dropout layer . The difference is that
SpatialDropout1D drops entire feature maps that is, vectors in our case.
Use the following code:
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.models import Sequential

from tensorﬂow .keras.layers import Embedding, Dropout,
SpatialDropout1D
 
The utilities are all loaded. We can start deﬁning the model. First, let us
deﬁne the text representation part - the embedding layer . We will learn our
embeddings while training. The input_dim argument to the embedding
layer must be the size of the vocabulary . In our case, it is 10,000 variable).
The output_dim represents the size of the resulting low-dimensional
vectors. We choose a value of 32 (arbitrary at this point, we will optimize
this in the section titled Tuning network hyper -parameters), meaning that
we will represent each term/ token by a series of 32 numbers.
Additionally , to minimize overﬁtting, we will use a SpatialDropout1D
layer with a 30% dropout rate. This can be done using the following code:
 
model_rnn = Sequential()
 
model_rnn.add(Embedding(input_dim=size_vocab,

number of recurrent units. Replacing the values in the formula, we get 33
x 32 + 32 x which equals 2,080. It is important to note that even for this
tiny network we have more than 300k parameters, mainly coming from
the embedding layer .

stop_early =

The results of the last few epochs are in Figure 10.6:
 
 
Figure 10.6: Output from ﬁnal training epochs
 
We see that the model obtained a validation accuracy of around 85.8%.
This is a decent result for a start with no optimization at all. Let us now
learn about a more complex RNN type.
Long Short Term Memory
 
The idea of plain RNNs is simple and works well with very short text. For
longer text, however , there are a few issues. When the sequences are long,
the training (that also happens over time steps) encounters issues like the
vanishing gradient (the backpropagation of the error to early time steps is
negligible). This made RNNs not too useful for long text and for handling
long-range dependencies in the text.
 
LSTMs were designed to better handle the vanishing gradient problem.
Instead of making all the information and error passing through all the
time steps and activations, LSTMs use explicit cell states. These cell
states are information that takes an expressway . The ﬂow of information
on these expressways is regulated by some gating mechanisms. The
explicit cell states do not encounter any activations and therefore the
vanishing gradient problem is irrelevant to them. This key difference is
what allows the LSTM to model long-range dependencies.
  
Further reading: The Wikipedia page for LSTM provides a good overview
of LSTMs and their development timeline.
https://en.wikipedia.or g/wiki/Long_short-term_memory For an
understanding of the detailed inner workings of LSTMs, refer to this great
blog: https://colah.github.io/posts/2015-08-Understanding-LSTMs/
  
Indeed, LSTMs are extremely powerful and have quickly replaced plain
RNNs in most real-world applications. This power comes at a cost.
LSTMs are far more complex owing to the additional gating mechanisms.
To enable these mechanisms, LSTMs have many more parameters. By
design, the LSTM cell has 4 times as many parameters as a plain RNN.
Let us create our own LSTM model for the review sentiment classiﬁcation
case study and see if we get any improvement in the results.
 
We will continue using the same code notebook. The code will be very
similar to the code we used for building the plain RNN model. The only
difference would be in the model deﬁnition stage where we will use an
LSTM layer instead of a SimpleRNN layer . To begin, import the LSTM
layer from Keras using the following command:
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.layers import LSTM
 
Now , deﬁne the Sequential model like we did earlier . Add an embedding
layer and spatial dropout using the following code:
 
model_lstm = Sequential()
 
model_lstm.add(Embedding(input_dim=size_vocab,
 
 
Let us now add the 32 LSTM units to the network. The result of the
architecture remains the same, deﬁne the same. Use the following code:
 
 
 
 
Now , compile the model using the same conﬁguration as in the previous
exercise using SimpleRNN and print out the model summary using the
following code:
 
 
 
 
model_lstm.summary()
 
The model summary is printed as follows in Figure 10.7:
 
 
Figure 10.7: Summary of the LSTM model
 
Notice the number of parameters in the LSTM layer . As stated earlier , the
LSTM cell has 4 times as many parameters as the plain RNN cell. This
can be easily veriﬁed as the LSTM layer has 8,320 parameters which is
exactly 4 times the parameters that the SimpleRNN layer had (2,080).
 
Fit the model using the same scheme as earlier and the same batch size
using the following code:
 
stop_early =
 
model_lstm.ﬁt(X_train_proc, y_train,
 
 
               callbacks=[stop_early],
 
The training output is printed as follows in Figure 10.8:
 
 
Figure 10.8: LSTM training output
 
The output shows that the LSTM model quickly breached 87% accuracy
on the validation data and a couple of epochs had close to 88% validation
accuracy . This is a signiﬁcant improvement over the simple RNN model.
The text sequences we are dealing with are not too long. We can expect
the performance difference between plain RNN and LSTMs to be larger
when dealing with longer text.
 
The LSTM clearly has higher accuracy than the plain RNN. But we also
notice that there are 4 times as many parameters and the training time was
also signiﬁcantly higher . This is due to the complexity of the LSTM. A lot
of research went into simplifying the LSTM cell in an attempt to create a
simpler version with fewer parameters without losing its predictive power .
The GRU was one such prominent version coming out of this research.
Let us learn more about it.
Gated recurrent units
 
The Gated Recurrent Unit was introduced in 2014 with the intent of
simplifying the LSTM cell without losing its predictive power . In many
applications, the GRUs were successful in matching LSTMs’  predictive
power with lower training times and fewer parameters. The main change
in the GRU is the omission of the explicit cell state. Instead, in the GRU,
the hidden state of the hidden layer is allowed to ﬂow freely . A simple
way to look at this is that the GRUs did to the hidden state what the
LSTMs did to the explicit cell state. This movement is still regulated by
gates.
 
As a result of these changes, GRUs still carry the major idea of LSTM,
freely ﬂowing information that does not pass through activations. Due to
the gating mechanisms, GRUs have higher parameters compared to plain
RNNs. Although, the GRU has 75% of the number of parameters that the
LSTM has. The GRU should therefore be able to model long-range
dependencies as well with better training times.
  
Note: There are many more variants of RNNs other than the 3 we
discussed. We encourage you to read and explore more. Some prominent
examples are Independent RNNs (IndRNN), Clockwork RNNs (CW -
RNN), and Time Aware LSTMs (T-LSTM).
  
We can easily assess the performance of the GRU model in our case study .
The code for the GRU model will be like that for the SimpleRNN and
LSTM models. The only change is using the GRU layer from Keras. The
following code shows the model deﬁnition for the GRU model:
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.layers import GRU
 
model_gru = Sequential()
 
model_gru.add(Embedding(input_dim=size_vocab,
 
 
 
 
 
The rest of the code remains the same. A summary of the model gives us
the following result, as in Figure
 
 
Figure 10.9: Summary of the GRU model
 
The number of parameters in the GRU layer is 6,240. We can see that this
is 3 times the number of parameters of the SimpleRNN layer (2,080) and
75% the number of parameters that the LSTM layer had (8,320). On
training the model, we saw that some of the epochs gave us validation
accuracy of over 87%.
  
Note: For brevity , we skipped the code for the complete model training
and evaluation. We encourage you to create the entire model for GRUs
and compare your results with ours.
  
In our case study , we saw that the GRU model had fewer parameters and
had performance comparable to the LSTM model. Both LSTM and GRU
models gave us 87%+ validation accuracy , conﬁrming that GRUs
successfully capture the power of the LSTMs while being faster to train.
We still have not extracted full potential from recurrent architectures,
though. Some creative uses of the RNN cells helped us get even better
results. Bi-directional recurrent networks are one such idea. Let us learn
about it.
Bi-directional recurrent architectures
 
The architectures we saw so far were predicting the next token/term based
on the previous tokens/terms. In that sense they were unidirectional. In
certain situations, it helps to know the terms that come after the target
term. For instance, when performing named entity recognition, the terms
right after the target term can be crucial for performance. The task of
translation also beneﬁts signiﬁcantly from this type of look Bi-directional
architectures allow for such a look ahead. The architecture of a bi-
directional recurrent network is illustrated in Figure 10.10:
 
 
Figure 10.10: Architecture of a bi-directional recurrent neural source
 
The key property of bi-directional networks is an additional recurrent
layer that processes the tokens in the reverse order . That is, the new
hidden layer receives the last token (in the sequence) ﬁrst. The bi-
directional model therefore employs two RNNs in parallel, the forward
layer and the backward At each time step, the states of the forward and
backward layers are combined to form the output.
 
The idea applies to all variants of RNNs. That is, you can have bi-
directional networks of plain RNNs, GRUs, LSTMs or any other variant.
Several tasks beneﬁt from this architecture that permits a look ahead.
Machine translation, named entity recognition, and part of speech tagging
are some prominent examples. Before the transformer architecture was
introduced, bi-directional RNNs (usually LSTMs) were present in state-
of-the-art models. LSTMs were the most popular approach and erstwhile
state-of-the-art models like ELMO and ULMFiT  were based on them.
Recurrent architectures with attention
 
RNN architecture using an encoder and decoder needs to memorise the
entire input sequence in a single hidden state. For longer text, the idea
intuitively does not seem great. For different tasks, different parts of the
sentence might be important. For example, for the translation use case,
multiple parts of the sentence are important and need emphasis. For this
purpose, the attention head was added. The attention layer gives the
network access to the entire input sequence. The attention layer gives a
different weightage to different parts of the input sequence, allowing it to
focus on or pay attention to. It uses a second recurrent layer to selectively
weight parts of the sequence.
 
In general, attention can be taken in two sentences and aligned with them.
The result of such alignment is the identiﬁcation of some form of
correlation or matching between the different sentences. In the general
case, the attention model allows us to align the input sequence and the
output sequence, identifying the parts in the input to focus on for a
speciﬁc output term. To make this idea clear , let us look at a translation
example in Figure 10.11 that was part of the original paper by Dzmitry
Bahdanau, KyungHyun Cho, and Yoshua Bengio in 2015 Take a look at
the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure Alignment matrix learnt for a translation
 
In Figure 10.11, white denotes a high correlation while black signiﬁes a
low correlation. We can see in the ﬁgure that the alignment matrix shows
that the term mon in the input is important to the term my in the output.
Likewise, the term Famille is most important for the output term It is
worth noticing that for the output term the model identiﬁes two input
terms, va and changer as important. Adding attention heads to RNNs
(usually LSTM with attention) was an extremely popular approach that
provided the then state-of-the-art results. However , with the introduction
of the transformer architecture changed this and current state-of-the-art
models are largely based on the transformer architecture. Let us
understand this ground-breaking development.
Transformer architecture
 
The RNN + attention scheme we discussed in the previous section
provides high accuracy and was state-of-the-art when it became popular .
The approach, however , had some drawbacks. For one, training was even
more time-consuming due to the attention layer . Secondly , by design,
recurrent architectures are difﬁcult to parallelize. Additionally , the long-
range dependency issue was better solved, but it came at a high cost. The
models were extremely complex. Attempts were made to simplify this
complex architecture, and the transformer architecture resulted from
  
Note: A great and intuitive illustrated explanation of the transformer
architecture can be found here: http://jalammar .github.io/illustrated-
transformer/
  
The key features of a transformer are self-attention and positional
encodings. The recurrence of RNNs is replaced by self-attention. The
transformer architecture explicitly processes the entire sequence at once.
This is particularly useful for tasks like translation and question
answering, where capturing long-range dependencies between the terms is
essential for high performance. To understand self-attention better ,
including the detailed workings of the components, refer to the illustrated
guide here: http://jalammar .github.io/illustrated-transformer/
 
The transformer architecture is composed of encoder/decoder
components. The encoder in a transformer is rather simple, a self-attention
layer followed by a feed-forward layer(s). This is illustrated in Figure
 
 
Figure 10.12: The simple encoder in a transformer
  
Note: While self-attention was a key feature of the transformer
architecture initially , the idea of the transformer doesn’ t need attention in
the way deﬁned earlier . Apple’ s Machine Learning Research team
introduced the Attention Free Transformer in June 2021, which simpliﬁes
the calculation and reduces the memory complexity - resulting in a high-
performant yet efﬁcient transformer variant. Details can be found here:
https://machinelearning.apple.com/research/attention-free-transformer
  
In Keras, a transformer block can be created using the
MultiHeadAttention layer . The implementation is based on the paper
Attention is All You Need et al., The layer performs 1D cross-attention
over two input sequences. The arguments to the layer are listed in Figure
For more details on the arguments and usage examples, refer to the
ofﬁcial documentation here: If the and value are the same, self-attention is
performed.
 
 
Figure 10.13: Arguments to the MultiHeadAttention layer
 
The functional notation needs to be used in Keras to perform self-attention
(using the same inputs twice). First, we can import the layer like any other
Keras layer , using the following command:
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.layers import MultiHeadAttention
 
The layer can then be used like any other layer (using the functional
notation) as shown in the following command. Note the repeated input
(x,x) that performs self-attention. Execute the following code:
 
x = MultiHeadAttention(
 
    key_dim=head_size, num_heads=num_heads) (x, x)
 
A full working example of a simple transformer in action using Keras
layers can be found here:
https://keras.io/examples/timeseries/timeseries_transformer_classiﬁcation/
  
Note: a manual implementation of a high-performance Transformer model
from scratch is tedious and requires a fair amount of ﬁne-tuning and
training. We will therefore use libraries speciﬁc to transformer models in
Chapter 11, Transfer Learning, where we will employ state-of-the-art
transformer models.
  
With this, we complete the important discussion around the various
architectures for NLP. We see that we have multiple choices for
architecture, each having its merits. After making a careful choice of
architecture, we need to optimize its hyper -parameters to get the
maximum performance from the model and the data. Let us learn how to
tune the hyper -parameters for text models.
Tuning network hyper -parameters
 
We have seen so far that NLP tasks differ from regular machine
learning/artiﬁcial intelligence tasks in two major aspects.
 
First, high performance needs the right data representation, which is
usually through word embeddings.
 
Second, the processing of the text as a sequence requires special
architectures like recurrence and attention.
 
Both these are usual features of modern NLP models and must
therefore be optimized along with other aspects. We will learn how to
tune the hyper -parameters for a text classiﬁcation task. The logic can
be used to tune other hyper -parameters, as we have learnt in Chapter 8,
Hyper -parameter optimization.
 
We will use the GRU for our case study as it is very capable as well as
fast to train.
 
In Chapter Hyper -parameter optimization we have learnt how to tune
hyper -parameters for deep neural networks using Keras Tuner. We will
continue to use the Keras tuner for the exercises in this chapter . We
recommend going through Chapter 8, Hyper -parameter optimization if
you have not done so yet, so that the code in this section is easier to
understand.
 
If Keras tuner is not installed already , install it using the following
command:
 
!pip install keras-tuner -q
 
You should get a completion message as shown here:
 
176.1/176.1 kB 4.6 MB/s eta 0:00:00
 
Once installed, import the Keras tuner using the following command:
 
import keras_tuner as kt
 
Now we are ready to tune the hyper -parameters for a text classiﬁcation
model and tune text-related speciﬁcs like embedding size. Let us do so
in the next exercise.

should get a conﬁrmation message similar to the following:

Best val_accuracy So Far: 0.8809999823570251 Total elapsed time: 01h
05m 52s
 
Note that because of system differences, the timing and resulting accuracy
might be slightly different in your output. The differences in performance
should be negligible, though. The difference in time can be signiﬁcant, of
course. Now . using the get_best_hyperparameters method of the tuner , we
will print the best hyper -parameter setting identiﬁed by the tuner .
 
Use the get_best_hyperparameters method of the tuner to print the best
hyperparameter combination.
 
Use the following command:

res.values

The best combination seems to be an embedding size of 48 with 64 GRU
units and TanH as the activation.
 
Print the validation performance of the top 3 models using the
results_summary method of the tuner . Use the following code:
 
 
The top three hyper -parameter combinations and their validation score get
printed as shown in Figure
 
 
Figure 10.14: Top 3 hyper -parameter choices
 
The best-performing model had 64 GRU units with TanH activation and
an embedding size of 48. The validation accuracy of this model was 88%.
Note that the next two combinations are extremely close in their scores.
The third-best model has 87.9% validation accuracy . However , this model
uses an embedding size of just 16 and uses 32 units (half of the top
model). The third-best model is signiﬁcantly simpler with practically the
same performance. A data scientist seeking parsimony may choose the
third model as the best one for the task.
 
The result we got is similar to the result we got from LSTMs earlier . We
encourage you to try out more combinations and larger search spaces to
maximize your chances of getting better scores. Now that we know how
to tune a recurrent neural network with embeddings, let us learn a neat
trick that helps with the performance and efﬁciency of the model. Using
convolutions for NLP is not very intuitive but is extremely useful. Let us
understand how that works.
Using convolutions for NLP
 
Convolutions are used for deep-learning image processing. As
discussed in Chapter Optimizing Image there are a couple of reasons
for their popularity . First, convolutions help the model learn local
features from the image. Second, convolutions help reduce the number
of parameters. The good news is that these beneﬁts of convolutions can
be obtained for NLP tasks as well, with some minor differences in how
we employ the convolutions. Instead of 2D convolutions for images,
we need to use 1D convolutions for text as the input is a 1D sequence
of tokens. In the case of images, ﬁlters extract patches from the image
to give us output features. In the case of one dimension, the ﬁlter
would extract subsequences from the input sequences and give us a
value for the output features.
 
Using convolutions also helps tackle dependencies in the data. Using
multiple kernels to the same text helps in identifying dependencies
among terms while extracting groups of terms. A ﬁlter size of 2 would
help extract two-word combinations. Likewise, a ﬁlter of size 3 would
capture relationships between trigrams/triplets of words and so on.
When followed by a pooling layer , the features get further subsampled.
This helps with reducing the number of features and therefore
parameters in the model and as a result can lead to faster models
without a signiﬁcant drop in performance. The gains in efﬁciency are
clear when working with large neural networks with multiple layers.
 
Let us apply 1D convolutions in conjunction with GRUs to our
sentiment classiﬁcation case study for AMDb and see for ourselves if
the performance of the hybrid model is comparable to the models we
built so far. Note that we expect the beneﬁts from 1D convents to be
apparent in larger neural networks. Our small neural network might
not see the same gains but is sufﬁcient for illustration. Let us get
started.
Exercise 10.2: Using 1D convolutions and RNNs
 
In this exercise, we will continue working on the review sentiment
classiﬁcation case study for AMDb. We will use the GRU as our base
recurrent cell for the model and use the same architecture as before. The
embedding layer will continue to be the ﬁrst layer . We will add a 1D
convolution layer right after the embedding layer so that the GRU works
with not just individual tokens but also data from combinations of tokens.
Let us begin:
 
First, import the Conv1D layer from Keras using the following command:
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.layers import Conv1D

The accuracy is good and comparable to the earlier models. As mentioned
earlier , for bigger neural networks, the efﬁciency gains are very
signiﬁcant. Also, we did not employ any pooling for feature reduction. We
therefore did not see any speed beneﬁt. We encourage you to build a larger
network for the task and employ pooling to see the beneﬁt ﬁrst-hand.
 
Using 1D convolutions along with recurrent neural networks is one handy
trick for improving the performance of your model. We will now look at
another useful trick that can be extremely handy , especially when working
with smaller datasets.
Using pre-trained embeddings
 
Recall that in the ﬁrst few sections, we stated that preprocessing and
appropriate data representation are key to success in NLP tasks. For
representation, we have been using low-dimensional embeddings so
far for this purpose. The embeddings have been trained during the
model training, meaning that the embeddings are task and model-
speciﬁc. The embeddings learnt as part of the LSTM-based model
would be different from those as part of the GRU-based model. Recall
from the exercises that this embedding layer led to a high number of
trainable parameters in the network. For 32-dimensional embeddings
and a vocabulary size of 10,000, this meant 320,000 parameters. Such
high parameters would contribute to overﬁtting as well. To effectively
train such embeddings, you would generally need a lot of data and/or
careful regularization.
 
Another alternative to training your task-speciﬁc embeddings is using
pre-trained This is a form of transfer learning wherein you use some
pre-trained model/feature mappings for data representation. In the
simplest form, we could use ready-made term mappings (as simple as
a dictionary) to simply look up the vector for a term. A more
sophisticated pre-trained embedding model would give you the
embedding for the entire input text. For our review sentiment
classiﬁcation case study , such a model would give us a single vector
for any input review , regardless of its length. Both approaches have
their merits and are worth trying out in your machine learning/
artiﬁcial intelligence solution.
  
Note: Using pre-trained embeddings is only one of the many forms of
transfer learning. We will learn about transfer learning types and
models in detail in Chapter 11, Transfer Learning.
  
Popular pretrained models are trained on billions of tokens from
millions of documents. Such training would be impractical for an
individual to perform. Thanks to transfer learning and the power of the
open-source community , such powerful representations are available
for anyone to use. Let us ﬁrst understand the beneﬁts and drawbacks of
using pre-trained embeddings.
Beneﬁts
 
Beneﬁt from transfer learning and training from high volumes of data.
 
Text preprocessing effort is reduced (especially for models that allow
raw text input).
 
Some embedding models have ﬁxed-size output, making the
processing simpler .
Drawbacks
 
Not necessarily good for your task.
 
Potentially biased (especially if derived from open data, for example, chat
forums).
 
To make the idea and the power of using pre-trained embeddings more
concrete, let us use them for our case study .
 
We begin with installing and importing the necessary libraries,
TensorFlow hub and TensorFlow datasets. The following code does this:
 
!pip install tensorﬂow-hub -q
 
!pip install tensorﬂow-datasets -q
 
import tensorﬂow as tf, tensorﬂow_hub as tfhub
 
import tensorﬂow_datasets as tfds
 
Next, we load the IMDb review data using the load function from
TensorFlow datasets, specifying 80% train data and reserving 20% data
for testing. Use the following command:
 
train_data, valid_data, test_data =
 
 
 
An iterator over the dataset is now ready . We can print an example review
from the data using the following code:
 
train_examples_batch, _ =
 
 
The review gets printed as in Figure
 
 
Figure 10.16: Sample raw text record from the training data
 
We can see that the training data contains raw review text. The version of
the dataset that we used earlier had tokenized indices. This is an important
difference and a useful one. In case you want to preprocess the raw text in
a customized manner , the raw text version is more useful. In our case, we
wish to employ an embedding model that takes in raw text. Therefore, this
raw text version is more appropriate.
 
We will pick an embedding model from TensorFlow Hub for our case
study , as it is convenient to use in TensorFlow and Keras as a regular
layer , as we will shortly see. We will use the Universal Sentence Encoder
model by Google. It is a highly capable model trained on a variety of
English text using a Deep Averaging Network encoder . It outputs a vector
of length 512 for any input (raw) text. The model is optimized for greater -
than-word-length text. That is, the model is designed to create
representations for phrases, sentences, or short paragraphs. This ﬁts our
purpose well.
  
Note: The Universal Sentence Encoder is a good general-purpose encoder
that provides high-quality embeddings for various tasks. To learn more
about USE, the ofﬁcial documentation on TensorFlow is a good starting
point. The page also has a link to a useful demo notebook model. Refer:
https://tfhub.dev/google/universal-sentence-encoder/4
  
There are many other pre-trained text embeddings available on the
TensorFlow hub. A good alternative is which outputs 128-dimensional
embeddings. Smaller size embeddings may speed up the training and
execution of your model but might lead to lower accuracy as fewer
dimensions would capture less information. If your text contains
punctuations or additional characters, you could try the model This model
is similar to the nnlm model but with additional text normalization/
preprocessing. You can explore more text embedding models on
TensorFlow Hub: https://tfhub.dev/s?module-type=text-embedding
 
We are using models that output a ﬁxed-length vector , regardless of the
input text length. It is important to realize that this means that we do not
need recurrence anymore as input text length becomes irrelevant. Let us
try this out ourselves.
 
First, we will load the model into a Keras layer . The TensorFlow Hub
package that we installed allows us to load models directly as a layer in
Keras, that can be added to any model like any regular Keras layer . We
need to specify the path and whether the layer needs to be trainable. Note
that using some other model from TensorFlow Hub is as simple as
changing the reference to the model. Use the following code:
 
embedding = "https://tfhub.dev/google/universal-sentence-encoder/4"
 
hub_layer = tfhub.KerasLayer(embedding, input_shape=[],
 
dtype=tf.string,
 
 
In our case, we do not want the embedding model to be retrained, so we
speciﬁed The hub_layer object can now be used as a standalone encoder ,
or like an ordinary embedding layer in a Keras model. Let us see how it
encodes the example review that we saw earlier . Pass the 4th review from
the train_examples_batch variable into the hub_layer as in the following
command and print its shape:
 
 
The shape gets printed as follows:
 
TensorShape([1, 512])
 
The output conﬁrms that we have a series of 512 numbers representing the
input text. Recall that the length of the input text does not matter as the
model outputs a ﬁxed length vector for the entire input text. To see the
ﬁrst 10 numbers in the vector , use the following command.
 
 
The output is as follows:
 
shape=(10,), dtype=ﬂoat32, numpy= array([-0.00274222, -0.04518872,
0.02080956, 0.03594078, -0.03814794, 0.07527251, 0.01817022,
-0.02279347, 0.07460126, 0.0103387 ], dtype=ﬂoat32)>
 
The ﬁrst 10 numbers in the vector are printed. These do not make sense to
us, but the 512 numbers together are a powerful representation that
captures very well the meaning of the text. We now have the embedding
layer ready and are all set to start building the model in the next exercise.
Exercise- Text classiﬁcation with pre-trained embeddings
 
In this exercise, we will build a sentiment classiﬁcation model with pre-
trained embeddings. We will leverage the embedding layer we created in
the previous section using TensorFlow Hub. As a result, we have a
hub_layer object which can be used like a regular Keras layer . Note that
we do not need recurrence due to the ﬁxed-length output of the embedding
layer . We will build a simple model with two dense layers with relu
activation. Let us begin.
 
Import the required layers and utilities from Keras using the commands
that follow .
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.models import Sequential
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.layers import Dense
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.callbacks import EarlyStopping
 
Initialize a sequential model and add hub_layer (the embedding model) as
a layer using the following code:
 
model_emb = Sequential()
 
model_emb.add(hub_layer)
 
Next, add two dense layers of 128 units each with relu activation and add
the ﬁnal dense unit for prediction. Print out the model summary . Use the
following code:
 
 
 
 
model_emb.summary()
 
The model summary gets printed as shown in the following ﬁgure:

Notice that there are merely 82,305 trainable parameters. There are about
256 million parameters in the embedding layer (USE), but the layer is not
trainable. We therefore have relatively very few parameters to train.
 
Compile the model with the adam optimizer and binary_crossentropy as
the loss function. Use the following code:
 
 
optimizer='adam',
 
metrics=['accuracy'])
 
Deﬁne early stopping with patience 10 and enable restoring the best
weights. Use the following code:
 
stop_early =
 
Fit the model and use the validation dataset for validation, run for 10
epochs. Use the batch function of the TensorFlow datasets to create

validation_data =

Partial output from the last few epochs should look like Figure
 
 
Figure 10.18: Partial output from ﬁnal epochs
 
We can see that we have achieved close to 86% validation accuracy .
Considering that we had very few parameters in our model (about 82k)
and performed no ﬁne-tuning, this is a good result. Also, note that we did
not use any recurrence or attention We encourage you to try out different
hyper -parameters and improve on this result. In this exercise, we used the
Universal Sentence Encoder model by Google, which is an embedding
model. As mentioned earlier , there are various approaches to embedding
and various popular models. Let us look at some other notable
embeddings.
Notable pre-trained embeddings
 
The information that the embeddings capture depends not only on the data
used for training but also on the task for which they were created. When
we created our embeddings from scratch using the embedding layer for
our RNN models, the vectors for the terms were getting optimized for the
task at hand that is, sentiment classiﬁcation. It would be incorrect to say
that the embeddings we got were general-purpose embeddings, or that
those embeddings captured the meaning of the terms. Those embeddings
were speciﬁc for the task of sentiment classiﬁcation, derived from movie
review data. Fortunately for us, there have been many attempts by
researchers to build more general-purpose embeddings that can be used
for multiple tasks. The Universal Sentence Encoder we used in the
previous exercise is one such example.
 
The GloV e project by the Stanford NLP Group is another extremely
valuable project. The group trained word embeddings on billions of
tokens over millions of documents and made the embeddings publicly
available. Embeddings are available for different datasets Wikipedia,
Common Crawl, and Twitter . Each dataset has billions of tokens.
Additionally , the vectors are available in different sizes, so you can
download 50-dimensional vectors or even 200-dimensional vectors. The
vectors from the GloV e project have some impressive demos and are
known to capture some very interesting regularities between words.
Figure 10.19 shows some semantic regularities captured by GloV e
vectors. Take a look at the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure 10.19: Semantic regularities captured by GloV e word vectors
  
Note: Read more about the GloV e project and access the different word
vectors at the following link. https://nlp.stanford.edu/projects/glove/
  
Google Research also has publicly made available word vectors based on
the word2vec algorithm and trained on the Google News dataset
containing over 100 billion words. These vectors too seem to capture
semantic regularities between terms. Using these word vectors, we can
calculate the nearest neighbour for any target term. Figure 10.20 shows
the nearest neighbours for some test terms. We can see that the neighbours
make a lot of sense and are semantically very close. Take a look at the
following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure 10.20: Nearest neighbours for each term’ s word vector
 
GloV e and Word2V ec are not the only candidates out there. The Facebook
Research group used their FastT ext algorithm to train word vectors on
large volumes of data in 157 languages and made it publicly available.
The vectors can be downloaded following the instructions on this page:
https://github.com/facebookresearch/fastT ext/blob/master/docs/crawl-
vectors.md
  
Note: For a general, illustrated overview of embeddings and the various
types of regularities and relations they capture, refer to the following link:
https://colah.github.io/posts/2014-07-NLP-RNNs-Representations/
  
Regardless of which pre-trained embeddings we opt for, we are bound to
reap the advantages of the extensive data and computing resources utilized
by these organizations to develop these embeddings. These embeddings
are remarkably potent, as they seem to grasp semantic patterns and
possess some level of meaning, making them close to general-purpose
embeddings. Armed with the capabilities of open-source models/data and
the NLP model optimization techniques we have learned, you should now
be well-equipped to build high-performance models.
Conclusion
 
We saw that natural language data has some peculiarities and a good
machine learning/artiﬁcial intelligence solution must consider these.
For most tasks, models must process the data as a sequence.
Additionally , preprocessing is an extremely important step in NLP for
which there are no generic/standard pipelines. A prudent data scientist
will study the data closely and customize their approach for the task.
With the pre-processing done, the representation of textual data must
be done appropriately , and we learnt that embeddings/ low
dimensional vectors are great for most tasks.
 
For processing language data appropriately , we looked at two major
approaches and architectures, recurrent neural networks, and the
transformer architecture. While there are many variants of the RNN,
the core idea remains the same. The network processes the terms one
by one and maintains and updates information at each step. The
transformer approach is different because it processes the entire input
sequence explicitly at once, using clever mechanisms to identify the
important parts of the sequence and to capture the dependencies
between terms. Both these approaches provide great results, with
transformer architectures now becoming the most popular approach.
 
We then saw ways to optimize the model architecture and hyper -
parameters. Using the Keras tuner , we learnt how to tune not just
regular hyper -parameters but also those about embeddings. We learnt a
useful trick that 1D convolutions used along with RNNs can provide
an efﬁciency boost without losing accuracy . Another strategy we
learned and implemented was using pre-trained embeddings. Using
this, we could get results similar to earlier models with very few
parameters. This helped us realize the power of Transfer Learning.
 
Pre-trained embeddings are one of the many forms of Transfer
Learning, which is the focus of the next chapter .

CHAPTER 11
Transfer Learning
Introduction
 
This chapter discusses the paradigm of machine learning/artiﬁcial
intelligence, that is, the next driver of ML commercial success which
will take artiﬁcial intelligence to the masses. The past few years have
seen rapid advancements in the ﬁeld of machine learning / AI. This
chapter will show how you can beneﬁt from these and utilize some of
the latest State-of-the-Art models available in the open-source
community and use them in your applications.
Structure
 
In this chapter , we will discuss the following topics:
 
•Motivation for transfer learning
 
•Using a SOTA image classiﬁcation model
 
•What is transfer learning?
 
∘Applications of transfer learning
 
∘Beneﬁts of transfer learning
 
∘Limitations of transfer learning
 
•Sources for pre-trained models
 
•General transfer learning workﬂow
 
•Transfer learning for images
 
∘Fine-tuning a SOTA image model
 
•Transfer learning for text
 
∘Applications of transfer learning for text
 
∘Fine-tuning a SOTA NLP model
Objectives
 
By the end of this chapter , you will be able to appreciate and apply
various forms of transfer learning. We will ﬁrst understand the
different types of transfer learning and see how they are helpful in
modern machine learning/ artiﬁcial intelligence. We will see both the
beneﬁts and limitations of it. We will explore various sources for
transfer learning modules. We will use a SOTA model out of the box
for our speciﬁc task and eventually learn how to ﬁne-tune the model
for better performance. Transfer learning is slightly different for image
processing and NLP tasks. By the end of the chapter , you will be able
to perform both. We will use various SOTA models in the chapter and
employ popular libraries for transfer learning.
Motivation for transfer learning
 
Let us take an example. As a data scientist at the fashion giant Azra,
you want to sort various images posted by customers on popular social
media platforms and identify images containing fashion items: dresses,
coats, shoes, etc. This would help the organization identify trends
better and anticipate demand better . This seems to be a perfect
situation for a machine learning/artiﬁcial intelligence model and where
you, as a data scientist, can add tremendous value. There are a couple
of approaches you can take to solve this problem.
 
The ﬁrst option is to develop your own solution from scratch. This
would entail all the steps of the CRISP-DM framework, as outlined in
Chapter 1, Optimizing ML/AI Solutions. Deep learning models would
be a natural choice for this task, and you will therefore require a large
number of labeled images, tens of thousands for sure. Additionally , the
model needs to be complex, that is, having many layers (SOT A have
more than 300 layers!). This naturally means very high training times
(possibly several days/weeks) and therefore high costs. This is clearly
an arduous task.
 
Consider another option, standing on the shoulders of using a pre-
trained SOTA. Pre-trained means that the model has already been
trained on massive datasets and is now available to us to beneﬁt from.
What if this model was free of cost and could be used with just a few
lines of code? This is exactly what transfer learning lets us do, among
many other things.
 
In this chapter , we will ﬁrst learn about transfer learning, an extremely
powerful way to beneﬁt from the hard work done by thousands of
researchers and contributors in the open-source community . Using
multiple case studies, we will see how transfer learning can be used in
varied situations for multiple use cases: for using the models for
predictions directly , for extracting features from the model, and for
ﬁne-tuning the model on a newer dataset.
Using a SOTA image classiﬁcation model
 
First, we import the necessary modules from Keras - EfﬁcientNetB5 and
its accompanying decode_predictions method to make sense of the
predictions. Then, we load the model into memory using the
EfﬁcientNetV2S function, as shown in the following code:
 
from keras.applications.efﬁcientnet_v2 import EfﬁcientNetV2S,
decode_predictions
 
model = EfﬁcientNetV2S(weights='imagenet')
 
The model is now available to make predictions. You have the power of a
SOTA model, trained on millions of images, at your command. To see if it
works, let us try out an image of a fur coat. The image can be found at
https://github.com/mirzarahim2197/Optimizing-AI-and-Data-Science-
Models/tree/main/Chapter1 1/data
 
We need to import some image preprocessing utilities from Keras. Let us
also import numpy for basic array manipulation, using the following code.
Then, we load the image and resize it to 384x384, as the model expects:
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.preprocessing import image
 
import numpy as np
 
img = image.load_img("Furcoat.webp", target_size=(384, 384))
 
Img
 
 
Figure Input image of a fur coat
 
The image too is now loaded into memory . As a pre-processing step, the
image needs to be converted into an array of shape 1x384x384x3, in line
with the model input expectations using the following code:
 
img_array = image.img_to_array(img)
 
img_array = img_array .reshape((1, 384, 384, 3))
 
Now , the image can be passed to the model to get predictions. The
decode_predictions function will provide the top 5 labels the model
predicts along with their probabilities and execute the following code:
 
myresult = model.predict(img_array)
 
decode_predictions(myresult)
 
The output is shown in the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure Top 5 classes predicted by the model
 
Looking at the top 5 predictions of the model, we can see the model is
very conﬁdent that the object in the image is a fur coat. The second
highest probability score is a low 0.2%. Such clarity is naturally preferred,
as we want the model to not be confused often.
 
It took just 2 lines to have a fully functional model loaded into memory ,
and 9 lines overall to make predictions on a user-provided image. The
result was extremely good too. The model that you used is from the
EfﬁcientNet family; one of the best models for the task of image
classiﬁcation is SOTA as illustrated in the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure 11.3: EfﬁcientNet family has SOTA performance
  
Note: To ﬁnd out the SOTA models right now and the associated research
papers, along with the code, you can refer to the following link:
https://paperswithcode.com/sota/image-classiﬁcation-on-imagenet?
metric=T op%205%20Accuracy&tag_ﬁlter=5
  
The EfﬁcientNetB5 model has 312 hidden layers and 30.6 million
parameters. The base data for the model is the ImageNet database which
effectively uses more than 14 million labeled images as training data. The
model was developed from late 2019 to early 2020 but is still among the
highest-performing models out there. Additions to the EfﬁcientNet family
are continuously being developed, providing incremental prediction
accuracy and memory optimization. Figure 11.3 shows that EfﬁcientNet-
based models have among the highest top 5 accuracies (correct class in the
top 5 predictions of the model) scores available right now. EfﬁcientNet is
also one of the fastest models to train and deploy , with some variations on
being as small as 88 megabytes in size. You have a high performance,
highly memory-optimized model at your disposal.
 
Now , consider the alternative approach; developing a comparable model
from scratch. Such development would mean gathering and labeling
several images, at extremely high training time and extremely high cost.
Of course, deployment of such a model would be another task and the
organization might end up needing multiple data science professionals and
engineers for this single problem statement. Unless there are
considerations against using a pre-trained model (discussed later in this
chapter in the Drawbacks of Transfer this approach does not make a lot of
sense.
 
In our example, we saw that transfer learning could save us quite some
time, money and effort and is an excellent option. However , what we saw
was just one of the applications of transfer learning. Let us build a holistic
understanding of transfer learning, what it is? its applications, beneﬁts,
and drawbacks. Let us get started.
What is transfer learning?
 
The big idea here is quite simple; metaphorically , it is standing on the
shoulders of giants. Not starting from scratch can have tremendous
advantages. There are a couple of perspectives that will help understand
the same.
 
The ﬁrst perspective is utilizing knowledge acquired for one task to solve
a related problem, and therefore going beyond the paradigm in which the
learning was developed.
 
Somewhat like a human who knows how to drive a car, might ﬁnd it
easier to learn to ride a motorcycle. A person who knows how to play the
guitar may learn to play the ukulele much faster . Not learning from
scratch is often a great advantage. Andrew in his NIPS tutorial in 2016
stated, After supervised learning — Transfer Learning will be the next
driver of ML commercial
  
Note: For those interested, the NeurIPS tutorial was titled Nuts and Bolts
of Building AI Applications Using Deep Learning. The slides for the talk
can be found at the following link:
https://media.nips.cc/Conferences/2016/Slides/6203-Slides.pdf
  
Another actionable perspective in our context of developing machine
learning/AI solutions is leveraging the contributions of others in the data
science community . To make the idea more concrete, we are talking about
leveraging ML/AI artefacts as follows:
 
•Ready to use pre-trained models, or
 
•Partly trained models that require some ﬁne tuning
 
•Feature generation/data representation modules.
  
Pause and ponder: In our case study , we have used transfer learning for
deep learning. Can you think of an example of transfer learning that does
not involve deep learning?
   
Hint: Even data representation modules are Transfer Learning solutions.
  
The following ﬁgure shows the basic difference between traditional
machine learning and Transfer Learning:
 
 
Figure Traditional machine learning vs. Transfer Learning
 
Figure 11.4 illustrates the difference between the learning processes of
traditional machine learning vs transfer learning. In traditional machine
learning, learning is focused on the data and task at hand. The
patterns/knowledge learnt by the system/model are not intended to be used
in another context. In contrast, the transfer learning process seeks to distil
knowledge from the source task and data (typically massive datasets) into
a model. It is this knowledge that we wish to leverage in another task.
This target task could be different from the source task, with the data set
usually being different. To understand better why this is beneﬁcial, let us
see some applications of transfer learning in the next section.
Applications of transfer learning
 
We understood the broad idea of transfer learning in the previous
section. There are several applications of knowledge leverage. Let us
understand some prominent applications and see how they beneﬁt the
ﬁeld of artiﬁcial intelligence.
Ready to use pre-trained models
 
Revisiting the EfﬁcientNet example from the previous section, we
encountered one of the typical situations where Transfer Learning
unlocks tremendous value. In the situation considered in the case
study , it was impractical to develop a high-performance model from
scratch. With transfer learning, however , we could very easily leverage
a SOTA deep learning model for image classiﬁcation (with 312 hidden
layers and more than 30 million parameters) and get good results on
our task. This was an example where we used the model out of the and
we used the model for prediction without making any modiﬁcations to
it.
 
In our case study , we did not require development to use the model and
get good results. In many cases, these results are good enough for
using the model. If not, these are at least good enough to form a good
the benchmark to compare future solutions. Let us say you wish to
build a highly customized model that understands the speciﬁcs of the
domain data to provide better accuracy . Naturally , there is a cost to this
development and justiﬁcation needed, along with benchmarks that the
solution must beat. If the solution you intend to create does not beat
the benchmark from Transfer Learning, it is probably not a good idea
to do so (as higher accuracy was the main objective).
Fine tuning models
 
Just because a model has been trained on millions of images, it does
not mean that the model will perform extremely well on your tasks as
well. Indeed, it is not always the case that the SOTA model was trained
on the same task and data as your application. We need to improve on
the baseline that the SOTA model provides when used out of the box.
The improvement comes from ﬁne tuning the model. While there are
different approaches to ﬁne tuning, the short explanation of ﬁne tuning
is modiﬁcations to the base model to perform better on the target data
and task. We will see this in more detail later in this chapter , in the
section General Transfer Learning
Data extraction
 
We do not always have to use the entire (sometimes huge) model. The
modular nature of deep neural networks enables us to extract parts of
the model that we can then employ in various situations in different
ways. One common use case is to use transfer learning for getting
good data representation, that is, feature extraction.
 
Consider the Inception model that was trained on millions of images.
The initial layers of the model have under gone extensive training on
millions of images to learn very powerful representations of the input
images. Indeed, good data representation through great features is a
key decider of the performance of the model. When dealing with small
datasets, using good representations can make all the difference in
model performance.
Beneﬁts of transfer learning
 
There are many beneﬁts of transfer learning. We will therefore not
dwell on these points to discuss them in detail. Rather , we will see
these beneﬁts ﬁrst-hand throughout this chapter through various hands-
on activities. A brief overview of the beneﬁts of transfer learning is as
follows:
 
•Little to no development needed to get a baseline : Often, with
minimal modiﬁcations (sometimes no modiﬁcation), the base model
provides a good starting point. If the source and target tasks and data
are similar , then the performance of the base model can be a great
baseline that you achieved with little developmental effort.
 
•Increased learning speed (that is, via quicker baselines) : Because you
can arrive at a baseline quicker , the rate at which you learn what works
for your problem is much higher and you can create MVPs and test
your hypothesis much more quickly .
 
•Access SOTA models with a few lines of code : If pre-trained SOTA
models are available freely , they would be a natural choice for transfer
learning. Most of the present sources for pre-trained models make
these models available to us with very minimal coding. In the section
Using a SOTA image classiﬁcation model , we got the SOTA
EfﬁcientNetV2 model at our disposal using just 2 lines of code.
 
•Create solutions with little to (almost) no labelled data : In case the
target task and data are like the base model, you can potentially use it
straight away without retaining newer data. In case the task and data
are slightly different, and you wish to optimize the model for your
data, this can also be achieved using a few hundred data points.
 
•Rich feature generation : Using transfer learning, we can leverage
feature representations from models that were trained on millions of
images/billions of tokens. For problems with smaller data sizes, the
features make a big difference and transfer learning lets you use the
best.
 
Does transfer learning have any drawbacks/limitations? Let us ﬁnd out
in the next section.
Limitations of transfer learning
 
For all its beneﬁts, transfer learning also has a few drawbacks that the
wise data scientist must be aware of before using it in their next data
science solution. The drawbacks are as follows:
 
•Works only if source and target problems are similar enough: It is
easy to see why this is a limitation. If the model was trained on chest
X-ray images, it may not be suitable for classifying if the animal in an
image is a cat or a dog. Likewise, a model trained on a corpus of legal
text might not be suitable for classifying the sentiments of tweets.
 
•Fine tuning is complex : There are several choices that you must
make when ﬁne tuning a model and many hyper -parameters - number
of layers to freeze, positions of these layers, learning rates to retrain
with and many more. Also, let us not forget that the model itself is a
choice we need to make. Fine tuning can be considered more of an art
than a science.
 
•Prone to overﬁtting : If you are using a SOTA model with a billion
parameters and ﬁne tuning it on a dataset with a few hundred records,
chances are that you will overﬁt the data extremely quickly . The data
scientist must be extremely careful while ﬁne tuning and monitoring
the training closely , using appropriate learning rates, regularization and
techniques like data augmentation.
 
•Propagate biases in input data : To understand this consideration, let
us consider popular NLP models. These models are trained on text in
the public domain: including online discussion forums, and comments
on social media. The models learn the toxicity and bias present in the
data. Any user of this model will inadvertently propagate that bias into
their application. Likewise, for image models, many of the images are
from the public domain or curated datasets. For example, these may
not represent people of all genders and skin colours adequately ,
making the systems perform differently and therefore induce a bias in
the solution that uses such a model.
 
The takeaway from these points is that while transfer learning is
extremely powerful and lets you truly beneﬁt from the great advances
being made by the data science community , the data scientist must be
extremely careful while employing transfer learning. It comes with its
own set of challenges.
Sources for pre-trained models
 
There are many sources currently available and practically anyone
with an Internet connection can start leveraging the immense
knowledge available through transfer learning. These
sources/platforms have varying maturity and ease of use of the
available models/data. Some notable examples are the following:
Keras Applications
 
For those who like the abstractions and simplicity that Keras provides, it
has several pre-trained models available as Keras The beneﬁt of using
these applications is that they are extremely easy to load, and then they
manipulate just like you would manipulate any Keras model. Keras
documentation also has several, very useful examples using transfer
learning and can help you quickly get up to speed.
 
Website: https://keras.io/api/applications/

For Tensorﬂow users, there is no dearth of models available on
TensorFlow Hub - a repository of several hundred (if not in thousands
already) models. These are available with minimal code, and you can
easily reuse state-of-the-art models that are frequently added to the
repository .
 
Website: https://tfhub.dev/

Hugging Face
 
Hugging Face is at present one of the most popular destinations for open-
source models and transfer learning. The transformer architecture in deep
learning, which took the deep learning community by storm, was ﬁrst
made accessible to the masses via the transformers library by Hugging
Face. Now , Hugging Face is a thriving community where most SOTA
models can be found. Hugging Face also has Spaces, which allows
individuals/or ganizations to host demo applications showcasing the

learning. We will use Hugging Face’ s transformer library heavily in this

Python libraries/ packages. It is not surprising that GitHub also hosts a
great many model repositories that the community can not only contribute
