Word embeddings for text
 
In addition to full-ﬂedged models for prediction/ﬁne tuning, you can
also beneﬁt from powerful data representations. This is particularly
useful for text problems, where pre-trained word embeddings can be
used to represent words/ sentences/ corpora. Word embeddings are
low-dimensional representations of tokens that, if trained well, can
capture semantic regularities between words which can be understood
as representations that capture the meaning of the term. The Global
Vectors for Word Representation (GloV e) project by Stanford NLP
group makes word vectors trained on billions of tokens available freely
to everyone. Google also has their Word2V ec embeddings trained on
about 100 billion tokens. Additionally , several other word embeddings
are available on the Hugging Face, and Kaggle platforms, and through
Python packages like Gensim, SpaCy , and many more.
 
Now that we know the sources for various pre-trained models, let us
learn how to use them for our applications.
General transfer learning workﬂow
 
We discussed earlier that there are 3 ways we can use transfer learning:
directly for prediction, feature extraction, and ﬁne tuning. In the
section Using a SOTA image classiﬁcation we saw that using a pre-
trained model for prediction can be achieved very simply in less than
10 lines of code. Now , let us understand the process of using transfer
learning for ﬁne tuning powerful pre-trained models to make them
perform better at our task.
 
The process of ﬁne tuning typically has the following steps:
 
Start with a base model with pre-trained weights loaded (for example,
ImageNet weights for image models).
 
Freeze the layers that are not to be re-trained (most layers, often all).
 
Add predictive head/new ﬁnal layers.
 
Train this new model on the dataset.
 
Depending on the size of the data and the extent of retraining you wish
to do, Step 2 can freeze varying amounts of layers. Figure 11.10
illustrates this with the example of an image model. The diagram on
the left illustrates the approach in which only the ﬁnal layer(s) of the
model are trained. The rest are frozen, that is, their weights will not
change during training. The ﬁnal layer weights, therefore, are the only
trainable portions of the model which can be trained with regular
learning rates. This approach makes the training extremely fast and
retains most of the knowledge the earlier layers of the model contain.
 
The second approach (in the right diagram in Figure shows another
approach where we allow all the layers of the model to be trained, that
is, all the weights of the model can be modiﬁed. Here, one must be
extremely careful. If the learning rate is high, then we may quickly lose
the knowledge of the original model and therefore lose the information
derived from training over millions of images. This phenomenon is
known as catastrophic A sensible approach, therefore, is using a very
low learning rate for the training. While this may seem like retraining
of the model, this is in effect a true ﬁne tuning of the model for the task
and data at hand. The following ﬁgure shows strategies for ﬁne tuning:
 
 
Figure Fine tuning strategies
 
While we took an example from the image processing problem space,
the principles and approaches apply to text problems as well. In the
next few sections, we will see how transfer learning can be applied to
both text and image problems and using a few lines of code, we will
employ SOTA models ourselves.
Transfer learning for images
 
Transfer learning is not a new idea. The computer vision community
has been practising it for several years now to learn features and even
used complete pre-trained models. The adoption of transfer learning
for images was signiﬁcantly accelerated by the ImageNet project,
which showed the importance of the quantity and quality of training
data on model performance. Most SOTA models for image processing
are trained on the ImageNet dataset. Let us understand the ImageNet
database better .
ImageNet database
 
One of the most signiﬁcant developments in the past couple of decades
of image processing has been the development of the ImageNet
project. It continues to have a signiﬁcant impact on research and has
contributed signiﬁcantly to the advances in artiﬁcial intelligence for
image tasks we see around us. The dataset was originally published in
2009. It contained 1.2 million training instances for objects from 1,000
different categories. It was one of the biggest publicly available
datasets at the time. The dataset and the project have been evolving
and the dataset contains about 14.2 million images (as of July 2023).
The ImageNet Large Scale Visual Recognition Challenge is based on
the ImageNet dataset and has been instrumental in advancing deep
learning and computer vision research.
  
Note: You can learn more about the ImageNet project here:
https://www .image-net.or g/index.php
  
The rich ImageNet dataset, publicly available for free, is an excellent
resource for training and testing models. Over time, the ImageNet
dataset became a go-to resource for research and development. One of
the key insights that greatly accelerated transfer learning was that
weights trained on the ImageNet dataset could be used for other tasks.
Consequently , most SOTA have been trained, in some way or the
other , on the ImageNet dataset. These models, with ImageNet training
weights, can be further ﬁne-tuned for the speciﬁc task. To understand
better if we can use the SOTA model out of the box or need to ﬁne
tune, we need to understand a few considerations. Let us do so in the
following section.
Choosing a Transfer Learning approach
 
The best case for ﬁne tuning is when the data and task on which the
model was trained are the same as or similar to your application. In
such a situation, it may be possible to use the model directly . This
means that we do not perform any ﬁne tuning and use the model as we
did in the section Using a SOTA image classiﬁcation However , such
alignment between our task and the model is rare.
 
For example, your task is to separate images of fashion articles into
shirts/ trousers/ shoes etc. Models trained on Fashion MNIST , or
similar datasets (that use full colour images) are great candidates for
the task. It might happen that the original model was trained to
identify , say, 20 item categories. While you need to identify just 5. In
this situation, using a pre-trained model and modifying the ﬁnal dense
layer (that makes the prediction) to adjust for the number of classes
might be a good starting point. The idea is that the many layers of the
pre-trained model have learnt some great feature representations that
you can use to get high performance yourself. The following table
shows a choice of transfer learning approach:
 
approach: approach:
approach: approach: approach: approach: approach: approach:
approach: approach: approach: approach: approach: approach:
approach:
approach: approach: approach: approach:
 
Table Choice of transfer learning approach
 
In yet another , much more common, situation, you might not ﬁnd a
pre-trained model that matches your task and data well. For example,
your task could be to identify the species of the ﬂower from the image,
but you might not ﬁnd any pre-trained model for this task. However ,
models trained on the imagenet database can identify a thousand
categories of objects in images and a ﬂower is one of them. This is a
situation where you might need to ﬁne tune a pre-trained model. If you
have little training data, ﬁne tuning might be a good option. However ,
if you have a fair amount of labelled data, then training a model from
scratch may be a better option. The four situations - based on the size
of your dataset and similarity with the data for the pre-trained model -
and the action in each case are listed in Table 11.1. Whether the
features learnt by the pre-trained model can be useful for your task is a
key consideration in this choice.
Fine tuning approaches
 
From Table 11.1 we can understand ﬁne-tuning approaches but it is rather
a simpliﬁcation. There are various other choices as follows:
 
•Unfreeze and train only the ﬁnal prediction layer .
 
•Unfreeze and train all fully connected layers, using features from
convolution layers.
 
•Replace ﬁnal layers with custom layers; train only those (freeze the
remaining layers).
 
•Unfreeze and train (very low learning rate) all layers.
 
Again, as discussed in the previous section, the choice depends on the data
at hand and the data scientist’ s judgement on what knowledge from the
pre-trained model can be transferred to the task at hand.
 
In general, freezing certain layers, i.e., making them untrainable is a usual
step in transfer learning. Including or excluding the top/ﬁnal predictive
layer of the model is also a standard choice we must make. The model
class in Keras/T ensorFlow makes such conﬁgurations convenient. Let us
see how these can be done.
 
Let us import the EfﬁcientNetB5 pre-trained model again using Keras
Applications by executing the following code:
 
from keras.applications.efﬁcientnet import EfﬁcientNetB5
 
model_base = EfﬁcientNetB5(weights='imagenet',
 
include_top=False,
 
pooling='avg')
 
With this, we have the base model in memory . The difference this time is
that we do not have the prediction layer (by setting include_top to and will
need to specify our own. This allows us to use the model on a task with
categories different from the original dataset.
  
Note: Another change in the code that you might notice is specifying avg
to the pooling parameter . This parameter deﬁnes the output format (agrees
with the ﬁnal convolutional layer). If set to None, the output would be a
4D tensor . If we apply an average pooling, as in the preceding code, the
output of the model would be a 2D tensor . Be mindful that the pooling is
done across width and height, thereby reducing two dimensions from the
input. If you add any layers to the model, you need to be mindful of the
output dimensions.
  
A (truncated) summary of this model is shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Trainable parameters in the model
 
Note that most parameters in the default state are trainable. If you wish to
train all these layers and parameters, you only need to add the ﬁnal layers
and ﬁt the model on the data. Of course, you will be training all the layers
and all parameters of the model. If we wish to make the base model
completely non-trainable, that is, freeze all the layers imported, we can do
so with the following command:
 
model_base.trainable = False
 
The truncated summary of this model is shown in Figure 11.12:
 
 
Figure Non-trainable parameters on freezing the model
 
The single command freezes all the layers in the base model. As shown in
Figure 11.14. We can see that the number of trainable parameters is
exactly 0 and the total parameters match the non-trainable parameters. In
this setup, you are using freezing the base model and will mostly add
some ﬁnal predictive layers that you need to train.
 
We can also freeze/unfreeze speciﬁc layers in the model. The Keras model
class makes the layers iterable, so we could loop over them and take
action. The following commands make all but the last 5 layers of the base
model trainable. Execute the following code:
 
model_base.trainable = True
 
for layer in model_base.layers[:-5]:
 
layer .trainable = False
 
A (truncated) summary of the model is shown in Figure We have about 1
million trainable parameters. These belong to the ﬁnal 5 layers that we
froze. You can try changing the number of layers to freeze and verify that
the number of trainable parameters changes correspondingly .
 
 
Figure Trainable parameters after freezing the ﬁnal 5 layers
  
Note: The number of layers to freeze is a choice and is therefore another
hyper -parameter . The data scientist must therefore be careful in making
this choice. As discussed in multiple earlier chapters, while some parts of
hyper -parameter optimization can be automated, a good understanding of
the data and the task at hand is essential for maximizing the performance
of the model.
  
Now that we understand how we can freeze/unfreeze parts of the model,
we have learned how to make our base model. To this base model, we
would need to add our layers and train on new data. Let us learn to do so
in the next section.
Fine tuning a SOTA image model
 
Our case study for this learning would be the task of classifying images,
our model would identify if the scene contained in it is rural or urban. In
modern automobiles with smart features, identifying trafﬁc signs is an
important task. A common application is to detect and inform the user
about a changing speed limit by detecting the appropriate trafﬁc sign. The
signs, their frequency and nature can differ between rural and urban
settings (for example, city speed limits are usually lower). Identifying
whether the user is in a rural/urban setting is therefore an important task.
We will use a small, curated dataset of urban and rural images and ﬁne
tune a SOTA model on it.
 
Some sample images from the data are shown in Figure 11.14:
 
 
Figure Sample images from the data
 
The top row in Figure 11.16 contains samples of rural images in the data,
while the bottom row contains images of urban settings. You might
already identify from these sample images that there are signiﬁcant
differences between the two classes. Although, it is not straightforward to
articulate the differences and it is therefore not a trivial task for the model
either . We need to employ a complex model that has learnt some excellent
feature representations that we can leverage.
 
Let us deﬁne our approach to solving this problem. The approach that we
will follow , to ﬁne tune a SOTA image model is as follows:
 
1. Start with a base model with pre-trained weights loaded ( ImageNet
weights).
 
2. Make all layers trainable (we will train with a very low learning rate).
 
3. Add predictive head/new ﬁnal layers.
 
4. Train this new model on the dataset.
 
We will use the EfﬁcientNetB5 model from Keras Applications. The
model is SOTA which has 30.6 million parameters and a depth of 312.
The model has been trained on ImageNet and therefore has learnt features
on training on millions of images. We will use most layers of this model
and take advantage of the extensive knowledge contained in them. But
ﬁrst, let us load and prepare the dataset for modelling.
  
Note: For the following steps, make sure that the ﬁle rural_urban.zip has
been added to your working directory .
  
First, let us unzip the dataset in our working directory . This can be done
very simply , using the following command:
 
!unzip "rural_urban.zip"
 
This should create two additional folders in the working directory . The
train and val folders further contain one folder each for rural and urban
images. Note that if you are not using Google Colab, the appearance could
be different and the sample_data folder could be missing. The structure is
shown in Figure. 11.15:
 
 
Figure Folder structure on unzipping
 
This structure is a common way of organizing image data and Keras has a
utility that lets us load data from directories conveniently . Let us import
the utility using the following command:
 
import keras
 
from keras.utils import image_dataset_from_directory as load_images
 
load_images can now be used to read directly from directories. We have
separate folders for training and validation. To create a dataset generator
for the training images, we can use the following code:
 
train_ds = load_images(
 
"train",
 
seed=42,
 
image_size=(224, 224),
 
label_mode='categorical',
 
batch_size=12)
 
The output of the command is:
 
Found 72 ﬁles belonging to 2 classes
 
We speciﬁed a seed 42 so that the batches generated are consistent and
created 6 batches from the data. We have a total of just 72 images, so our
dataset is small. The image size is 224 x 224. Next, create a generator for
the validation images using the following code:
 
val_ds = load_images(
 
"val",
 
seed=42,
 
image_size=(224, 224),
 
label_mode='categorical',
 
batch_size=20)
 
The output should be as follows:
 
Found 20 ﬁles belonging to 2 classes
 
With the dataset generators for train and validation at hand, we can load
and start conﬁguring the model. Let us begin by loading the model,
excluding the predictive top and specify the input size as 224 x 224 using
the following code:
 
from keras.applications.efﬁcientnet import EfﬁcientNetB5,
decode_predictions
 
model_base = EfﬁcientNetB5(weights='imagenet',
 
include_top=False,
 
pooling='avg',
 
input_shape=(224,224,3))
 
The model is now instantiated as we desired. The next step is to make sure
all layers of the model are trainable. We do so, using the following
command:
 
model_base.trainable = True
 
The base model is ready . Now , we must make our modiﬁcations. We will
keep it simple and add only one dense layer with 32 neurons and a dense
layer with 2 classes for the prediction. Note that this is a choice, and you
could add more layers as well. Let us see the following code:
 
from keras.models import Sequential
 
from keras.layers import Dense, Dropout
 
model_upd = Sequential()
 
model_upd.add(model_base)
 
model_upd.add(Dense(32,activation='relu'))
 
model_upd.add(Dense(2,activation='softmax'))
 
The model architecture has now been deﬁned. The summary of the model
is shown in Figure
 
 
Figure Summary of our model
 
Note the number of trainable and non-trainable parameters in Figure Most
of the layers are trainable. Figure 11.17 also conﬁrms that the base model
has been incorporated into the new model architecture. Let us now specify
the optimizer and compile the model using the following code. We will
use a very low learning rate of 3e-5. Execute the following code:
 
optimizer = keras.optimizers.Adam(learning_rate=3e-5)
 
model_upd.compile(loss='categorical_crossentropy',
 
optimizer=optimizer ,
 
metrics=['accuracy'])
 
Now , we ﬁt the model on the train data for 15 epochs using the following
code:
 
history = model_upd.ﬁt(train_ds,
 
epochs=5,
 
validation_data=val_ds,
 
validation_steps=1,
 
verbose=1)
 
To assess the model performance on the validation data, let us plot the
learning curve using the following code:
 
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt
 
plt.plot(history .history['val_accuracy'])
 
plt.title("V alidation accuracy")
 
plt.show()
 
The output should be similar to that shown in Figure 11.17:
 

 
Figure Validation accuracy over the training
 
We can see in Figure 11.19 that towards the end we got 95% accuracy on
the validation data. This can be considered a pretty good result
considering the small size of our data and that it took us only a few
minutes to arrive at this result.
 
The case study above is a good summary of the learnings on transfer
learning for images. Note that there were several choices that we needed
to make during the process. We also discussed the various considerations
in making these choices. We used the SOTA EfﬁcientNetB5 model (30
million parameters, depth 312) and ﬁne-tuned it for our task in a few
minutes. We got a very impressive result that highlights the power of
transfer learning and how easy it is to use SOTA models for anyone. Now ,
let us turn our attention towards transfer learning for text, an extremely
exciting space with rapid developments taking place every week.
Transfer learning for text
 
Like it is for computer vision/ image processing tasks, transfer learning
is a boon for text/NLP  tasks as well. The applications of transfer
learning are as follows:
 
•Prediction using pre-trained models
 
•Feature extraction
 
•Fine tuning
 
As with considerations for image use cases, we need to be mindful of
the task at hand and the data we have. We discussed the various tasks
in Natural Language Processing in Chapter 10, Optimizing NLP Let us
understand how transfer learning can beneﬁt NLP tasks.
Applications of transfer learning for text
 
Recent developments in Transformer models (models using the
transformer architecture) have enabled machines to outperform
humans on many (if not most) NLP tasks. As the open-source model
becomes more prominent, most of the state-of-the-art models on these
tasks are now available to the public to use via transfer learning.
 
Hugging Face is a leading organization in the open-source movement
and it makes most of the SOTA models easily available in Python.
More on this in the next section. SpaCy is another great package that
provides models for many tasks available via a simple API. Stanford
NLP is another notable example of some great NLP research work
available to the community . Let us see how these can be used for the
three main applications of transfer learning.
Prediction using pre-trained models
 
For most of the NLP tasks listed in the previous section, you could
easily ﬁnd stable implementations of SOTA models via the
transformers library by Hugging Face. As we will see in the next
section, these are available through a simple-to-use API and Hugging
Face is therefore great for beginners. SpaCy also has a clean and
simple-to-use API and high-performance models available for multiple
NLP tasks.
Feature extraction
 
Word embeddings are an extremely popular way of representing the
meaning in the text. The Stanford NLP group has made available pre-
trained word vectors trained on massive datasets (for example,
embeddings from 27 billion tokens from Twitter tweets). Similarly ,
Facebook created FastT ext embeddings that are available to all.
Google’ s Universal Sentence Encoder available on TensorFlow hub) is
a relatively recent model (based on the BER T transformer model) that
helps generate powerful representations for sentences. Hugging Face
also has made available various other transformer models which you
can employ for feature extraction.
  
Note: To learn more about word embeddings and the amazing ways in
which they capture meaning, and to learn about GloV e Product by
Stanford NLP, visit https://nlp.stanford.edu/projects/glove/
Fine tuning
 
Hugging Face is a notable example that allows easy ﬁne tuning of
most models available for most tasks. Tuning can be done using
Tensorﬂow (or even Keras), PyTorch and Flax conveniently using only
a few lines of code. The process is very similar to that for image
models.
 
Let us now see for ourselves how we can leverage transfer learning for
text using Hugging Face’ s solutions.
Hugging face for transfer learning
 
At the time of writing this chapter , Hugging Face is one of the most
popular organizations in the open-source community . The transformers
library by Hugging Face is the most popular NLP library in Python. It
allows you to build, train and deploy state-of-the-art models via a clean
API that is simple to use and perfect for beginners. They can be credited
with making transformer models widely accessible to the masses. In
recent times, most SOTA models became available on Hugging Face
shortly after being published. This can also be attributed to the fact that
more than 5,000 organizations actively use their solutions, and their
research teams readily make the latest models available on the Hugging
Face platform. Let us now familiarize ourselves with the extremely
popular and powerful transformers library from Hugging Face.
  
Note: To learn more about Hugging Face, visit their website The site
contains details on the many models available on the platform, some great
documentation on using the Transformers library , and some great courses
on deep learning.
  
To access the models made available by Hugging Face, you need to install
the transformers library in Python. Using the pip command makes it
simple as shown in the following code snippet:
 
!pip install transformers
 
There could be differences depending on the existing packages on your
system. If you are using Google Colab, the output should not be very
different. The output should be like the image in Figure
 
 
Figure Transformers library installation
 
Hugging Face also provides access to several datasets to benchmark
various NLP tasks. To access them, install the datasets library using the
following command:
 
!pip install datasets
 
On successful execution, you would have access to all the models
available on Hugging Face and all the datasets. Using the transformers
library is straightforward with the use of
 
Pipelines are a straightforward way to use existing models for
inference/prediction. You could also build your own NLP pipelines with
ease. These are essentially abstractions (objects that have various methods
available for various NLP tasks). The way to use the pipeline varies
slightly with the task and the API is therefore very general purpose. The
way to use a pipeline for, say, text classiﬁcation is like the usage for text
summarization. The Transformers library smartly performs the
background actions needed without complicating the usage.
 
Pipelines perform the following steps:
 
•Preprocessing: A tokenizer takes the raw input text, breaks it down, and
maps it to token (IDs).
 
•Model application: A model (usually pre-trained) is used to make
predictions from the inputs.
 
•Some (optional) post-processing on the model’ s output, modifying it to
suit the requirements of the speciﬁc task.
 
To gain a better understanding, let us create our initial sentiment analysis
pipeline. We will observe how Hugging Face simpliﬁes the process of
utilizing the most up-to-date transformer models. We initiate by importing
the pipeline module with the given command:
 
from transformers import pipeline
 
For instantiating a pipeline, the task must be speciﬁed. For a sentiment
analysis task, we must specify sentiment-analysis as an argument to the
call, as shown in the following command:
 
classiﬁer = pipeline("sentiment-analysis")
 
The library would download the model and required utilities, as seen in
the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure Output from pipeline creation
  
Note: As shown in the output in Figure because no model was speciﬁed,
the library defaulted to distilbert-base-uncased-ﬁnetuned-sst-2-english.
This is a stable, state-of-the-art model that does well on numerous tasks.
For production pipelines, it is advisable to specify a model (as the default
could change), as also communicated in the output.
  
With the given two lines of code, you now have a powerful SOTA model
for sentiment analysis loaded in memory and ready to apply to any input
text. Let us go ahead and try it out on a simple input text, as shown in the
following command:
 
res = classiﬁer("No good acting or great story in this movie.")
 
res
 
The output gets printed as follows:
 
[{'label': 'NEGA TIVE', 'score': 0.9997885823249817}]
 
The model predicts the sentiment in the text to be negative, with a high
level of conﬁdence (0.99978 probability). Despite the highly positive
individual phrases like good acting and a great the model identiﬁed that
the no at the beginning of the sentence negates all the positive phrases. Go
ahead, and try out a few other input sentences to assess for yourself if the
predictions are good.
 
This is simple, we use a SOTA model for sentiment analysis on arbitrary
input data with just a couple of lines of code. This example should
reiterate the power of Transfer Learning for text. In the next sections, we
will see some examples of using the transformer library for various NLP
tasks.
Sentiment analysis
 
In the previous section, we saw an example of a sentiment
classiﬁcation task. For simplicity , we used the default model and only
one sentence as input to the model. However , the transformers library
allows us to choose the model to employ in the pipeline. For example,
if you are working on Tweets and wish to use the Twitter RoBER Ta
model for this purpose, choosing the model for the pipeline is as
simple as using the following command:
 
classiﬁer = pipeline("sentiment-analysis",
 
"cardif fnlp/twitter -roberta-base-sentiment")

Additionally , we are not restricted to providing just a single input
string. We can easily provide a list of strings and the pipeline would
provide the output corresponding to each of them in a list.
Additionally , data can also be provided in batches (using generators) to
the pipeline. Let us try out this Twitter data-trained model on a few
different input texts in the next exercise.
Exercise 11.1: RoBER Ta for tweet sentiment classiﬁcation
 
In this exercise, we will use the Twitter RoBER Ta model for
classifying the sentiment of a few input tweets. The roBER Ta-base
model was trained on approximately 58M tweets for the purpose of
sentiment analysis. We therefore expect this model to handle some
non-standard language usage and some usual features of tweets
(hashtags, smileys for example). Let us perform the following steps.
 
1. Begin by importing the pipeline from the transformers library and
instantiating a pipeline using the Twitter RoBER Ta model. Make sure
to use the model reference we used in the previous section. Use the
following code:
 
from transformers import pipeline
 
classiﬁer = pipeline("sentiment-analysis",
 
"cardif fnlp/twitter -roberta-base-sentiment")
 
2. Create a list that contains a few simple sentences that contain
smileys, as shown in the following command:
 
input_tweets = ['Love the mountains! :D #blessed',
 
'Super supper wid besties xoxo #livingitup',
 
'Dis is it. Njoy it to the fullest #YOLO :D'
 
]
 
3. Run the tweets by the classiﬁer and print the output, as shown in the
following code snippet:
 
res = classiﬁer(input_tweets)
 
res
 
The following output is printed:
 
[{'label': 'LABEL_2', 'score': 0.98894441 1277771},
 
{'label': 'LABEL_2', 'score': 0.9282486438751221},
 
{'label': 'LABEL_2', 'score': 0.9688595533370972}]
 
All three tweets were correctly labeled as having positive sentiment,
with high conﬁdence. We must appreciate that creating a model from
scratch that can handle such non-standard text would require a lot of
effort. With the transformers library , we have a solution with just a few
lines.
Text generation
 
Another popular task in NLP is text generation. In a common form of text
generation, the user provides some initial text that provides the necessary
context for the model. The model then generates a speciﬁed number of
sequences with a speciﬁed length. GPT-3, GPT-4, and ChatGPT  are
popular examples of models that have been trained in a semi-supervised
manner and are SOTA for text generation.
 
With the Transformers library , a pipeline for text generation is extremely
simple to use. Let us try it out ourselves. We ﬁrst create a pipeline and
specify text-generation as the task in the following command:
 
generator = pipeline("text-generation")
 
You should get the output with the details of the download progress as
shown in the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure Output from initializing the text-generation pipeline
 
Note that the library defaulted to using as no model was speciﬁed. GPT2
is good for our purpose. Now , we simply need to provide the initial text,
the maximum length of the output string, and the number of sequences to
return. Take a look at the following command:
 
res = generator("A  father's love for his daughter",
 
max_length=30,
 
num_return_sequences=3)
 
The output is shown in the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure Output of text generator
 
The output in Figure 11.21 contains the three responses from the model.
All three sentences are grammatically correct and individually coherent.
The ﬁrst output is interesting as it seems to be inspired by a movie
review/synopsis. This gives some indication of the kind of patterns and
data that the model was trained on. Let us now look at another important
task in NLP: Zero Shot Classiﬁcation.
Zero-shot classiﬁcation
 
In a general machine learning context, Zero-shot Learning is when the
model can predict the class without any prior training data. In the context
of NLP, Zero-shot classiﬁcation would allow making predictions without
any prior training. The user provides only the input text and the choice of
labels. Conceptually , this is possible when the model understands the
labels and the input text in the same way. Let us create a simple pipeline
for Zero-shot classiﬁcation using the following code:
 
classiﬁer = pipeline("zero-shot-classiﬁcation")
 
The output is shown in the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure (Truncated) output from pipeline creation
 
The library chose the BAR T Large model by Facebook, more details
about the model can be found at the following link:
 
Now , let us create the input corpus (the sentences that the model must map
correctly to a candidate label). We must also create a list containing the
candidate variables the model must choose from. Use the following code:
 
inp_corpus = ["The family will meet at my uncles place'",
 
"A CEO must be organised.",
 
"My niece is super annoying."]
 
labels = ["business", "politics", "family"]
 
The labels we provided were: politics and If the model works ﬁne, no
sentence should be mapped to Next, we use the pipeline similarly . This
time, the pipeline needs an additional input, the Use the following code to
make the predictions and print:
 
res = classiﬁer(inp_corpus,
 
candidate_labels = labels)
 
res
 
The output is shown in the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure Output from Zero shot classiﬁcation pipeline
 
Figure 11.23 shows that the model pipeline output contains, for each
input, the top labels, and their associated scores. The most probable is
listed ﬁrst. Sentences 1 and 3 were mapped to family with extremely high
scores. Sentence 2 was mapped to business as we expected. Now , let us
look at another extremely important application of NLP, translation.
Translation
 
Translation between languages using the Hugging Face transformers
library has very similar usage with its pipeline module. Note that for the
translation task we must have a source and a target language. The model
for the pipeline is also chosen accordingly . For example, if we are to
translate text from English to French, the model in the pipeline must be an
English-to-French model. Let us create our own simple pipeline for this
task.
 
Create a pipeline with translation_en_to_fr as the task, as in the following
code:
 
en_to_fr = pipeline("translation_en_to_fr")
 
The output is shown in the following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure Output from pipeline creation
 
Note that as no model was speciﬁed, T5 was chosen. T5 is a powerful
translation model trained on a very large corpus. More details can be
found at: https://huggingface.co/t5-base
 
With the pipeline ready , translating is as simple as supplying the source
language text to the pipeline. Use the following code:
 
text_en = "Aiza is moving to London"
 
text_fr = en_to_fr(text_en)
 
text_fr
 
The output is given here:
 
[{'translation_text': 'Aiza déménage à Londres'}]
 
The resulting translation is indeed the standard translation one would
expect. Yet again, a SOTA model like trained on several billion tokens,
could be employed easily with very few lines of code.
Fine tuning a SOTA NLP model
 
By now we have seen how easy it is to use pre-trained models from
Hugging Face for various NLP tasks. But sometimes we need to ﬁne
tune the model to work better on our domain/data. The transformers
library enables you to perform ﬁne tuning with ease using a couple of
utilities and support for PyTorch and
 
The key steps in the process would be:
 
1. Import the dataset to ﬁne tune on.
 
2. Import the preprocessing utility (tokenizer) for the task.
 
3. Choose and load the pre-trained model designed for the task.
 
4. Compile the model with necessary settings (optimizer , loss etc.).
 
5. Fit the model.
 
The initial decision involves selecting the appropriate tokenizer , which
is responsible for dividing the input text string into a sequence of
tokens for the model to handle. It is crucial to choose a tokenizer that
aligns with the speciﬁc task at hand. For instance, when dealing with
Twitter tweets, it is advisable to utilize tokenizers trained on Twitter
data or similar social media datasets. In the following section, we will
explore the usage of a tokenizer and its application.
 
We will use the tokenizer from the popular transformer model that
showed great success in language modelling and next sequence
prediction tasks. The tokenizer from the BER T model can also be used
with other models. In our case, we will use the BER T case model for
tokenizing. Let us begin by importing the AutoT okenizer utility from
the transformers library . Execute the following code:
 
from transformers import AutoT okenizer
 
Using the AutoT okenizer utility , we can load the tokenizer from the
BER T case model, using the following command:
 
tokenizer = AutoT okenizer .from_pretrained("bert-base-cased")
 
The tokenizer is ready to process input text. It can take in iterables and
can return numpy tensors that can be then used in TensorFlow or
  
Note: The tokenizer must be agreeable with the ﬁnal model that you
intend to use. For example, if you wish to use a BER T cased model for
ﬁne tuning/prediction, a Twitter RoBER Ta-based tokenizer is not a
good choice.
  
The second choice you must make is that of the pre-trained model you
wish to ﬁne tune. HuggingFace has a large list of models available.
You must identify the one suitable for your task and compatible with
the tokenizer you chose. For loading a model for text, a frequently
used utility is TFAutoModelForSequenceClassiﬁcation from the
transformers library . This is a generic model class that can be used to
get a speciﬁc model. It returns a model object that you can use in
TensorFlow or using the following code:
 
from transformers import TFAutoModelForSequenceClassiﬁcation as
TFA
 
To load a speciﬁc pre-trained model, all you need to do is use the
following command, where model_name needs to be replaced by the
name of the model you wish to load. Use the following code:
 
model = TFA.from_pretrained("model_name")
 
With this command and the previous one, you now have a trainable
SOTA model at your disposal to make predictions or ﬁne tune. This
can be used like any other TensorFlow/Keras model. To ﬁne tune this,
you can compile the model, supply the dataset and ﬁt the model using
a very low learning rate. Note that depending on the dataset size,
training settings and the extent of ﬁne tuning, the ﬁne-tuning activity
can take a very small or very large amount of time.
Exercise 11.2: Fine tuning a BER T model
 
In this exercise, we will ﬁne tune the BER T cased model (that was trained
on a large amount of text in the public domain), on an NLP task in the
General Language Understanding Evaluation(GLUE) dataset. It consists
of data for 9 NLP tasks around natural language understanding/
modelling. The GLUE benchmark format is model agnostic, therefore
many different types of models can be benchmarked and compared.
  
Note: More information on GLUE benchmarking, detailed descriptions of
the tasks involved, some starter code, datasets and a leaderboard with the
best-performing models on GLUE tasks can be found at
https://gluebenchmark.com/
  
We will ﬁne tune the BER T model for the Corpus of Linguistic
Acceptability (CoLA) task. The task is to classify if a provided input
sentence in English is grammatically correct. It consists of 10,657
sentences along with an annotation indicating if they are grammatically
correct. More details on the dataset can be found at
 
Follow the given steps:
 
1. Begin by importing the data for the CoLA  task from the GLUE dataset.
We use the load_dataset method from the datasets library . Use the
following code:
 
from datasets import load_dataset
 
dataset = load_dataset("glue", "cola")
 
The (truncated) output is given in the following code:
 
 
Figure 11.25: GLUE dataset downloaded and prepared
 
2. Extract the training subset. Print the contents of the train_ds variable.
Use the following code:
 
train_ds = dataset["train"]
 
train_ds
 
The output is shown here:
 
Dataset({
 
features: ['sentence', 'label', 'idx'],
 
num_rows: 8551
 
})
 
The output shows that the dataset has 8,551 sentences for training and a
label.
 
3. Print 5 sentences and their corresponding labels to examine the data,
using the following code:
 
train_ds['sentence'][40:45], train_ds['label'][40:45]
 
The output is shown here:
 
(['They caused him to become angry by making him.',
 
'They caused him to become president by making him.',
 
'They made him to exhaustion.',
 
'They made him into a monster .',
 
'The trolley rumbled through the tunnel.'],
 
[0, 0, 0, 1, 1])
 
As you can see, the ﬁrst three sentences are not grammatically correct, and
the corresponding labels conﬁrm this.
 
4. Use the AutoT okenizer utility from the transformers library and load
the tokenizer from the bert-base-cased model. Use the following code:
 
from transformers import AutoT okenizer
 
tokenizer = AutoT okenizer .from_pretrained("bert-base-cased")
 
5. Convert it into a dictionary for ease of processing. Apply this tokenizer
to generate training tokens using the following command:
 
train_tokens = tokenizer(dataset["sentence"], \
 
return_tensors="np", \
 
padding=T rue)
 
train_tokens = dict(train_tokens)
 
6. The labels are currently in a list, copy them into a numpy array using
the following code:
 
import numpy as np
 
labels = np.array(train_ds["label"])
 
The training data is now ready to be supplied to a model.
 
7. Next, import TFAAutoModelForSequenceClassiﬁcation and import our
target model that is, bert-base-cased using the following code:
 
from transformers import TFAutoModelForSequenceClassiﬁcation as
TFA
 
model = TFA.from_pretrained("bert-base-cased")
 
8. Import the Adam optimizer from Keras and compile our BER T model
with a low learning rate of 3e-5 , as shown in the following code:
 
from tensorﬂow .keras.optimizers import Adam
 
model.compile(optimizer=Adam(3e-5))
 
The model and data are now ready . We can start the training process.
 
9. Fit the model for 3 epochs using the following code. Use a 20%
validation split :
 
history = model.ﬁt(train_tokens, labels, \
 
epochs=3, \
 
validation_split=0.2, \
 
verbose=2)
 
The training should take a few minutes. The output is shown in the
following ﬁgure:
 
 
Figure Training output
 
In a short span of three epochs and a minimal training time, we
accomplished an accuracy of approximately 84% on a sequence
classiﬁcation task. This outcome was achieved with minimal coding on
our part, without the need to delve into the intricate details of the SOTA
model’ s implementation.
  
Note: We used just 3 epochs for pedagogical reasons. Please feel free to
train for more epochs to get a better result. Of course, that needs more
time and computation.
  
In this exercise, we demonstrated the simplicity of ﬁne-tuning a cutting-
edge NLP model using just a few lines of code. Thanks to the abstraction
provided by HuggingFace's transformer library , we can easily accomplish
this without having to handle the intricate implementation details of the
model. This level of convenience enables anyone to effectively leverage
SOTA models in their applications.
 
In the previous sections, around transfer learning for text, we saw that
with the advent of transformer models, it is remarkably easy to utilize
SOTA models with just a few lines of code. Excellent packages like the
transformers library by HuggingFace abstract the details and enable
anyone to start using SOTA models in their applications.
Conclusion
 
In this chapter , we learned about transfer learning - a powerful way to
beneﬁt from the latest advances in the ﬁeld of artiﬁcial intelligence and
machine learning. We saw that transfer learning can be used in three
ways - prediction, feature extraction, and ﬁne tuning. While there are
numerous beneﬁts, we also acknowledge that transfer learning has its
limitations that the data scientist must be aware of while employing it.
 
We saw a general transfer learning process, including one that ﬁne
tunes a SOTA model for a different target task. For image problems,
we used Keras which lets us conveniently and quickly use SOTA
models Applications) with minimum coding. We also saw that we
could make several choices around this ﬁne tuning easily using Keras
and we could ﬁne tune the model, using all the utilities, just like we
would train any Keras model.
 
Finally , we learnt and practised transfer learning for NLP on various
tasks. We saw that the transformers library by Hugging Face is a great
way to access various SOTA models for NLP. We used many of the
SOTA transformer models for different tasks. We also ﬁne-tuned a
BER T model for an NLP task, getting good performance with just a
few epochs of training.
 
You now know not only how to create your own end-to-end models
from scratch but also how to beneﬁt from the rapid advances in the
ﬁeld of machine learning and artiﬁcial intelligence by using transfer
learning effectively .
